The Red Page
A. Gore-Jones deliberately forfeits the prize
that should have been hers by departing from
the rime-scheme expressly laid down as neces-
sary in these competitions. She uses four
rimes, instead of two, in her octette; and
the finest sonnet in this month’s competition
, is therefore reluctantly laid aside: —
The shining seas are sivept with beryl green.
The purple hills stand clothed in milky
haze,
While on their timbered flanks great bush-
fires blaze.
The sun, each eve, a scarlet globe is seen,
As daylight's blinding hues of white and blue
Give place to tearless splendors of the
night;
Where brazen stars burn insolently bright,
And shrill mosquitoes chorus the hours
through.
Oh. golden morning month of heat and sun!
With eager lips yon bruise the fragile face
Of fading green on sloping hill and plain.
And as their fervid course your moments run,
The dumb earth, swooning 'neath your
fierce embrace,
Dreams pantingly of February's rain.
A. Gore-Jones.
Of the three remaining sonnets, two are
elaborate allegories, but in neither case is
the allegory sufficiently identified with Aus-
tralia. Either might as easily an ply to any
hot place anywhere, including Ilades. Yet
Nina Murdoch —a name honorably familiar
in these competitions—does draw a fine pic-
ture of heat: —
Her tyrant lord, the Summer, bids her bear
Twain of his lusty children thro' the heat,
And as she creeps along with listless feet
And eyes half-blinded by the noon-day glare,
The moisture of her temples makes her hair
Oling to her brow in little tendrils meet
For ■kisses, while her languid pulses beat
Ever more faint upon the swooning air.
For this, with sudden tenderness, her lord
Holds her within his arms the whole night
through,
Charming her sleep with amorous ivhispers
borne
On scented breezes. Yet ere day has scored
The East with flam<f, he bids her rise anew
Panting beneath her load to face the morn.
Nina Murdoch.
Herbert Tracey’s allegory is new and strik-
ing, and, with the exception of his fifth line,
where the word “grand” is too obviously
thrown in for the sake of the rime, he
carries his imagery bravely through:—
Proud Summer stood, a dice-box in her hand,
Put yestere’eh beneath the moon's pale glow,
Whilst we, impatient, waited for the throw
That should decide the fortunes of our band.
Then straightforth she, the gambler, reckless,
grand,
Cast out the Dice upon the Plains of Woe,
And, as we watched, amid vague wliisp’rings
low,
Behold! A new year broke upon the land.
And, when, amid the dried grass where it
fell,
We saw the six-spot upmost on the Dice,
All knetv that Summer's toll would not yet
end;
But that, relentless, she would wring her
price
From sun-dried crops, till weary men should
bend
And pay full tribute to this Bride of Hell.
Herbert Tracey.
The winner, who must thank Miss A.
Gore-Jones for his position, is C. E. Moore,
Coan Downs. Mount Hope, N.S.W., whose
sonnet, inferior in originality to either of
the latter entries, more admirably suggests
our Australian January. The obvious de-
fect is the unnecessary intrusion of July
and the too-strained metre of the line in
which July occurs. Much may, however, be
forgiven the winner for his sestette.
J. Inches Thomson’s 7 Voyages and Wan-
derings in Far O* Seas and Lands (Headley,
per N.S.W. Bookstall Cos.) deals plainly
with the author’s early experiences in M.L.
and the Pacific. A matter-of-fact narration
of exciting adventures.
Part 9 of the second volume of J. H. Maiden’s A
Critical Revision of the Genus Eucalyptus (2s. 6d.),
and the second part of Vol. 6 of the same author's
The Forest Flora of N.S.W., illustrated with plates
and photographs of the trees mentioned, show the
progress towards completion of two valuable scien-
tific works.
January.
The poetic quality of the 33 entries for
the best sonnet on our January is
distinctly higher than that of those
submitted for the December competi-
tion. Technically, too, the sonnets are
better, though “Nairda” supplied a
IG-line attempt. Good sonnets were sub-
mitted by D.H.B., It. G. Henderson, Stuart
Mack, G.B.D., Mabel Nagle, W.L.D., B.C.
Marriott Watson —a consistent competitor,
T. V. Tierney, Ben Sun —whose otherwise
fine sonnet was spoiled by a four-foot line,
and M. W. L. Roper.
Mick Much, as usual, provides the comic
relief; but this time his sonnet is so Austra-
lian and so picturesque that he narrowly*
escaped winning the prize. It is not the in-
tention of the adjudicator to bar the Aus-
tralian language from an Australian sonnet;
but his entry is too local and too personal to
be a prize-winner. In this sonnet-sequence
there should be a largeness of reference suit-
able to the largeness of this continent: the
conditions noted should be, as far as pos-
sible, the conditions that would apoly
throughout the Commonwealth.
If I cud set me peepers on the coot
Thet wants a sonnit on this weather writ
I’d put it to ’im: “’Ow’d he fancy it
Fer yakkerin’ on a thresher ’Twould be beaut
Ter ’ave the cow w'ere he mus’ foller suit
En ’eave the straw—-he’d ’ave ter do ’is bit
Er else git swaller’d in a smotherin’ pit
As hot as ’ell is at the flamin’ root.
Thet’s me this month; I’m racin’ in the heat.
In dust en rust.en’ sweat near choked en’ blind,
Wile bags er gettin’ bulged wi’ golden wheat,
En’ tossin’ empty straw in tons behind.
If there was January sonnits neat
This ’ell’s machine ’ud thresh ’em frum the mind.
Mick. Much.
Another humorous and personal sonnet, a
second entry from Ben Sun, is allowed quo-
tation :—
Hail! January, natal month, all hail!
You lop a chunk of summer off for me,
For I set out upon my birthday spree,
And I’ll care not for blistering heat or gale
The while 1 revel in the good brown ale,
Or gin or whisky, as the ease may be —
My oath ! the time will go right rapidly,
And though the fiftieth it will not stale.
And though I’ll suffer when you’re dead and gone,
And see quaint tinted snakes with flaming tongues,
Uncelebrated you won’t pass me by.
It was your sun that first upon me shone ;
It was your breath first filled my infant lungs—•
Mayhap that’s why my throat is always dry.
Bun Sun.
The vagueness of the sestette, weakened,
too, by a trochaic line, upsets this excellent
beginning by Mary A. K. Robb :—
-No Ice King holds our sunny land in dread:
-Her happy children play upon the sand,
Or hie them out in woods in merry band;
And if, in fear, ong turn a startled head,
It is, lest he, the Sun God, fierce and red,
Lift up his firebrand in his cruel hand
And burn the sun-kissed verdure of our land.
’Tis thus his fickle humor, grim, is fed.
Allen Farroll, too, fails to fulfil the pro-
mise of his youth, though even that is marred
by the rimes to the third and the sixth line:
The white sand hillocks drown in quivering, heat,
The emerald sea ’s a-dream and satin white
About the thin black reefs. All day the light
Pours in a sapphire passion, till the sweet,
Warm dusk of night comes by on jewelled feet.
Again the Summer feeds his grown delight
While golden daylight mellows into night,
And Time's white starry pulses slowly beat.
Duncan Brown, who has sent in some
good sonnets, submits an octette which con-
sists of clauses tacked ramblingly on with
links like “how soon. .. .when. . . .which....
that. .. .until”—a cumbrous construction.
But in his end he, in more business-like
phrases, says a word for the kiddies:—
And while, beneath the ardent summer sky,
All nature shrinks and quivers visibly,
From sea and hill there comes in shouts the good'
Glad laughter of the children freed from tie
Of school; and in their lusty ecstasy
Is heard the strong note of our nationhood.
The winner of the December sonnet com-
petition becomes colorless after an interest-
ing opening:—•
In flaming martyrdom the stubble dies,
That from its ashes an enriched earth
May know again the glory and the worth
Of happy harvesting. The pasture sighs
Beneath the brazen, mirage-haunted skies;
Still are the birds : all hush'd the insect mirth,
And, in the languidness that follows birth,
Exhausted, drugg’d and drowsy, Summer lies 1
S. Elliott Napiek.
Of the six sonnets that remain for final ad-
judication no less than three are disqualified
owing to faults in technique. In W.L.D.’s
entry the rime of the first, fourth and eighth
line cannot strictly be allowed ; but, if this
slight fault were condoned, his sonnet is too
congested, and there is an uncouthness in
some of his lines that detracts from their
lucidity:—
Bright first bead on our twelvemonth rosary,
We tell thee at high altar of the sun,
Where oft, the past year’s toiling all undone,
Irrev’rent fires incense with misery
Of homes despoiled. Startling noon lethargy,
Through the hot vault deep diapasons stun
The trembling air; the rapid lightnings run,
And all midsummer imps hold revelry.
Yet as we bow to Phoebus’ strength, his beams
Are brimming fast the new year’s vintage cup,
That haply we may drink death to the drouth,
While soft autumnal thoughts balm fevered dreams,
And even as our hopeful eyes look up,
Comes a glad wind full of the cooling south.
W.L.D.
“Ecnirp’s” “January on the Plains” offers
us an extra foot in the sextette —a fault,
easily corrigible, due to the author’s careless-
ness ; and though fine sonnets have been writ-
ten in which there, is no pause or period at
the end of the octette, the sense running over
into the sestette without a break, the neater
modern usage is in favor of keening the twr
divisions as separate in construction as they
are in rime.
The day approaches as a flaming sword,
AVhich sweeps the plains of every breath of life.
The thirsty earth, unequal to thie strife,
Retreats within herself. Shadows afford
No welcome resting place. In hot accord
The beasts and birds flee from the blade
To wait until its fiery gleam will fade
Into the dark’ning night: The scanty hoard
Of waterhole and dam pays precious toll.
Perhaps at eVe a sullen russet cloud
Stirs living things to rise and hope again.
Old Sol retires before a hostile echoing roll,
And rushing on with whistling shrill and loud,
All-conquering comes the earth-reviving rain.
BONIRP.
JANUARY.
BY C. E. MOORE.
From blazing skies she comes with flaming
face,
And scant red robe the which no charm
conceals;
And Earth, the Titan, trembles when he
feels
The searing passion of her warm embrace.
Incarnate Summer, full of languorous grace
And ripened lusciousness, her beauty steals
All gain from virtue —only that appeals
When July, austere, reigneth in her place,
So January—by day a scorching flame,
By night a lio'uri breathing love and bliss,
Like erring mistress fearing words of blame
Who stops her lover with atoning tciss.
Ah! Hail the heat of torrid days that frame
The velvet splendors of a night like this!
The dosing day for entries for the sonnet
competition on our February is March 4.
Fiction
J. D. Beresford, author of Jacob Stahl and
The Hampdenshire Wonder, is one of the
new authors who count. Goslings (Heine-
mann, per George Robertson, Sydney ) is a
novel too remarkable for indiscriminate con-
sumption. Beresford has struck a big idea,
and worked it out with a realistic detail
possible only to authors of the calibre of
H. G. Wells. Compared with Beresford’s
new plague that practically wiped out the
male half of humanity, leaving the women
alive, Conan Doyle, in his ventures into the
future, and the hosts of writers who de-
scribe strange warfare in the air, are but
childish trihers. The new plague began in
Thibet, and swept over the world, killing in
its progress all the males except a few per-
sons who were for some cause immune. There
is thus left a world full of women, with bu.t
a few thousand males left to look after them,
in England the survivors probably number
1500 males. The problem brilliantly worked
out by the author is what in such a predica-
ment would happen to the race and inciden-
tally to our elaborate system of morality aud
law. In Beresford’s hands the solution is an
elaborate piece of sociological deduction,
through which runs a grim story, lit here and
there by romance.
The Goslings are the typical lower-middle-
class Londoners, futile, hide-bound and. in
the face of the upheaval of modern civilisa-
tion, utterly helpless. The father is a tame
clerk, the mother a specialised house-female,
and the two daughters pretty girls with a
mental outlook limited to the shop-fronts of
Oxford street. And into their lives, as into
the lives of everybody in London, the cata-
clysm comes. London’s incredulity, and then
London’s panic as the plague makes its ap-
pearance in England, the exodus from the
doomed city, and the death that followed
fast, making London a silent ruin—-all these
are graphically told. Then we take up the
story of the Qosling family. They had
managed to survive the starvation that fol-
lowed from the cutting off of all foVd-supplies
by looting a provision store, and the father
is one of the few males who escaped. How-
ever, this tame clerk suddenly finds that men,
and even tjime clerks, are at a premium In a
world of helpless women, and he disappears
from the story in the comoany of a parti-
cularly fine specimen of Girl. Then the two
girls explore the stricken and deserted city.
Everywhere is desolation and death, but they
find entrance into one of the great dress
shops at whose windows they had so often
vainly peered. The scene where the two girls
in the vast deserted building find dresses that
had been almost beyond their wildest dreams,
and excitedly ‘‘try on” one gorgeous costume
after another is done in a vein of mordant
irony. And the picture of the fnad women,
wearing the Rot of a Bond-street, jeweller’s,
has its full weight of horror.
Driven by the fear of starvation, the three
women trek into the country. They follow
, the route along which thousnnds of starving
women had fled to die. Already they see
the beginnings of a new civilisation, 'little
nuclei of capable farming women who have
managed to defend their holdings against
that ravenous but incompetent horde; And
here and there are more fortunate communi-
ties who have managed to get hold of one of
the few surviving men. Already a new
morality has arrived.
“It’s like Heaven, now, you see —no
marrying or giving in marriage. With one
man to every thousand women or so, what
can you expect? It’s uo good kicking against
it. It’s got to be,”
Jt
They come at last to Wycombe—fortunate
Wycombe that possesses a man. The young
butcher of Wycombe has taken the new situa-
tion in the easiest way. They meet him
drawn in a beflowered landau, round
which a dozen members of his harem were
dancing and singing. “They were graceless,
civilised savages; Bacchantes who had never
known the beauty of unconscious abandon-
ment. . . .In the carriage a man and a woman
were sitting side by side. The man
young, with strong claims to physical beauty
—tall, broad-shouldered, swarthy, with boldly
modelled features and heavily-lidded eyes.
But his skin was coarse; the bulk of his
body was too gross for clean, muscular
strength; his curly, well-oiled hair was thin-
ning at the temples ; his loose mouth leered
aud gaped. Pie was dressed in a suit of
broadly-patterned tweed, his great red fin-
gers were covered with rings, he wore a
heavy gold bangle on each thick, round wrist,
and a sweet, frail rose was thrust into his
black and greasy hair.” He had enthroned
beside him the temporarily reigning favorite
of his harem.
Millie Gosling escapes the butcher’s atten-
tions —for the time; and the women hasten
on. Better fortune meets them in the next
community, where a finer type of male has
organised a commumtv of women workers on
communistic lines. For a new law had made
itself from the ruins of all our laws: every
woman had a right to fyer share of food as
long as she had earned it by her labor. The
story of how this community strove with
and conquered the difficulties of providing a
food supply until the harvest is reaped is
admirably told. .Religion broke out in the
community, and was promptly dealt with by
the controlling committee. When Milly Gos-
ling comes back from Wycombe, no one
blames her. For the old morality was no
longer any use. “If a man is only to have
one wife now, the race will decline, probably
perish.. It is a woman’s duty to bear chil-
dren.” And when the kero falls in love with
the daughter of a duke, or something that
had no meaning in these new conditions, he
asks her fiercely: “Wouldn’t you be fright-
ened of me if I picked you up and ran shout-
ing into the woods V” And her eyes met his
without reserve. “Dear did man,” she said,
"I should love it.” Afterwards she was so
glad that there had been “no footle about
marriage.”
But though here and there throughout
England small communities of women and
an occasional man were evolving some sort
of temporary civilisation, what was going to
happen to the world? There weren’t enough
men to go round. The women had lost in-
terest ; the zest had gone from their lives.
They were, too tired at the end of the day’s
work to dress up. .. .and why dress up when
there were no men? Hysteria and vari-
ous forms of religious mania were rife. There
were no factories, and no one able to start
them.
“The age of iron and machinery was fall-
ing into a swift decline. AH that the coml-
- could look for in the future was a
return to primitive conditions and the fight
for bare life. Every year their tools and
machines would grow less efficient, every
year Nature would return more powerful to
the attack. In 10 years they would be fight-
ing her with rude and tedious weapons of
wood, grinding their scanty corn between
two stones, and living from hand to mouth.”
Then relief comes —how or whence we
need not here disclose. Goslings is a fantasy
by means of which Be res ford launches his
frank criticism of modern womankind.
d*
The most useful criticism of Polly anna.
by Eleanor H. Porter (Page, U.S.A., per
Dymock’s Libi’ary) is supplied by the pub-
lishers. It begins with: “First impression.
Feb. 1913,” and 10 lines later concludes:
“Tenth impression. Aug., 1913.” Pollyanna
is a sister of Little Lord Fauntleroy, and a
cousin of Little Eva. She never cries ; she
never is naughty; and she radiates such hap-
piness by just being glad that all the grumpy
persons—all grown-ups, you know, are
grumpy—exclaim unsteadily, “God bless you,
little girl!” or “Well, of all the —!” or
laugh the laugh that “sounds almost like a
sob,” while their hearts , are “stramrelv soft-
ened.” And-when, as is inevitable. Pollyanna
is run over and paralysed and “will never
walk again”—though the gentle reader knows
that the new doctor will cure her —“in kit-
chens and sitting-rooms, and over backyard
fences, women talked of it °nd went openly.
On street corners and in store lounging-
places the men talked, too, and wept—though
not so openly.” In fact the gravy of senti-
ment and pathos and child-humor is thick
and rich —which is just what circulating
library gravy should be.

It is a pity that Walter Kilroy Harris —
we add his labels, since lie seems fond of
them — F.R.G.S., F.E.C.1.. should have titled
his entertaining volume of Australian experi-
ences, Outback in Australia (Garden Citv
Press:; 55.). since his travels extended merely
over a triangle of wJiieh the points were
Brisbane. Adelaide and Melbourne. Walter
Kilro.v Harris merely got as far as Aus-
tralia’s backyard. However, lie is entitled
to the claim that his book is an account of
“the longest overlanding journey ever at-
tempted in Australia with a single horse,”
provided he narrows the term “overlanding”
to the extreme south-eastern corner of the
continent. The usual Australian connota-
tion. we may mention for the benefit of
Walter Kilroy Flams and his Fellows of the
Royo l Geoaranhical Society, is transconti-
nental. His pictures of life in the outback-
yard, however, are brilliantly done, his nar-
rative is picturesque, and his atmosphere
pleasantly Australian.
Ralph Stock has not caught, in The Py-
jama Man (N.S.W. Bookstall Co.), the
careless rapture of his earlier vivacious work.
The Recipe for Rubber. The lightness of
treatment is still evident in this romance of
the surf at Manly, but the story lacks body.
Cheerful reading for sun-bathers.
Scott’s Last Expedition is now obtainable
in tjvo volumes in Macmillan’s Empire
Library (2s. fid. each), without, of course,
the plethora of photographic and color repro-
ductions of the big edition, but with the full
text of this wonderfully interesting work.
Nearly 1000 pages of Antarctic exploration

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On the Northern Road.
Through ways made strange by a magic hand
One morn on the Northern Road,
Like a lone, lone soul in an alien land—
A dead man’s land —I strode.
I heard no bird and I saw no bloom,
And the world’s loud winds were dead;
For all night long from a hidden loom
Had a web of wonder sped.
No house-dog barked, and I heard no shout
Of wayside lads that played ;
Like ghosts I saw the trees rush out,
Like ghosts I saw them fade.
My blood grew chill and a master-fear
Around, about me fell;
My senses ailed to see and hear,
And touch, and taste, and smell.
I said, “When Death’s dark mists o’erwhelm
Some pallid soul, sad-eyed,
In truth it knows no stranger realm
Than this, wherein I bide.
I left hot-foot that land unblest,
And ere the morn was done
I climbed a slope, and on its crest
I gloried in the sun.
And now, when life grows grey and chill
And trouble trumpets war,
I see the sun on Harper’s Hill—
The mists on Lochinvar.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
An Idyll of Distrustful Joy.
A bitter wind blew down the dark
And tingling on men’s noses smote,
And it was sad to see and mark
A world without an overcoat;
But we were snug and safe and warm,
Close-nestled in a pleasant place—
She in the shelter of my arm,
I in the glowing of her face.
Her hair, in splendid coils of flame,
Blushed as she breathed my happy cheek :
And all small joys that have no name
Played at a dimpled hide-and-seek
About the sweetness of her chin
And the hot scarlet of her lips,
Where little hints of laughter thin
Sat trembling on a sigh’s eclipse.
Which ought to make it pretty plain
That times were good and seasons brisk,
And youth and pleasure once again
Prepared to take love’s ancient risk '
Of rheumatism, and the chill
Which brings pneumonia in its wake,
Lest gladness from her cup should spill
One drop that gleaming eyes may take.
Yes, everything was very nice,
And nothing troubled us at all;
We could have sat on polar ice
Without a blanket or a shawl.
(Which fact some scientist may trace
To its remote and hidden seat,
And show how kisses on the face
Are forces to develop heat.)
I swore some tremulous large vows
In whispers at her shell-curled ear,
Which, like old Pharaoh’s leanest cows,
Ate up the oaths of yesteryear;
thoughts of damsels, dark or fair,
\Y hose eyes were black or brown or grey,
With one full wisp of rosy hair
I brushed from memory away.
The moon was worn a little thin,
The stars were rather weak and pale,
One cloudlet like a small grey sin
On wind-blown whiskers seemed to sail;
And. softly nestled on my knee
Like wild delight the dark makes tame,
The maiden lightly turned to me
And wonder-wistful asked my name.
I knew the tragic hour had struck.
The simple query hit me hard,
Yet, trusting to my love and luck,
I gave her someone else’s card.
Then, like a crimson rose a-swoon
With summer on its petal tips,
She raised against the dying moon
The grateful splendor of her lips.
The night grew very cold and late.
We parted with hot tears unshed.
I led her to her garden gate
And kissed six portions of her head.
Iler hair was like a thick-coiled flame;
Small dimples played about her chin,
She blushed as red as rosy shame
And laughed as light as silver sin.
And now when nights are bleak and chill
I walk alone in ways remote,
And watch the weary world that still
Goes round without its overcoat;
And while a sick moon rides above
And pale stars keep their watch and ward
I wonder if the eyes of love
Are reading someone else’s card.
N.S.W.
PAT O’MAORI.
Married.
We scabbard in wi’ courtesy
The uaked steel o’ hate,
And so we live our lives along
And so we watch and wait.
And so we face our little world,
And play our little parts ;
H e ha’ bartered both our bodies
But the dead folk hold our hearts.
And in the cosy bright room,
Wi’ all the curtains drawn,
There is nothing left to pray for
But the coming o’ the dawn.
And oftentimes there one will turn
An’ strike a sudden light,
To rid the breathing darkness
O’ the ghosts that haunt the night.
Our heads upon one pillow rest,
But we’ve neither chick nor wean,
An’ its sad an’ sore an’ sorry
lien the dead folk peer between.
S. AUSTKALIA.
LILLIAS GORDON.

A Halley Lorry carrying Wool in the Rough Back Country.
The “Man-on-the-land”
Appreciates the Halley.
For the heavy transport of wool and produce in the rough out-back country
the Halley Motor Lorry has proved invaluable. It will carry bigger loads
than horse teams, carry them quicker and at less cost.
The speediness of the Halley alone is an advantage. It practically brings
the markets nearer and often means the gaining of the best prices. The
Halley, moreover, unlike horses, does not tire. It performs its work at
the end of a long trip with undiminished efficiency.
All models of the Halley built for Australia embody the recommendations of
an expert, who was sent out specially to study local conditions. The Chassis
has an extra-high clearance, is fitted with steel wheels, extra-large radiator
that cools the engine in the hottest weather, special axles, springs, frame,
and dust-proof chain cases, and a “fool proof” gear that can be operated
even by a novice.
Arrange for a trial demonstration. Free literature on request.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
papei (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions , ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, Tiie Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
emen loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 19, 1914.
THE DEVELOPMENT OF OUR
ESTEEMED BROWNISH FRIEND.
The lengthy and muffled argument between
Japan and the United States re the Japanese
demand for free access to California and the
other States of Abraham Lincoln’s Union
has been temporarily concluded. It isn’t
likely that it is permanently concluded, be-
cause the debate between the white man and
the colored one is endless, but things are
patched up for the moment. Nippon pre-
sented something dimly suggestive of an ulti-
matum, and the Government and Congress
at Washington climbed down. The Jap is to
have the free run of the United States,
until something happens. The color, line is
thrown overboard, and the pomp of the Pil-
grim Fathers is up a tree. Some people
might regard the claims of Japan as almost
outrageous, but’the Japanese, with his bland
front and his inscrutable countenance, is
capable of claiming almost anything. He is
often outrageous, but he is so plausible that
he seldom looks that way. His profession is
that he freely admits foreigners into his
country, therefore his people should be freely
admitted into other countries. And it is quite
true that he freely admits foreigners into
his country—in fact, he receives them with
open arms, and a beaming smile, and a
geisha, and a yoshiwarra, and all manner
of gay appurtenances. But when they are in
he makes it so impossible, by his thousand
small restrictions and his million pin-prick
regulations, for them to do business that
they seldom take any Jap. legal tender away
with them. What they are allowed to do is
to go round viewing the sights and the girls,
and spending money. The white mau isn’t
so agile at the pin-prick policy, so when he
lets Nippon into his country he finds that
Nippon has come to stay, and then he gets
angry in public and begins to dash things
round openly. In fact, he is short of plausi-
bility. Here are a few recent records:—
Japanese
December Foreigners Japanese U.S. citizens in U.S.
31- in Japan. abroad. in Japan. Territory.
1906 .. 18,970 97,166 1650 80,453
1908 .. 17,893 326,161 1684 219,106
The later statistics are still unfinished, but
even so far as the completed records go the
Nipponese were doing very well at the work
of keeping the white man out of their land
and pushing their way into his land. Their
ferocious clamor about the other fellow’s
objection to a free and unlimited Japanese
influx is interesting in the circumstances.
* * * *
As already mentioned, the United States
has climbed down. The Pacific Coast, where
the trouble lies, is a long way from Washing-
ton, just as Australia is a long way from
London. And as Washington, like London,
hasn’t the Asiatic at its own door, it can’t
see why the other place, which has the
Asiatic, not only at the door but right inside
the house, should feel annoyed. The same
sort of thing happens in Canada, where Brit-
ish Columbia, on the Pacific Coast, wants
to keep the Mongol out, and the Govern-
ment at Ottawa, which is far east and quite
unaffected by the intruder, can’t imagine
what the disturbance is about. The Legis-
latures at Washington and Ottawa and Lon-
don know all about the undesirable from
Continental Europe because he lands on their
personal doorstep. At various times they
have made most stringent laws against him.
But they fail to see why the undesirable from
Asia should be objected to, seeing that he
doesn’t land where the Cabinet can person-
ally view and consider and smell him. That
is where Australia, unless it makes far more
strenuous efforts for its own defence, is likely
to fall in. At present it is almost as depen-
dent on London for the maintenance of its
white policy as California is on Washington,
and California has found Washington verv
much what Isaiah, the prophet, found
Egypt. When the Brown Man’s veiled ulti-
matum comes this way there are reasons why
London is likely to climb down even more
suddenly than Washington has done.
In the first place, Japan is Britain’s ally
while it isn’t Uncle Sam’s ally. A quarrel
with Japau would mean that the fighting
value of some 50 big ships (to say nothing of
a lot of small ones) would be subtracted from
the. British strength and added to the list
of its enemies. That would make a differ-
ence of 100 big ships. To the U.S. a quarrel
with Japan would only mean a difference of
50 ships. The British Empire, so far as
population goes, is mostly colored and
Asiatic, and J. Bull has a horror of draw-
ing the color line and of speaking uncivilly
to any colored Asiatic lest he should knock
the prop from underneath the throne. The
United States Dominion is mostly white, and
it has drawn the colored line so many times
already, and in so mahy ways, that it could
hardly make things much worse by drawing
the same old line once more. Britain would
have the great bulk of its empire at stake
in a serious naval war, for most of the
scattered premises of Geo. V. are only de-
fensible by sea. The United States hazards,
in a sea campaign on the Asiatic side, the
Philippines, which it has almost decided to
abandon in any case; the scrappy Kanaka
islands of Hawaii, Guam (210 square miles),
a fragment of Samoa, and possibly, but not
probably, the frost-bitten district of Alaska.
The kernel of the American Empire is the
States themselves —nearly 3,000,000 square
miles—and only the small and comparatively
valueless residue is liable to be captured by
salt-water aggression. On the other hand
Britain has 11,300,000 square miles of
scattered and accessible possessions, depen-
dent for defence in the last extremity on
the ships of a little North Sea archipelago
of 121,000 square miles. Again, Britain has
its merchant fleets on every coast, all liable
to capture in case of a big scrap on the
waters, while the U.S. has no mercantile
marine of any importance to be seized. And
Britain’s unutterable dread is a naval re-
verse, because it now depends almost en-
tirely on imported food, and a comparatively
short blockade might bring down its pride
and dignity and power like a house of cards.
The U.S. can more than feed itself, and 100
years of blockade would hardly starve it
into submission. It might even amuse itself
during the blockade by annexing Mexico and
Canada and Central America, and become
greater than ever. On the face of things the
United States is a dozen times better quali-
fied than Britain to consign to the Devil any
Asiatic Power which objects to its immigra-
tion laws. Yet Japan hinted, gently and
diplomatically, at the possibility of trouble
over the question of White California, and,
as hereinbefore described, Washington
climbed down. When Japan drops the same
kind of hint re the question of White Aus-
tralia, and London climbs down, and this
country, with its empty Northern Territory,
and its unbuilt northern railway, and its
exhausted credit, and its army of school
children, and its mere semblance of a navy
must either give in or set up for itself and
fight its own battle ! ! !
* * * #
There is one more reason why London is
even more likely to give way than Washing-
ton when the Japanese lodges his protest
against White Australia as he has already
done against White California. The United
States, now that the Panama Canal is in
working order, could concentrate all its naval
force against the enemy, and it is no small
naval force when everything is counted. And
it could rush all its available troops to oppose
a possible landing of the enemy. It wouldn’t
be afraid lest the Mexican hosts should take
the country while its regiments were other-
wise engaged, for Mexico has no hosts —-only
brigands. But Britain has the German bogey
looking over its back fence. It must, with
its dependence on imported food, retain
enough ships in its home waters to abso-
lutely ensure itself against this spectre. And
when that is done there aren’t enough ships
left, evep in the stupendous British fleet, to
defend the White Australia policy should
Japan become hostile in real earnest, assum-
ing that Father Bull was serious in
the defence of the White Australia
policy. Britain under present conditions
couldn’t send so big a fleet against Nippon
as the United States could —the republic
that has just climbed down, as once or twice
alluded to in the course of this article. It
might help our land defence materially with
its handy and numerous Indian troops, but if
White Australia had to fall back on a col-
ored soldiery it wouldn’t have much white
dignity left, and it could never draw the color
line again without feeling that it was some-
thing of an ass and a good deal of a fraud.
* * * *
The moral of it all is that this country
doesn’t take its white status seriously. It is
too much addicted to relying on paper
statutes and regarding the thing as settled
because it is formally enacted, and duly
printed in black ink on white paper. The
baby fleet, the army of infants, the absurd
factory which turns out one gun-barrel or
more every three months, the neglect of
immigration, the policy which makes no pro-
vision for the defence of the north-western
half of Australia save by appointing a horse-
doctor to be a sort of viceroy at Darwin —
all these matters will look very small when
London is up against the question which has
just been put to Washington. And if the
Jap. is a consistent sort of person he will
assuredly put the question to London as soon
as he is ready. He has had the subject on
his mind for quite a while.
DACEYVILLE.
Fkom J. D. Fitzgerald, chairman of the
N.S.W. Housing Board :
In The Bulletin of 29/1/T4 appeared the follow-
ing :
“And even Daceyville is in trouble; a recent
deputation declared that the rents were too high,
and a harassed Labor Minister said that the buildings
had cost so much that, even at the present allegedly
high rents, the place was a dead loss and a pauper
institution. So the interview ended to the dissatis-
faction of all parties.’’
I was not fortunate enough to see in print the
allegation of the “harassed Minister” that “the place
was a dead loss and a pauper institution.” If he did
say so, it must have been through want of knowledge
of the true facts. Let me place them before the
nublic; for the matter is of the highest public in-
terest and importance.
Here follows a description of the excellent
fashion in which Daceyville is laid out. To
this The Bulletin makes no objection
whatsoever.
* * * *
The business part of the scheme is prescribed by
law. The rents must be fixed at such a rate as will
bring a return sufficient to pay 4 p.c. on land and
buildings, together with assurances, rates, taxes,
repairs and maintenance, a share of the expenses of
administration and a sinking fund on a 50 years’
basis. The land was charged against the scheme at
£65 per acre, and the estimated cost of “subdivision
work”—such as drainage, levelling, road-making,
kerbing and guttering, etc.—was £312 per acre.
Every charge which a private owner makes against
his property save one—profit—is made against the
scheme. The State desires no profit save what is
gained to the whole community in the enhancement
of the comfort, health and happiness of the people
who live in it; and the comparatively small relief
(none the less valuable as an example) which it
affords to the terrible hygienic and moral problem of
housing- congestion in Sydney.
The rents are, of course, based upon the
cost of the scheme and the sinking-fund period.
It will be seen that the tenants pay for the whole
scheme. It cannot, under these circumstances, be a
“pauper institution,” nor is there the slightest sus-
picion of charity in the matter. The rents for the
first cottages were fixed at 17s , but a re-appraise-
ment was found necessary (the Board having had to
fix the rents before the returns of cost were available
for the first houses built), and as these houses become
vacant the tenants will pay 18s. For the same class
of cottage in Mosman or any similar suburb, on land
half the size, you cannot find a rent lower than
£1 ss. to £1 10s. For the second class of cottage
the rent is 14s. 6d. Besides the modern conveniences
mentioned above the cottages have verandahs back
and front, specially designed for outdoor sleeping.
* * * *
And then conies another mass of litera-
ture about the pleasant life led at Dacey-
ville, which, as this paper cast not one
single brick at the hygienic aspect of that
suburb, will be taken as read. It is hereby
fully admitted unless evidence to the con-
trary turns up.
But Mr. Fitzgerald admits, after all,
that Daceyville isn’t quite financial, because
houses are let at 17s. a week which should
have been rented at 18s. in order to make
the scheme solvent. That was, perhaps,
what the Minister was thinking of when he
told the deputation 'that asked for reduced
rents (Mr. Fitzgerald says the rents aie
surprisingly low already) that the enter-
prise wasn’t paying its way, which cer-
tainly implied pauperism. On this matter
the Auditor-General has made some remarks.
He said that the shortage for the first year,
when the buildings were still new
and the accounts for repair and reno-
vation hadn’t begun to come in, was
£207, and as the sinking fund contribu-
tion was £97, that meant that there was no
payment whatever to the sinking fund, and
a considerable deficit besides. Also he said
that a sinking fund scheme, which was to
repay capital in 50 years, assumed a remark-
ably long life for the Daceyville cottages.
In these circumstances this paper fails to
see what reason Mr. Fitzgerald has to com-
plain of its observations. It didn’t say a
word against the sanitation or plan or
methods of Daceyville—only about the very
casual finances. When the year’s liabilities
are £1357, and the year’s income is £lO9O,
and the year’s deficit is £267, or nearly 20
per cent., it certainly seems that somebody is
living, in part at least, a pauper existence on
somebody—presumably on the general tax-
payer. And the Auditor-General added that
the interest on the capital value of the land
had not been charged in the accounts.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Gentle Art of Lurking.
Dr. Fiaschi, of Sydney, appears to be at
times one of those people who see ghosts in
the dark. Speaking of a perfectly ordinary
and commonplace and respectable part of
Australia, he is reported to have spoken thus
to the Medical Congress in Maoriland: —
It should be incumbent on the officers to carry out
a strict physical examination of all young male in-
habitants, to make a searching test of the soundness,
strength and virility of the rising generation. The
second problem dealt with a possibility of having
to send an expeditionary force to the Northern
Territory, which was likely to become a point of
invasion. A great number of unknown diseases pro-
bably lurk in the Territory.
Now why should unknown diseases “lurk”—
“lurk” is a very fine word —in one thinly-
peopled section of North Australia, when
they don’t lurk in the more populated sec-
tions which occupy the same latitude, and
are just as hot, and often a great deal more
damp. Up the west coast of Australia mys-
terious horrors don’t lurk worth 2d. Nor
do they lurk worth 3*d. up the east coast,
for Rockhampton and Townsville and
Mackay and Cairns and Cooktown are good
enough. They don’t lurk to the extent of
an old pair of boots in the top parts of
Queensland and the Territory, for quite a
healthy population lives there. They don’t
lurk on the farthest horizon to the south, at
the end of the unfinished railway from Port
Augusta. For many, many years line re-
pairers have ridden from end to end of the
Territory keeping the transcontinental tele-
graph in order, and missionaries and pro-
spectors and police and even a bishop, a
university professor and a State Governor
have crossed the province from end to end,
and not a single lurk did they ascertain.
TLjy were strong men and armed, and they
badly wanted to bring some stuffed lurks
back with them, yet they got nothing. Why
should it always be assumed that there is a
skeleton in the unopened cupboard, and that
all manner of horrors are lying low in the
coal-scuttle in the shadow, and that there is
a spook round the next corner? Every place
is a scene of mystery to a credulous and
superstitious generation which hasn’t been
there. That was why England was once
supposed to be an isle where the ghosts of
the dead wandered in misery, wringing their
hands on the present site of London, and
weeping wretchedly all round Birmingham
and Liverpool— not to mention Wolverhamp-
ton.
♦
Some of the Realities of War.
“The Old Soldier” writes: —
What the S. M. Herald called “one of the most
important discussions of the Political Labor League
Conference” befell in Sydney the other day. 'Two
local leagues submitted resolutions asking for the
abolition of compulsory military training, and
another proposal from the sub-committee on Federal
affairs called for a ltoyal Commission to inquire into
the whole Defence scheme. Then Stuart-Robertson,
M.L.A., said that he had “no sympathy with the
idea that Acts of Parliament could make either
capable or enthusiastic soldiers out of unwilling
citizens.”
He might have added that no Act of Parliament
can make enthusiastic taxpayers out of unwilling
economists, or enthusiastic washers out of unwilling
dirty people, or enthusiastic prisoners out of unwill-
ing burglars, drunks, assault-and-battery men, and
bigamists; nor can compulsion turn unwilling non-
unionists into enthusiastic unionists. And the best
brand of religion can’t turn unwilling sinners after
death into enthusiastic damned. It might even be
added that when Stuart-Robf.rtson finds his dog
raiding the dinner-table, and the mongrel is unwill-
ing to depart, he can’t turn that animal into an
enthusiastic fasting beast in the back yard by chasing
it out.
But, after all, why all this talk about the “unwill-
ing citizen” in war matters? This country has adult
suffrage in all Federal affairs, and unless the
majority is in favor of compulsory military training,
how did the thing get on to the statute-book and
stay there? Of course no one, save a fool, really
imagines that it is the Act of Parliament itself that
is to turn the unwilling loafer or drifter into a sol-
dier. That is the job of the drill sergeant, author-
ised by the Act of Parliament.
J. C. Watson threw himself into the breach, but
was interrupted in this fashion: —
A Delegate: “Perhaps you have something in the
country to defend.”
The remark made Watson somewhat angry, and
like Jonah (chapter 4, verse 9) he had justification.
Australia has the most liberal suffrage on earth. It
has had more Labor Government than any other
country ,in modern times. Yet there are still Labor-
ites who profess that the people of Australia, and
the Labor party of Australia, have made such a hash
of their opportunities that the country isn’t worth
defending against a foreign invader. They don’t think
their liberties, and their homes, and their wives
and children, and their trades unions, and their old-
age pensions, and their Arbitration Courts, and their
eight-hours procession good enough to amount to
a stake in the country.
Of course, it goes without saying that these folk
never had hostile soldiers quartered on them, and
saw foreign troops putting their muddy feet on the
dining-room table, and pawing the women and girls
of the household, and eating up the victuals, and
plundering the household treasures, including the
baby’s photo, and the beer and the Saturday night's
wages. They were never hanged for objecting to the
ways of the invaders, or had their feet put in the
fire to make them reveal the whereabouts of imagin-
ary savings. They haven’t yet been flogged for being
out after dark contrary to martial law, or shot for
refusal to work on the basis of bare rations upon the
invader’s fortifications, or otherwise tortured and
scragged because they objected to being the hostile
army’s beasts of burden. All these things are liable
to happen in war time to the nation which hasn’t
learned the complete art of defence—especially to
the nation which is the white man’s most lonesome
and undefended outpost, and which has angered
a warlike community 10 times its size by its nigger
exclusion policy. And all these things do happen,
even in our days of alleged civilisation, and happen
frequently. In the late Balkan war, men and women
and children had their hands and feet cut off, their
eyes put out and their tongues amputated, and there
was unpleasantness all round—by reason of an
Asiatic enemy who was no worse than some that
Australia may have to meet before long.
Yet, as already mentioned, there are blithered
patriots in this country who at once suspect any
advocate of defence of being a bloated capitalist and
the possessor of portable property. The idea is
strangely wooden-headed. It isn’t the bloated capi-
talist who suffers most in war time. Sometimes he
buys off the invader, and sometimes he flees in his
motor-car, .and gets out of the trouble. He needn’t
stay unless he wants to. But the man who can't
bolt and who has nothing to offer by way of ransom
save his wife and daughters and his own body and
his own slave-labor, is liable to experience a bad
time. His wages stop because the factory or shop
is shut up. His little savings vanish because the
savings bank closes owing to its assets being nnreaiis-
able. His life-assurance policy might as well be on
the planet Jupiter. In fact, he is simply the in-
vader’s famished dog, and he hasn’t a dog license to
protect him.

Six Millions.
It has been seriously announced that it is
necessary to spend another £6,000,000 on
providing extended wharfage for the port of
Sydney. A great deal of this sum will go
to private landlords for the repurchase of soil
at inflated prices, said prices having been
inflated by the policy which aims at drag-
ging all the commerce of a huge State to
one city regardless of convenience or ex-
pense. It isn’t suggested that the outlay
will return big dividends ; that it will help
along the policy of State land-ownership:
that it will save places like Monaro and
Riverina and New England paying absurd
and ruinous railway freights that their ex-


ports may be carried to a remote port when
there is an equally good one close at hand ,
that it will make the Sydney house famine
any less acute; or that it will alleviate tne
lot of the sheep which spends a week with-
out food or water in the truck on the long
and overburdened railway that leads to tne
Only Port. None of these matters come into
the calculation. If the amount in question
was devoted to building two scraps ot rail-
way to connect the existing N.S.W. lines
with Port Stephens and Twofold Bay, to
constructing a few wharves there, and to
resuming adjacent lands at their existing
value —which is not much more than prairie
or wilderness value —something might hap-
pen. That is to say, the State might be-
come sole ground landlord of two new cities
which would, before very many years had
passed, be as great as Sydney. In other
words, the ground each city stood upon
would be worth something like £45,000,000.
That would be a very fine asset for the
State, as well as a relief to the Sydney
house scarcity and the railway pressure,
and a magnificent vindication of the policy
of public landlordism. Unfortunately, the
so-called Labor Government of N.S.W. —
partly composed of Sydney landlords —seems
to be too busy looking after the dirty,
squalid, miserable interests of Toryism to
consider the large national propositions on
its programme. The N.S.W. Tory pressis
centred in Sydney, and has its biggest in-
vestments and profits there. It is com-
mitted. in its own interests, to the Conges-
tion idea. And so long as the Holman
Government sinks those features of its pro-
gramme which are really hateful to the old
somnolent crowd, the Tory press has no rea-
son to be any more disagreeable than it can
avoid while “saving its face.” It may have
been that the politically-petrified papers re-
garded some of the second ballot results at
last State election as suspicious. They may
have even viewed them as outrageous. Still
they said very little; in fact, they said
almost nothing. Why should they throw the
clod of exposure at a Cabinet which, if
roused to fury, might get level by honestly
carrying out its policy of decentralisation
and its creed of the public ownership of
land? It might even go farther, and carry
out its pledge to reduce borrowing and to
spend loan money only upon works which
will nay full interest and sinking fund, and
the financial profligates of the Sydney daily
press would regard that kind of integrity as
Sheol. If the Tory backsliding of the
Holman Government earns the good-
humored forbearance of the backslid press of
Sydney, the press certainly gets good value
in return.

The Pious Press Begins to Mention It.
Last week both the S. M. Herald and the
Sydney Evening News struggled suffici-
ently over the barrier of their false modesty
to hint that syphilis should be made a notifi-
able disease, like such comparatively unim-
portant troubles as small-pox and consump-
tion and plague and leprosy; and that a
national campaign should be started against
it. There is really more need nowadays for
a national campaign against syphilis than
against all other diseases put together. It
falls alike on the just and the unjust. It
is handed down from generation to genera-
tion, and the person who has innocently
acquired it may pass it along to other inno-
cent people in many ways. It may not
directly kill many people, but it leads to
blindness, idiocy, paralysis, locomotor ataxia,
skin annoyances, general debility, baldness,
bow-legs, knock knees, dwarfs, hunchbacks
and every kind of human decay and dry-rot.
For a long, long time one of the polite con-
ventions of Snuffledom was to the effect that
this complaint should not be alluded to save
indirectly, and that its existence should be
ignored. There was a sort of impression or
mental haze that it might be wiped out by a
process of disqualification, just as the Alps
might be flattened by making it illegal to
mention them. Now Snuffle is itself being
disqualified and flattened out, and there is
hope that the worst evil known to man will
be seriously attacked. The fact that the
S. M. Herald and Evening News have
begun to mention it by name, instead of
alluding to it vaguely as the inward thing-
umbob or the devastating whatyoumaycallit,
is a sign of progress.

Some Notes and Queries with a Bearing
on Women’s Suffrage.
Dear Bulletin, —Discussion in the Vic.
Assembly on the question of equal pay for
both sexes of State school teachers has
weakened this child’s enthusiasm for the
equality doctrine. There must be something
in the Watt Government’s contention that
the “injustice to women” is a pardonable
economy, because the men, as a rule, give
life-long service to the State, whereas 300
women teachers, on an average, leave the
service every year to get married. It might
be added that a large percentage of the 300
take male teachers to husband. Surely the
young man, who doesn’t dream of quitting
his job to get married, is likely to be a more
valuable servant than the young woman who
does. Anyhow, the Labor members showed
a strange lack of imagination when they pro-
tested that any reference to the annual
exodus of brides-elect was “beside the ques-
tion” of equal pay for equal work. It is
more than questionable whether an unearnest
young spinster with matrimony in her spark-
ling eye is as valuable a worker as the young
man (single or married) who has his profes-
sional prospects in view. I would suggest
that this hard and fast Parliamentary swear-
ing by the equality of the sexes is mostly a
bid for the women’s vote on polling day. A
politician bidding for a vote will say any-
thing. For instance, G. M. Prendergast
professed to favor higher pay for women
teachers on the ground that “the more they
were cared for the less likely they were te
hastily marry.” After which he assured the
House that “a decreasing birthrate followed
when people of comparative poverty married.”
Which was so transparently untruthful that
Orangeman Snowball interjected: “The
highest birthrate is among the noorest
people.” as a gentle reminder that Prender-
gast had better shut up. If all the arguments
in favor of treating women as men for edu-
cational business purposes (and making the
taxpavers provide the extra expense) are no
sounder than Prendergast’s amazing argu-
ments, it is time for everybody to shut up.
Are all women workers to receive higher
wages as an inducement for them to refrain
from getting married, as soon as possible?
Is the State to act as an anti-matrimonial
agency? Are we to assume that an addition
of a few pounds a year to a school teacher-
ess’s salary for a few years would enable her
to make an “excellent match,” when she was
getting a bit old in the tooth, whereas the
denial of the increment causes her to rush
into a married state of comparative poverty
while she is young and blooming? I repeat
my allegation that a politician bidding for
votes will say anything. Thus the extension
of the franchise to women has deepened the
profundity of my disrespect for politicians.
Formerly they had only the men’s votes to bid
for, and they were at least reasonable, ac-
cording to their ancient lights, in discussing
questions concerning payment of women.
Now, they have also to curry favor with the
less reasonable sex, and they talk nonsense
on both sides, the nonsense they sometimes
utter on the female side of their constituency
—when they are agin the Government —be-
ing audaciously stupid. How else are we to
account for the Labor party’s voting to in-
crease the Estimates by hundreds of pounds
in order that women school teachers may de-
lay their entrance into matrimony, and
become larger producers of offspring on
account of the delay?—Yours etc., “0.K.”
The Sydney Meat Convulsion.
So far as the public convenience or incon-
venience goes the Sydney meat strike is, or
was—it may be over by the time this appears
in nrint—a harmless sort of business. There
will always be a certain amount of beef
hanging round at a price; also there remain
bacon and ham and tinned foods and vege-
tables and sundries for the over-fed Fat Man
to fall back upon. The affluent stomach gets
through somehow. The bullock doesn’t mind
waiting a day or two longer in the paddock
to be killed. The anguished Railway De-
partment is glad of a rest from stock trains,
so that it may try its hand at moving the
wheat harvest. And the fact that one more
agreement has been burst up and one more
boulder thrown at the industrial arbitration
principle hardly matters now, for there is
only a tattered rag of the arbitration prin-
ciple left. The strikers were working under
an agreement—it is wholly probable that
the wages fixed by the agreement were in-
adequate as alleged—and the agreement was
broken just as casually as if it had been a
Mexican general’s oath of allegiance. No
one thought of keeping it when it didn’t suit,
and no one thought it worth while to be
seriously angry about the breakage, for that
was all in the day’s work. Nowadays when
an industrial trouble happens the Arbitra-
tion Court counts for little; union officials
for less; the Minister for Labor and Indus-
try for nothing at all. The attitude of
trades-unionism is that it doesn’t know
enough to create a Government that knows
enough to supply a legal remedy for the
grievances of its supporters. So it runs its
show by sporadic insurrection against its
officials and its Ministry and its Parlia-
mentary majority and its programme and
its own industrial organisations. In a
manner of speaking, it kicks itself along.
As the chronic rebellion isn’t done in the
chronic Mexican fashion with guns and
dynamite, and as it is generally a perfectly
good-humored and gentlemanly shindy, no-
body is badly hurt. Worse things happen
at a football match. The real harm or
absurdity lies in the fact that every strike
is a declaration on the part of Labor that
it is too great an ass to make a success of
one of its most cherished ideals. Even
though it owns the whole machinery of
State it says that it doesn’t know how to
get decent wages for a butcher’s assistant
save by the clumsy, wasteful methods which
it had to adopt when its enemies owned the
machinery. The position seems illogical,
but there is nothing in it that is worth get-
ting really wild and apoplectic about. It
isn’t a bad thing to have a meat stoppage.
This community eats too much hot. fat beef,
especially in the summer time, and the best
thing it can do at a flesh crisis is to buy a
water melon and remain calm.
About Slouch, Junior.
The Victorian military authorities have dis-
covered that a large proportion of the shirk-
ing cadets who are run into the Queenscliff
camp to do their arrears of drill are not
Quakers, nor youths with other varieties of
religious scruple, but cigarette fiends. Also
they are mostly dirty about the face, and
matted about the hair, and shambling about
the feet, and the post-leaning habit has got
them badly. As a rule the clean, perpendicu-
lar boy who doesn’t smoke attends his drill
voluntarily; while the unclean, sloping one,
with the frequent curse in his mouth and the
brown stain on his fingers, has to be chased
to the last extremity. But when he is cap-
tured, after a long race, and regularly
washed and bugged, and has his hair cut and
his cigarette supply stopped, and is well fed,
and made to stick out his chest in the light
of Heaven, and do vigorous exercise, the re-
sults are marvellous. Even in a couple of
weeks the improvement of Slouch, junior, is
often beyond belief. Still there are big diffi-
culties, for when young Slouch is in the pre-
liminary stages _ of his detention on Swan
Island, and is in anguish for want of his
little paper-and-tobacco abomination* it is
about as difficult to keep him from smuggling
in cigarettes as it is to tear an opium fiend
from his drug. Curiously enough it is said
that young Slouch, the military defaulter
in N.S.W., hasn’t quite so gross a craving
for cigarettes as his unseemly brother down
South, but has an extra desire for beer to
make up. In other respects—his ambling
gait, and his dirt, and his hair, and his ten-
dency to flop against some fixed object—he
and his Melbourne relative are alike. And
there is the same capacity for improvement
when the arm of Authority has washed him,
and put him in clean clothes, and taught him
the goose-step which is for the incipient
pushite the beginning of orderly citizenship.

From W. T. Moss, secretary of the Animals’
Protection Society, Sydney :
Another shocking spectacle was witnessed at Flem-
ington Siding yesterday (Feb. 5), when a stock train
arrived from the West. This train was three hours
late, and the owner of the sheep in two of the trucks
will have some cause for complaint. One of our
inspectors was present when the sheep were removed.
The unfortunate animals were so weak from the rough
treatment they had received on the journey that
some difficulty was experienced in detraining them,
and dogs were sent into the trucks over the sheep’s
backs to eject them. Ten sheep were dead in two
trucks; some of them were almost devoid of wool,
and must have been dead over 48 hours. They were
swollen and decomposed. Nine others, in a mangled
and dying condition, were dragged out on to the
siding, and there left in the scorching and swelter-
ing sun from 11 o’clock in the morning until three
o’clock in the afternoon, when I put them out of
their misery, I adopted this course because I was
informed by the carter who removes the dead sheep
that any animal showing any sign of life would be
removed to the abattoirs by the contractor, to be
used for human consumption. The Inspector of the
Meat Industry and Abattoirs Board has been for
some time engaged in killing all dying and mutilated
sheep after their removal from the trucks, and was
so engaged during the nie'ht previous and early morn-
ing, when his duty ended. The state of the law is
such that our Society is almost helpless to cope with
these eases of cruelty, and prosecute with any hope of
success. The sooner it. is altered the better.
The facts regarding the appalling cruelty to
stock in transit and during and immediately
after derailment at Flemington are indisput-
able. The allegations have been proved right
up to the hilt. What is needed now is a set
of explicit proposals from the experts in this
matter to end the atrocities —so far as they
can be ended under a railway system whose
object has been to lead the whole traffic of a
State, live and otherwise, up to the narrow
bottle-neck of a single port, and push it
through somehow, anyhow.
♦ •
The question re the Victorian Closer Settle-
ment Board is not one as to whether it ought
to be dismissed without further ado, but as
to when its unfitness was first proved, and
why the long-time discredited body has man-
aged to exist all these years without being
impeached in Parliament. The Watt
Government is neither more nor less to blame
for the scandalous operations of the Closer
Settlement land buyers and allotters than
previous Governments. Every old member
of the Vic. State Parliament stands con-
victed of responsibility for the Board’s mis-
deeds in the past. The first revelation in the
press of the fact that large sums of good
money had been paid for bad land dates
back to long ago. The cruel disappointment
of the earliest closer settlers is now an
ancient story. But the same old system
went on unchecked whilst matters of minor
importance were taken up and dropped (as
a rule), by the crowd of party politicians.
The'Lands Department must always have
been intimately acquainted with the work-
ings of the Closer Settlement Board of Con-
trol, yet the truth, or some of the truth
about the Board’s amazing land purchases
and want of business capacity never leaked
qut until the Watt Government’s commit-
tee of inquiry published a damning report a
fortnight ago. At the back of the discredited
C.S. Board is the notoriously rotten Lands
Department. It seems that no new Board
can effect thoroughly satisfactory reforms
in the Closer Settlement system until Par-
liament has reformed the Department, where
each transient Minister in turn is made to
appear incapable.

Much may be forgiven to Victoria’s remark-
able magistrate, Read Murphy, for his vig-
orous pronouncement in the case of a drill-
shirking cadet who had a Quaker in court to
sneak for him: “I have no time for a boy
who talks nonsense about conscientious ob-
jections,” said 11. Murphy. Probably it was
the Quaker friend who made Murphy feel
“stuffy.” If the Quakers would settle down
largely in Mexico, Turkey,' Persia and such
like countries, where the Government is weak
and the protecting arm of an irreligious sol-
diery is shortened, and demonstrate the
workable character of their policy of non-
resistance amid real dangers, the world would
begin to take them seriously. There are good
business inducements to lead them to such
countries. In a fertile but troubled land,
where political unrest is the chief bar to
progress, magnificent chances lie before the
man who —by reason of his bright smile and
his broad brim and his faith and his “thee”
and his “thou” —is proof against trouble. A
non-military Quaker has no better chances
in England or the United States than any-
body else, but in Asiatic Turkey, assuming
that there is anything in his doctrines, he
might be a great boom and a bright and
noticeable light. It is the Quaker’s rigid
aversion to places where his views would
have a fair show that makes the unregener-
ate scoff at him. He is a gun with an in-
scription attached to it: “Thi« is an Infalli-
ble Weapon, but it Must Not be Fired
because it Will Burst.”
The N.S.W. Labor Conference, at the bid-
ding of Delegate Blakely (A.W.U.), de-
clared for a straight-out campaign against
syphilis. One woman-delegate, who was
especially effective, cited a case where a
mother was infected by her son’s kiss, and
demanded that the male “unfortunate” be
treated the same as the female “unfortu-
nate.” Another speaker declared that
women were interested on selfish grounds—
first to guard themselves, then to guard
their children, and last but greatest duty of
all, to guard the race from a preventable
horror, worse than any other infective hor-
ror known to man. The 1914 N.S.W. con-
ference of Labor-rulers was worth while if
only for this bold declaration, and the
Holman crowd had better hustle its slow-
moving Minister Flowers if it doesn’t want
its own directors to presently stamp right
over it in sheer exasperation.
Judge Rogers, of N.S.W., last week sympa-
thetically described “two-up” as “a perfectly
fair game,” and then, like a fair-minded man,
made things as easy as he possibly could for
some unfortunate pupil of a raided academy
who had appealed against his police-court
conviction for playing an unlawful game.
The Law is a worse thistle-eater than usual
where gambling is concerned —and all life is
made up of gambles. It never bursts into a
high-class club in search of gamblers, nor
breaks up Mrs. Midgeley-Blatters’ bridge
afternoon, and tramples the best china to
death in an effort to reform the Best People.
The land-agent who advertises a low-
lying swamp “beautiful park land” is
allowed to work his swindle uproariously.
Mrs. Insufficient Nourishment, the baby
adopter (on a financial basis) plays the game
of “one down” for keeps in the columns of
the respectable Liberal press, and never a
helmet looks over her backyard fence or the
front counter of the evil rag that prints her
sinister advertisement. The discolored alien
in search of a fool girl-theatrical to act (or
otherwise —mostly otherwise) in the un-
drained and miscalled “East” tries his very
private little game in the same way, and even
when some policeman “moves,” or rolls over
in bed. it is done very gradually and after
considerable reflection. Then the rogue-
politician, who has an elevated swindle of his
own, libels the land that keeps him alive—or
all that part of it in which he not directly
interested. Even marriage, with which the
Law frequently fools and interferes, is very
often a swindle of the worst description.
Really, when one comes to stand off and
view the matter in all its aspects, “two up”
seems to be the only fair game on the list.
“Vagabond,” from an English shipyard :
“A III.,” writing of the last naval manoeuvres here
in British iced-waters, concludes from the cables
that the submarines were so destructive that they
were called off the job before they burst the big ships
and spoiled the war-play. I wonder the cable didn’t
blush to twist the facts so. Actually the sub-
marines were severely handicapped and in many cases
were adjudged “out of action” by the scouting of
the aeroplanes. A submarine under water and in-
visible from a ship or shore is perfectly plain to the
vision of the flying man; and time “ after time a
’plane was able to spot the sub. sneaking out on
the battleships, and by signalling its whereabouts
enabled the warship to drop a spouting whirlpool of
shells on top of the underwater craft. The exploding
of a few dozen shells from a 13in. gun in the
waters, round, under and over the submerged craft,
is not conducive to their health or happiness, and
the judges, at any rate, promptly ruled out the theor-
etically bombarded ones.
“A ITT ” says, again, that the result of the war-
game shifted the bogey of invasion off J. Bull’s
chest. On the contrary, it demonstrated that invasion
was a simple matter. Desnite the larger defending
fleet, the attackers were able to land armed parties,
destroy (in theory) important ports and railwav
depbts, coal, stores, oil and other etceteras—all
under close watch of the judges, who clocked off the
movements of the landing parties, and made them
take such time as the actual operations would have
eaten up. The common and accepted belief here was
that the maribsuvres were hurriedly closed down
because the success of the invasion was a proof of the
Australia’s Greatest Statesman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Prime Minister (Mr. Joseph Cook), on
arriving home yesterday from Canberra: “I went to
see what was going on there. It was several years
since I was last there, and very great changes have
taken place since then.... I was very pleased with
all I saw. It is to be very much wished that more
of our members will run up and see whac '; taking
place. There have been some little troubles there,
incidental to camp life—difficulties in obtaining water
in sufficient quantities. Personally, I have very great
sympathy with the men, who are being put to so
much inconvenience.”
’Twas Australia’s Greatest Statesman —and
his other name was Cook —
Who informed the dazed reporter that he’d
been to have a look
At “the Capital”—Stoneberra—whfere the
dust storms do their sprints—
It was years ago he’d seen it, and, there’d
been some changes since.”
Then that bold and bad reporter, in his bold
reporter’s way,
Asked Australia’s Greatest Statesman if he’d
generously say
Where and why and what great changes had
occurred to move him so;
Was the dear, old pub still standing? And
that Statesman answered “No!”
“1 believe they have a railway?” said that
bold reporter, next;
“Oh, they have,” replied the Statesman; “I
will take it as my text.
It’s a very charming railway, or it will be
when it’s done;
And it comes straight into Sydney, where all
railways ought to run !
“At the moment —you’ll believe me—though
it isn’t finished, quite,
I can confidently answer that the railway is
all right;
For the survey is completed, and the track
is being laid,
Though the route is rather dusty and there
isn’t too much shade.”
“How about the rocks and mountains, and
the gritty paddocks there?”
(Then inquired that bold reporter with a
mild, persuasive air).
“Er — oh—rcr-r-ah, yes ; exactly!” said the
Statesman; “through the dust
I just saw them* still : they’ll be
there —of cofirse, they must!”
“And the site- —is that still standing?” asked
the pressman. “Yes, indeed!
All the roads and parks and buildings still
exist upon the screed;
And they’re laying pipes and fountains, and
they’re excavating tanks,
And I couldn’t tell you what-all, such as fac-
tories and banks.
“And they’re planning great skyscrapers for
the papers—when thev start;
Papers which will move the rabbits and
gohannas to the heart!
And they’ve got huge lakes and rivers on the
Plan they showed to me;
And, although there is no grass there, I am
told they have a tree.”
“Heavens!” cried that bold reporter; “I
must really put that down !”
(Here he scribbled “ Splendid timber — trees
to ornament the town.)
While the Statesman smiled serenely, and
remarked, “A gum- tree too;
Though they never named the species—red
or white or green or blue.”
“And” (continued the reporter) “how about
the weather there?”
Said the Statesman, looking thoughtful, “It
was—well—er —only fair ;
For, you see, it was mid-summer, and the
sun was very hot:
Still, I don’t think I should say so—No;
perhaps you’d better not!”
“Any rain?” (asked that reporter, as he felt
for a fresh pad),
“Not exactly,” said the Statesman, “though
they need it very bad :
Say—er— dew fell last September—yes, I
think if I were you.
And your editor would like it, I’d say, ‘Some
nights there is dew.’ ”
“But, great Caesar!” yelled the pressman
in the accents of despair,
“If there’s neither rain nor moisture, what
the devil is there there?”
“Hush!” remarked the Great Man sadly;
“you won’t go to Kingdom Come!”
“Well, what do they do for water?” But
the Statesman he Was dumb.
N.S.W.
R. McGEDDON.

HEIGHT Increased
If you are not over 40 years of age I can
Increase Your Height by from two to three
inches within three months.
No Medicines. Ordinary Habits Retained.
No appliances or drugs of any kind are used,
and it is impossible for my system to cause
overstrain or to injure the health in any
way. On the contrary, my students are in-
variably astonished at the tremendous improve-
ment in their health which my course pro-
duces.
All the time required to be devoted is 10
minutes morning and evening.
I do not ask you to make any drastic altera-
tion in your diet, your smoking, or your mode
of life.
GROW TALL, MY SYSTEM WILL
SHOW YOU HOW.
Send two penny stamps with your name and
address, for further particulars.
HUGH GIBSON,
Specialist in the
