need of a stronger British navy for defence, and the
Liberal Government doesn’t hanker for any more
arguments in favor of the Big Navy views.
I agree that Australia would find submarines useful
in case of an invasion, but I believe a fleet of air-
craft would be invaluable. The invader’s ships would
certainly cart along a number of the underwater
craft, and they’d play Old Henry with our fleet if
we have no ’planes to pick them out and drop a
few explosive bricks on top of them. If a final
argument is required of the value of the ’planes, it
can be found in the programme that was in full
operation within a month of the end of the navy
manoeuvres—a circle of shore stations are to be estab-
lished, orders were placed with private firms for
’planes, and so on. It was immediately laid down by
responsible Ministers that Britain must establish the
largest and best air-fleet in Europe; and Bull is
bogging in hard to do so.
The “Living Wage.”
Justice Heydon, of N.S.W., lias issued a
sort of verdict about the cost of living, but
he hasn’t thereby done much good for the
world. The most important feature of his
finding is that a family of one man, his wife
(one wife) and two children aged under 14
can live in a house of three rooms and a
kitchen on a wage of 48s. per week. And at
present rents and prices they probably can
—bar accidents and incidents. But suppos-
ing there were four children under 14, and
considering the way the allegedly insufficient
birth-rate is talked about there certainly
ought to be four! And there might easily,
without discredit, be six. That knocks out
the estimate as regards the board bill and
the size of the house at one blow. When
you make allowance for chronic bachelors
and spinsters, and childless couples, and
married people who die after the first child
is born, and children who die and leave the
home empty, the idea of taking two parents
and two children as a sort of average for
those homes which are really helping along
the population is absurd. It would mean a
steady perishing of the race. And apart
from the absurdly small size of the pro-
posed house and family there are the acci-
dents and incidents as already mentioned
The world is full of them. They are more
numerous thpii dust-boxes. And they
cost a lot of money. A margin must
be left for sickness and death and burial,
and periods of unemployment, and for old
age, and an occasional drink, and an occa-
sional trip, and some occasional amusement,
and Santa Claus once a year, and possibly
some contribution to the support of what
Benno, Henry Lawson’s dweller in Elder
Man’s Lane, called the Old ’Uns. Also some
men have a hobby which costs a little; it
ismt .everybody who can extract sufficient
amusement for his spare hours out of a
street corner and a post. For a family of
four boiler-metal people who were proof
against all hazards and possibilities, and
who lived in a boiler-metal world that never
varied or went wrong, 48s. a week would
doubtless be a living wage of a sort. Civili-
sation wouldn’t improve at the price. Yet
it might not die out. But in a shambling,
haphazard, bow-legged planet which is fiill
of chances a surplus is required. If the
housefather fell over a dog on the doorstep
on a dark night it might upset all Mr.
lleydon’s calculations for six' months. Let
his Honor multiply the number of house-
fathers bv the number of dogs, and the re-
sult by the number of doorsteps, and that
result by the number of dark nights, and
then, even after he has deducted the number
of places where the dog might be instead
of on the doorstep and the number of times
the house-father might not come out after
dark, the result will probably make him re-
consider his ‘‘living wage”—and also feel
ashamed of it.
BiLrA' B.” : This aviation business frazzles
me. I used to think that the flying man sat
on his machine, touched over the lever, ran
a , w , y a . r< f s along any old piece of reason-
ably level ground, and then hiked skyward.
But I was wrong. S. M. Herald has ex-
plained things. Observe:—
About a year ago Senator Pearce imported three
aeroplanes of the Bristol type. Thev were brought
to Sydney because it was honed to establish a Com-
monwealth flying ground in New South Wales. For
various reasons the rarity of the atmosphere was one
—the Government was advised not to select a site
m New South IVales, but to look for one in Victoria.
Eventually a site suitable for a flying - ground was
chosen in the vicinity of Altona Bay and Werribee.
A|id, of course; when brother Nogi is incon-
siderate enough to start walloping his war
drum up North, Australia will be at a devil
of a disadvantage by reason of the difficulty
in transporting the vicinity of Altona Bav
and Werribee to Darwin or thereabouts, and
the delay in gathering sufficient of the right
brand of atmosphere in its carpet-bag to
float the air scouts on arrival. I suppose
the end of it will be we will have to fall
back on a couple of black trackers and an
air-bloodhound to watch Nogi, or else send a
trained crow after him.
In a recent issue of The Billboard (published in
Cincinnati. U.S.) the editor announces his firm
conviction of the “saving grace of truth” in regard
to show criticism, and says The Billboard will
have it or drop anchor. Over the page his Australian
correspondent reports: “Gene Green and the Prim-
rose Four will so far have to be handed the docket
that they have been the two greatest successes that
the Rickards people have ever had from .the other
side of the world.”

“Diplomacy,” the modernised version of
Sardou’s “Dora,” is a snail-like society melo-
drama, with a plan of a British dockyard
instead of the usual corpse. The Julius
Knight Co., which produced the resurrected
play at Sydney Royal, last Saturday, does a
fair amount of justice to the good things that
are mostly not provided amidst scenery and
mountings that are really admirable, and the
audience (the patience of the Australian
audience is sublime or ridiculous, according
to the way you look at it) waits like a flock
of pastured sheep for the first big situation,
which happens at 9.45 precisely. On Satur-
day evening a man who had an appointment
at the painfully brief boxing match of the
week at the Stadium, and who had to leave
the theatre after the opening ten minutes,
got back at 9.40 and straightway understood
how far the play had moved without a word
of explanation. In the intervening hour and
a half, the ‘‘action'' had moved just about as
far as one could throw a tramcar by hand.
Even to accomplish that the diplomats of
the play have to be represented as next door
to half-witted —at least the younger of them
has to be that way all the time, and the elder
is a complete ass till the last act, when his
sense of smell suddenly becomes as acute as
a bloodhound’s. Then he follows the heavily
scented trail of the lurid Comtesse Zicka over
his documents in a way that would put the
best dog to shame and cause it to hold its
brow with one paw. As most people know, the
blessed plan is stolen from the younger dip-

AT SYDNEY
ROYAL.
Julius Knight dispenses with bloodhounds by
proving, with his keen nasal organ , that the
scented lurker (Irene Browne) is guilty of
larceny.
lomatist’s despatch box on his wedding day
by the jealous Zicka, and slipped by her
into a friendly note which Dora, the bride,
sends at her (Zicka’s) instigation to old
Baron Stein, the foreman-spy who wasn’t
invited to the diplomatic wedding and given
the run of the place. This is the cne notice-
able streak of sanity in the diplomatic pro-
ceedings. As hinted above, the elder diplo-
matist gets on the trail in the last act
through the thieving Zicka’s use of a pecu-
liar scent, and forces her to confess in a
more or less broken-up condition. The plan
having been recovered previously by a
straight-out demand on Baron Stein, there
isn’t as much sting in the end as there might
be,
Julius Knight, as Henry Beauclerc, the
elder diplomatist, is suave and reposeful.
Martin Lewis, as Julian 8., the worried
young diplomatist brother, looks the part
but appears to suffer from a chronic inabil-
ity to portray deep emotion. The same re-
mark applies to Miss Irene Brown (Com-
tesse Zicka), who droops and lingers very
artistically, but can’t squeeze a stage tear.
Hers is a hopelessly melodramatic part. She
stresses the fact by her sensational gowning
and her particular manner of lurking watch-
fully up-stage. But it really doesn’t matter
much, and she is highly decorative. Also,
when she lurks, her fine eyes are seen to the
best advantage. Leslie Victor, as Baron
Stein, is in a part where it is expected of
him to be villainously conventional, but he
gives it an artistic flick where possible.
Arthur Greenaway is too restrained as the
hunted and insulted and generally bumped
Orloff. Lizette Parkes, as the innocent
Dora, is delightfully fresh and dainty, but
a little overweighted by the tragedy of the
misunderstood bride. Emma Temple is an
alert, genial and very human Marquise de
Rio-/ares, who would disarm most creditors
without trouble. Eily Malyon gives a
sprightly imitation of a shrill, chattering
French maid as the delighted Australian
audience understands such characters. And
Mrs. Brough, as Lady Henry Fairfax, sup-
plies some sparkling comedy moments. The
absence of Lady Henry from the later scenes
is greatly to be deplored. It is a complete
and interesting production and above the
average as regards mounting.

“Officer 666” will resign from the force
stationed at Sydney Criterion this week, in
order to make room for another of these
slick American comedies, the name of which
does not really matter, since they are all the
same.
Hugh Buckler and his co. at the Little
Theatre, Sydney, still keep “Ilis House in
Order. ’ The mansion is not capsized or in-
verted, nor is the tenement torn in two pieces
and tottering at an angle of 45 degrees. The
fly that walks upside down on the ceiling
finds the ceiling in its usual place, and the
spider on the wall isn’t disturbed by any
upheavals. The play embodies a powerful
warning to every man’s second wife. She
should have it: specified in the marriage set-
tlement that the first wife, being dead, must
stay dead; that being buried she must re-
main buried; that her alleged perfections
must not *be thrown up at her successor;
and that her relations must not pervade
the house and eat the victuals and say what
a pity it is that the tidy and immaculate
predecessor is gone. In this respect Henry
VIII. was a wise bigamist. When he exe-
cuted a wife he chased all her family connec-
tions right across the horizon, and started
afresh.
After an extra blow-out on Tuesday in
aid of the Actors’ Benevolent Fund, the
White City of Sydney has settled down to
its regular habits, or as many regular habits
as it has acquired in the way of providing
diversion. Calvert continues to walk across
a cobweb in the stars or thereabouts, and
the hundred' side-shows not weary in
well doing.
Since The Bulletin remarked last time
it blusliingly appeared on the Block that the
programme at the National Amphitheatre
(Sydney) deserved the support of amuse-
ment-seekers nothing has occurred to in-
culcate repentance in its mind. The pro-
gramme is sprinkled with strong turns, and
the weak turns afford an admirable oppor-
tunity to go out and subscribe to the fund
for the relief of the famine in Japan.
As threatened last week, William Ander-
son’s American Burlesque Company at the
Palace revived “The Speculators,” and Syd-
ney crowded as much of itself into the build-
ing as possible. The rest of it stayed out-
side and uttered execrations re its exclusion.
The show is a sort of twin or triple
brother to “The Grafters” and “A Day at
the Races.” It is light and airy, and there
are dances and persiflage in it. A certain
tendency on the part of somebody to kiss
somebody else by the light of the moon is
manifest in places. Any individual of robust
intelligence may observe traces of a Bowery
or Blink.v Bill element, without which no
play dealing with New York or thereabouts
is complete. To-morrow week will be the last
appearance of a co. that has made a lot of
friends, and if it has made any enemies
they haven’t been identified at this office.
Australian-born Hawker, one of the flying
men of the world, will make his first public
flight above Sydney on Saturday from Rand-
wick racecourse at three o’clock, and manager
M. J. Bloomfield guarantees that, bar acci-
dents, the time will be adhered to. Hawker
is a mere youngster of 22. and his principal
vice, from a show point of view, is a hatred
of publicity. He has never “sat” for a
photograph, such photos of him as are in
existence having been “snapped” by news-
paper men, whom he loves with a blown-in-
the-bottle resentment. Another cherished
detestation is the person who offers a
weighty bank-note attached to an advertise-
ment to be pasted or painted on his ’plane.
On a recent occasion, when his manager
lovingly rolled the bank-note before him,
Hawker is alleged to have foamed at the pro-
peller. Likewise, he is chary about the
species of passengers he carries, and will
onlv make appointments after an interview
with the prospective cargo. The reason is
that the cargo is stowed beside him, not be-
hind, where it could fall out comfortably,
and if it has a tendency to become excited
or infuriated it may take charge of the
bridge, to the daiager and great annoy-
ance of the pilot, xvt time of writing about
40 aspiring birds had made application to
chance Hawker’s critical eye. After Sydney,
he will probably make flights at Newcastle
and Bathurst.
“ THE WEARING OF THE
GREEN," AT MEL-
BOURNE PRINCESS'S.
Finnegan , the doorkeeper. Fitzgerald , the Squire.
The Sydney Adelphi got out of its panto-
mime stockings on Saturday, scraped the
rust and cobwebs off its gun, and “came
back,” more or less successfully, with “The
Pride of the Prairie.” One needn’t have put
the X-rays on the title to guess that the
bellowdrama would be full of Indians and
cowboys, witli a half-breed to cause trouble,
most likely. The lirst scene is full of promise.
Great Storm, an Indian chief, upset at his
daughter keeping company with a half-
Spanisli rancher, threatens her with deatli
so successfully that she loses the power of
speech. Then, instead of trying his luck on
the rest of the co., he inconsiderately stops
short —apparently waiting for this paper to
offer him a bonus of 7s. (id. h head to treat
several others in the same way. After that
disappointment “The Tripe of the Prairie”
takes the line of least resistance, and, pro-
ceeding through numerous plots and counter-
plots, captures, escapes and murders, winds
up with the same old promised marriage,
while a funeral or two get ready in the
background. The scenery is, as usual, very
good, particularly in some of the Indian
scenes, in one of which, for the first time,
Adelphi patrons have the pleasure of seeing
a suspected Indian lynched. Cyril Mackay,
as Dick the Britisher, succeeds in being
exactly the same hero as he has been several
hundred times before; and Miss Gertrude
Boswell, a new arrival, makes a very good
impression as Clattering Kate, the pride of
the prairie. Miss Muriel Dale (the wronged
female), Max Clifton (Great Storm, the
Indian), and Vivian Edwards (Carlos
Durango, the half-bred scoundrel), all re-
ceive honorable mention. As for most of the
others, on second thoughts the bonus will be
increased to 10s. 6‘d.
But for the reappearance of Lily Eyton,
a comedienne who called for a certain amount
of praise when she was here before, and
artist Eric Stone, who didn’t call for so
much, the Sydney Tivoli programme 'is
much the same as it was last week. That
is, the most interesting items are supplied bv
the Two Bobs, the Tom Davis Trio of
motor-cyclists, Waterbury Bros, and Tenney,
baritone Talleur Andrews, dancers Vivien
Talleur and Margaret Klos, and Salma
Braatz.
The best of this week’s moving pictures in
Svdney:—
Crystal Palace: “Her Majesty the Little Queen.”
wherein a queenlet refuses to sign certain death-
warrants because her tutor’s name is on the black-
list. Understood to be a dark hint at Alice Wilkins
in this somewhere.
Lyric : “The Parasites,” concerning the financial
affairs of a pair of blackmailers and the domestic
worries of the society gentleman to whom they are
looking for dividends. Lady partner makes a death-
bunk confession, and the co. goes bung.
Colonial : “Within the Enemy’s Lines,” yet
another Yankee Civil War film, teeming with all the
horrors of a moving-picture campaign.
Empress: “The Pitfall,” dealing with the dangers
that await the innocent geurl who goes on the stage,
especially when the stage-manager isn’t otherwise
engaged.
West’s Glaciarium and Olympia : For the rest
of the week, “Honesty.” Next week, Asta Neilsen,
who has been recovering from a hunger-strike, will
reappear in “Suffragette.” Police arrangements are
in the hands of the Inspector-General.
Spencer’s Lyceum : Last nights of “A Message
from Mars.” On Friday night, picture of the
America-N.S.W. athletics, with a lecture by manager
Peixotto.
Imperial : “The Trail of the Hanging Rock,” a
Canadian story of derring-do or something.
The revue is nearing the three months
mark at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, and the
populace still crowds to it with something
that is either enthusiasm or a pretence there-
at which constitutes dashed good acting.

The foregoing ample statement of the
plot of “Joseph and His Brethren” will ex-
plain to everybody not wholly unacquainted
with Holy Writ that the large and pic-
turesque drama at Melb. Royal is less Bibli-
cal than sensational. It takes many liberties
with the only authorised history of Joseph,
especially as regards Mrs. Potiphar, who sins
with all the enthusiasm of the worst woman
in London. The prominence given to Mrs.
P. by the dramatist throws a heavy respon-
sibility upon Miss Ethel Warwick. Miss
Warwick, speaking frankly, has neither a
musical voice nor a forceful personality.
Her appearance is the only really striking
feature of her performance. The Joseph
(Malcolm Tearle) is pleasing but not
powerful, whilst some of the other people—•
newly imported or otherwise —do nothing
that lives in one’s recollection through a
play of four acts and 13 scenes, with con-
siderable pauses between scenes. Satur-
day’s performance, doubtless, will be bet-
tered during this week, not to mention the
weeks thereafter. First impressions were
most favorable to Edward O’Neill as Simeon
(an excellent actor in each of his scenes),
Frank Harvey as Potiphar, Henry Hallam
as Judah, with one long, effective speech to
deliver, and George Bryant as elder brother
Reuben, he being about the only member of
the co., bar Simeon, who looks his part very
impressively, and carries a Biblical atmo-
sphere about with him. The scenery is good,
but not startlingly beautiful, and the final
tableau would be quite brilliant in its way
if Father Jacob produced a solemn effect
on the beholder. Perhaps The Bulletin’s
beholder expected too much of the inspired
founder of the Cohen family.
This is the ninth week of the panto, at
Her Majesty’s (Melbourne), where Mr. and
*?Mrs. Baba will continue to appropriate
property with impunity until further
notice. Also it is the same week of the
“Land of Nod” at the King’s, where a
change of bill is impending. The Bert
Bailey combination takes the floor at the
King’s on Saturday (21st), with “The
Ninety and Nine.” Melbourne has heard
satisfactory accounts of this drama from
Sydney, and will accept the bush-fire scene
as a seasonable attraction. On Tuesday
next week C. R. Stanford is to have the
use of the King’s for a matinee benefit per-
■ formance, the details whereof will be duly
announced.
“THE WEARING OF THE GREEN,” AT
MELBOURNE PRINCESS'S.
A snap from the audience.
At Melbourne Rrincess’s for the past
10 nights artless young Allen Doone, in ver-
dure clad, has been playing “The Wearing of
the Green,” and breathing a fond message
to the Irish in a voice much impaired by a
recent cold. Probably due to this affliction
he reduces his singing to a minimum, and on
one or two evenings has preferred to patter
rather than to warble the sintiments that
bring large lumps into pathriotic throats.
Miss Edna Keeley, who is alleged to have
been indisposed by day but sufficiently re-
covered by night to portray the usual colleen,
has rendered useful assistance in the good
work; and a company, including Messrs.
Buckley and Cullenane and Miss Ethel Grist,
has dutifully pretended to be torn by
emotion. An improved Doone and a con-
valescent Miss Keeley are expected to attract
the coin of the faithful in “The Rebel” to be
produced next Saturday night. In this
play, which is the sole novelty in Doone’s
job lot of dramas, James B. Fagan presents
his views on the trying times of ’9B.
The present Melbourne Opera House bill
is embellished by the talent of Josephine
Davis, who employs a nice voice and an un-
pretentious manner on some of Irving
Berlin’s song successes. All of her ditties
she renders acceptably enough, but achieves
the best results with “In My Harem,” a
cute song of Yiddish character wherein she
is probably most herself. The Richards and
Pasquali brethren earn full approval for
their gymnastic doings, but Barton and Ash-
ley’s new turn seems too protracted and
piffling for Opera House consumption. Also
present are the clever Romanos with the
beauteous muscular appointments; the talka-
tive Du Calion, whose turn might be called
“The Tower of Babel”; and one James
Eraser, who is charged with the deplorable
distinction of being “Australia’s champion
ragtime singer.”
** * *
The Dandies continue to improve on ac-
quaintance in their specially constructed
residence on the St. Ivilda (Vic.) Esplanade.
Claude Dampier gives songs and character
sketches with a humor that combines the
robust effects of a knockabout with some of
the graces of a light comedian. Gavin Wood,
baritone, is pleasing; Oliver Peacock, the
buxom tenor, sets flappers demanding his pic-
ture postcard ; and the three charming ladies
seem to be worthy of their exceptional
salaries. Some people also find George Mor-
gan diverting, though there are probably
many things in life to be preferred to
George’s little pleasantries and curate-like
cavortings. This critic would sooner have a
glass of' milk.
Chief among the attractions at Melbourne
Olympic on Saturday was “A Prisoner in the
Ilarem, ’ a strip of Indian bellowdram'a con-
taining a bad old Sultan and a man-eating
tiger. These animals collaborate to make
life exciting for the sweet young heroine.
“Two Aristocratic Penitents” and “A Hus-
bands Awakening” which also started, were
both dull sermons for giddy husbands, and
declared that female worship, like Charity,
should begin at home. Next Saturday
Spencer is starring “The Story of a Siren,”
a pretty Pathe drama.
Prom a Bulletin man’s point of view:—
If Australia possesses a theatre suitable-for Tango
lea parties, it is not in Melbourne. The first Tango
attraction at the Opera House drew a crowd whose
curiosity, in most cases, was fully satisfied by this
sample of the entertainment provided. The air of
social gathering was lacking. In the stalls and d.c.,
where refreshments are provided as part of the
entertainment, the wedged-in condition of the audi-
ence gives no scope for comfortable “discussion” of
the tea and cake, or spoonful of iced material, that
comes along. Therefore the refreshments count for
practically nothing on the bill of fare, and the
Tangio display on the stage is a matter for cold-
blooded criticism. Two or three selected vaudeville
turns are sandwiched between demonstrations of
Tango and Turkey Trot by a pair of capable (but
unremarkable) young practitioners and some slow,
unstately processions of girls who are deputed to
exhibit gowns, hats and undergarments. The dancing
is educational (as the Le Brun turn was) to any
earnest student of Tango steps and Turkey trots;
also, it is interesting on account of its claim to be
beyond Wowserian reproach. George Salinger cheer-
fully informs the audience that he is about to show
how groundless are the charges of impropriety brought
against Turkey trotting in ordinary. And then he
and the 3’oung, lady proceed, in a respectable and
matter-of-fact manner, to justify all that Wowser
has ever said concerning the possibilities of the
steps. The Tango, danced by experts, may be passed
at the Customs without demur ; but the artists who
can persuade a reasonable audience that a full-fledged
Turkey Trot is nothing for anybody to snort at have
not yet revealed their purpose in Australia. So much
for the dancing. As to the processions of girl in
dress and undress, the only objection is their long-
drawn-out ungracefulness. The ladies in prehistoric
costumes are obviously ill at ease. They haven’t
learned the business of making stage exhibits of
themselves in easy, show-room style. The girls who
serve as advts. for somebody’s corsets are as pleasing
as they ought to be under the circumstances ; but the
damsels doomed to march along the gangway in
drooping bloomers seem conscious of their uncalled-
for behavior. The bloomers are not at all shocking.
They, are merely saddening. No man can rejoice in
the thought that the woman he loves is required by
fashion to be arrayed like one of these. The long,
drooping, saggy bloomers of Tango Tea ladies drive
thoughts of love clean out of his head. He goes
away in a monastic frame of mind.
Adelaide shows are mostly dominated by
the weather. The Dandies are outdoors,
with the exhibition to fall back on in case of
rain. Wondergraph keeps on its North
Terrace open-air stand, as well as its big
new home. The Tivoli shows the quaint
Ferry Corwey, with the Schmettans, Bellora,
the Rexos, and the rest, down at the Oval,
but on Saturday nights opens the Grote-
street house as well. West’s, on the other
hand, are indoors with Forbes-Robertson’s
filmed Hamlet, to the Ophelia of Gertrude
Elliott. And the King’s manages (o
keep cool enough with the “maniac” bull-
dogs, the gymnastic Benos, the reckless
“Juno Salmo,” and other strong Brennan-
Fuller turns.
“Harrison O.”: The chief effect of a first-night in-
spection of “Joseph and His Brethren” is to immensely
increase one’s respect for the late Moses, or whoever
it was, who originally told the story of how Joe
the Nark made himself unpopular, first with his
brothers, and, later, with Mrs. Potiphar. He put his
tale in a few pithy chapters. Louis Parker unfolds
his narrative in 13 scenes, with the aid of 45 named
characters and a host of supers; so that the scenery
synopsis looks like a slab of the “World’s Gazetteer”
and the cast of characters like a page of the “Post
Office Directory.” Nearly every incident is hedged
round with superfluous dialogue, and incidents that
have already been witnessed by those in the audi-
torium are narrated by some of those on the stage,
for the benefit of other characters on the stage who
happened to be away when the thing occurred. For
a comparatively interesting, reasonably sub-edited
yarn, give me Genesis every time.
From London: Beerbohm Tree has closed the run
of “Joseph and His Brethren” at His Majesty’s after
the 150th performance. “Many of the leading lights
of the Church have given it their blessing,” he says;
yet he has no hope that there will be much of a
crop from the Biblical ground now opened up—the
Lord Chamberlain stands in the way. Anyhow,
Tree switches off on to a revival of an old success,
“The Darling of the Gods,” with Marie Lohr as
Yo San. “She has revealed at rehearsals,” says Tree,
“a depth of intensity of tragic feeling for which I
was hardly prepared.”
JOSEPH AND HIS BRETHREN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dreamer Joseph, son of Rachel, wore a
many colored coat;
Which his father placed upon him his im-
portance to denote,
Whereat the sons of Israel expressed their
deep concern,
For Joseph was a younger son with brether-
ing to burn.
Do! Six of them were Leah’s lot and Zilpah
claimed two more,
And brothers Dan and Naphtali were two
that Bilhah bore;
Of all the sons of Israel was Joe the young-
est Yid,
Save Rachel’s last and final —little Ben-
jamin, the Kid.
On a sheep-run near to Dothan, lo ! it fate-
fully befell
That Joseph’s jealous brothers dropped the
Dreamer down a well;
And the dried-up well of Dothan might have
proved his silent grave
Had not giddy minx Zuleika chanced to buy
him for a slave.
Ah ! the guilt of this Zuleika has been trum-
peted afar.
She is mentioned in the Scriptures as the
wife of Potiphar,
And any blushful Wowser for the very words
can vouch
Which she used to say to Joseph whilst re-
clining on a couch.
In the play she looks upon him and adores
the goodly youth ;
She chews his chilly shoulder and his chaste
unwilling mouth;
Then, having failed to tempt him to a co-
respondent’s fault,
She weeps, and charges Joseph with a
criminal assault.
They cast him into prison where the
Pharaoh’s servants lie,
And he tells his fellow captives what will
happen by-and-bye ;
But Zuleika still pursues him, and the audi-
ence is warned
That ll—l contains no fury like a she-
Fgyptian scorned.
Two famous eerie visions came to Pharaoh
in the night,
And his staff of fortune-tellers failed to
read his dreams aright;
So the monarch sent for Joseph, who was
quick to understand
That his Nibs must make provision for a
famine in the land.
And the King spake unto Joseph: “Prithee,
run the blessfed show
In the wisdom of thy purpose through the
years of weal and woe;
And whatsoe’er thou doest shall the law of
Egypt be,
For I set you up as ruler of the country—
after Me.”
Ripe corn was stored by Joseph whilst the
seasons yet were good,
Till the land waxed dry and dusty. Then he
doled the masses food.
And the squattahs pawned their acres, when
their “stuff” and stock were spent,
lo the good and wily Hebrew who controlled
the Government.
Hats off to Joe the Dreamer, who foresaw’
the rainless day,
And _ squeezed the drought-struck squattahs
in a crude Egyptian way ;
Who loved the State and labored to preserve
the public health !
M hen cometh such a Joseph to our careless
Commonwealth?
To purchase corn in Egypt came his brether-
ing with gold;
Yet they knew not gentle Joseph for the
brother they had sold.
1 hough he bade them bring him Benjamin.
and schemed some little schemes,
lhat all mieht come to happen in accordance
with his dreams.
Zuleika still pursued him in her fond un-
dying hate,
But Potiphar, the fathead, grew suspicious
of his mate;
He listened round the corner and he took
her by surprise
And he told his faithful minions to remove
the lady’s eyes.
Lo! All the sons of Israel to Joseph’s house
were brought,
And when they knew their brother they be-
haved as Jews distraught.
Reub, Simeon, Gad, Levi, Judah, Naphtali
and Dan,
Asher, Isaachar and Zebulun went dotty, to
a man.
And all the folk of Goshen rolled up,
friendly like, and cheered
When Jacob, alias Israel, in sudden nomp
appeared
Perched high upon a platform—like a pro-
phet out for larks,
Or a limelit personator of the recent Henry
Parkes.
VICTORIA.
O.K.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Private Boxer”: Died the other week in N’York
Wm. F. Hawtrey, familiarly known as “Bill.” He will
be remembered in Australia for many good character-
comedy parts. Glabrio, in “The Sign of the Cross,”
was the first, and probably the best, seen here. His
Tramp, too, in “A Message from Mars,” would take
a lot of beating. He made a lot of money in his
time—for bookies and poker-schools. His only sur-
viving brother is the popular Charles, who made a
fortune out of “The Private Secretary,” and lost it
in the family manner. He is at present playing the
part Niblo is to have in “Never Say Die.” “Never
Say Die” would very well stand for the motto of the
Hawtreys ; but no family is allowed to have the last
word in that matter. Bright’s disease was the thing
that concluded the argument in Bill’s case.

Albert (Waxman) Whelan, the Australian “enter-
tainer,” gets spoken to by London Sketch
(17/12/’l3) for his lack of variety in staging his
turn at the Palace. Back after an absence of some
years, he still “enters in dress-clothes, and divests
himself of his overcoat, hat and gloves, and whistles
a well-known air before getting to business. The
business consists of two topical songs, several good
stories, a French song with whistling refrain, and
an imitation of Chirgwin. At the end he sits down
to the piano just as the curtain falls, once again
whistling the old familiar tune.” As “the whole
thing was extremely well done, and went as well as
could possibly be hoped,” and as “the crowded
house was completely satisfied, and asked for nothing
more,” Whelan may possibly think that his way of
finishing a turn is still good enough.
Marcus, so long manager of Adelaide Tivoli for
Rickards and then for Mclntosh, has given up theat-
rical work to run as an electricity expert. His
first big work was an advertising sign of great size
and brilliance, and the Adelaide papers, in memory
of old times, gave it a glowing notice. It is “not
ingenious,” the Register said incidentally—and left
Marcus guessing whether “most ingenious” was in-
tended.
THE MUSICAL MAGSMAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Slapoffski, the musical conductor, in an interview,
said that Australia and Maoriland, like other English
communities, were the prey of musical humbugs and
charlatans. “The best means of combating them,” he
declared, “is the establishment of municipal or
national conservatoriums for the purpose of cultivat-
ing the public taste. The publicity given by munici-
pal enterprises will kill* impostures.”
- —News from Wellington (il/.L.).
The musical magsman arrived in the South,
And honey and spices flowed out of his mouth ;
He came with a rote to a critic or two,
Who gaped and admired, as a critic should do ;
Oh, great was the glory and fine was the fun
When the public was doped by the musical gun !
He played a concerto and something in F ;
The “notice” was splendid—the critic was deaf;
He wagged the great mane on his leonine head —
“How perfectly lovely!” the pretty girls said;
And signing his name in their autograph book
Meant one-guinea seats for the musical crook.
He sang them a cycle of polyglot verse—
Hungarian, Finnish, Mongolian and Erse;
The praise in the press was unstintedly hot.
And nobody ventured to mutter, “Damn rot!”
The concert-promoter said, “Lord, what a swag!”
And fawned at the feet of the musical mag.
He said to himself in his moments of fright:
“Mein Gott, I haf vork for a guinea a nighdt!
I haf pawn my tress-goad in der days dot vos past,.
And dis is too himmlizeh —how long gan it last ?
Dose embryo beoples shoost grawling mit oof,
How long gan they shtant for der musical shpoof?”
The musical inag in his homeland will yearn
For the season when southward in joy he’ll return;
He’ll sigh, in his job at a fiver a week,
For the public that called him “supreme” and
“unique” ;
“I got to ged pack to id, von of dose days,
Vere der shtnff dot vos schlenter is music vot pays.”

SOCIETY
“Full Astern” reports a new danger:—
The N.S.W. Navigation Department’s new regula-
tions about life-jackets and lifebuoys on ferry-
steainers in Port Jackson got an unexpected adver-
tisement one night last week when the Balmain
steamer Lady Rawson, on a moonlight excursion,
bumped into a motor-boat. The engine of the launch
broke down when off Dawes Point, and the craft,
without lights, drifted round the busiest corner in
the harbor. The ferry steamer did the rest, and
the launch sank like a petrified elephant. Five men
jumped into the water, and the excited passengers
on the picnic boat began to throw lifebuoys and life-
jackets after them. A collier, ambling along at this
juncture, also cast buoys and belts into the water
by the armful. Then the passing ferry Kirrule
heard that 10 men, a herd- of women, five orphan
children, a bald baby, a defaulting cashier and a
bigamist were struggling in the water and began
energetically to empty whole stacks of cork life-
savers upon the troubled sea. Meanwhile two
athletic heroes on the Rawson plunged into the water.
When they found their man he was swimming franti-
cally in an endeavor to escape being overwhelmed by
the quantity of floating things which threatened to
submerge him. Finally the swimmers got clear of
the jackets, and reached the Kirrule, where the
rescued person angrily demanded the names and
addresses of the people who had pelted him with life
obstacles. Next day irascible mariners went poking
round Port Jackson looking for their missing
jackets, most of which had dodged past Bradley’s
towards the open sea.
Richard Butler, S. Aus. Minister for
Works, after his run round the water-ways
of the Eastern States :
The control of the Melbourne and Sydney Harbor
Boards is confined to those two harbors respectively,
the trade being largely centralised in those two
capitals. In South Aus. the Government believes
strongly in decentralisation, trade wherever possible
being encouraged to flow to its natural outlet, and
the control of our Board’ shortly to be appointed
will extend from the west coast and Port Augusta
to Port Mac Donnell on the sount coast.
Was there ever a Government that wasn't
in favor of decentralisation? The only real
difference between S. Aus. and most other
States is that the Broken Hill mines, not
being interested in boosting Adelaide,
poured their traffic into Port Pirie, and gave
it a sizeable trade; while the Wallaroo and
Moonta mines, being right on the coast of
Spencer’s Gulf, necessarily created another
seaport there. The oversea trade of those
two ports and of Adelaide represented
£15,100,000 of the State’s total of
£16,600,000 for 1912, giving a poor little
£1,500,000 for all the others. The richest
tract of country in the whole State is in
the south-east; but so persistently has traffic
been dragged from there to Adelaide that
there isn’t a liarbqr thereabouts that would
keep one man and a hand-truck employed.

There is a point of humorous interest in
the story recently told to the Inter-State
Commission in Melbourne anent the indigna-
tion of a lady when she discovered that her
favorite brand of “imported” footwear was
really the work of Australian hands. The
lady it appears is one of the big personages
among our Idle Classes, and what seasons
the joke for low plebeian consumption is the
fact that her husband is a very high fune-
tionarv who, after the fowl has been dis-
membered and the champagne laid to rest,
often preaches the great gospel of support
to Australian industries.
Two witnesses of the execution of Ned
Kelly passed out last week. Jim Middleton,
late of Melbourne Herald, was the Age
representative at the hanging of Ned in
1880. Ex-sergeant Arthur Steele, who died
at Wangaratta (Vic.), was the actual cap-
turer of the bushranger aforesaid. He
came of fighting stock, for his father was a
captain in the Ist Inniskilling Dragoons,
and five of his brothers died on battlefields.
Middleton came of writing stock, for his
father was an Age man before him. Although
the pen is mightier than the sword. Middle-
ton was eight years younger than the old
policeman when they both went the way of
the most notorious person they ever met.
If they haven’t already found him, they are
probably singing, “Has anybody here seen
Kelly?”

An obelisk, carrying on its four faces
matter relative to the discovery and settle-
ment of Portland (Vic.) in the year 1834,
■is presently to be erected on the northern
foreshore of Portland Bay, and amidst much
cackle a “foundation stone” will be laid on
April 18. On the same occasion there is
to be the cracking of a bottle or two to sig-
nify the joy of the inhabitants over the
recent discovery of their anchorage by the
State OoA r ernment. Harbor improvements
have at length been arranged for, with an
inward reservation that, in order to pre-
serve the Melbourne monopoly, the differ-
ential railway rates and other'swindles will
kill them dead. The fathers of the
hamlet hope to borrow a warboat for the
festivity, and. already the school-children of
the district are rehearsing a variety of patri-
otic warbles. But a straight line of rail-
way to. connect Portland with the country
for which it is the natural outlet, and an
honest attempt to give the great harbor
a show, would be better than all the warbles
and obelisks between Marathon and Water-
loo.
The poor old Australian language had
another shock last week when letters began
to appear in the Sydney papers about “the
Hospitalisation of Infants”’
With due decorum the Melbourne
Athenaeum (the library, not the club) this
month celebrates its 75th birthday. The
place was started as the Melbourne
Mechanics’ Institute in 1839, and Captain
Lonsdale, Superintendent for Victoria
(then part of the Ma State), was its first
president. The institute, an old brick
building, was described in the defunct Daily
Telegraph as “the noblest edifice in the
province”; but its nobility was sacrificed
in 1872 and the present hall (which is not
exactly palatial) was put up. At the same
time the name was changed to the Mel-
bourne Athenaeum. The idea of the library
was Redmond Barry’s; he started it by
allowing members the use of his collection
of books. Several valuable historical works
are included in the present collection. Gar-
net Welch was secretary of the Athenaeum,
1872-9, and Marcus Clarke also held the job
for a brief period ; while the present sec. is
“Bung” Wilmot —“Old Boy” of the sporting

columns of the Argus. The Athenaeum is a
refuge for parsons and retired oldsters, who
find the soporific atmosphere of the reading-
room much to their liking. The place has
always been so dreadfully respectable that
there was almost a riot among the most
ancient members when the committee lately
so far forgot itself as to build a smoking-
room; and there is a feeling in some quar-
ters that the management has fallen into
the hands of revolutionaries or pro-Boers.
A gallery for art exhibitions was added to
the building in 1911, and is still the best
room of its kind in Melbourne.

The remarkably ignorant Sydney Tele-
graph, which persists in describing James
Cook as the discoverer of Australia and ar-
guing that because Smith isn’t so helplessly
in debt as Brown he is perfectly solvent,
couldn’t be expected to do anything but fall
over a statistical obstacle no matter how
absurd it appeared. Thus, when a cable-
gram reached it from the medical congress
at Auckland (M.L.) last week that an
authority had declared .4 per cent, of Aus-
tralian children to be feeble-minded and 1.2
so mentally dull as to require special train-
ing, it burst upon a staggered community
with the figures minus the decimal points.
Probably the cabled message did not mention
decimals, the despatcher reckoning that even
the Telegraph would know that four per
cent, and 12 per cent, respectively were
first cousins to a lunatic asylum. But the
dull old lady just ran it in that way and
when Minister for Education Carmichael
arose and scoffed at the figures she shook
her gamp at him and told him that “no good
purpose was to be served by extravagant
denunciations... .For Mr. Carmichael to in-
troduce so much vehemence shows that he
has got the subject altogether out of per-
spective.” There’s superiority for you!
A complaint was raised last week that the
onetime Henry Parkes of N.S.W. isn’t suffi-
ciently remembered, and that his grave isn’t
kept green. “Is Henry Parkes forgotten?”
shouted a strong daily paper correspondent
in his agony. Well, to tell the solemn truth,
even though it may be dragged from the com-
munity with difficulty, the ancient poser and
opportunist is just about forgotten. So are
his deeds of statesmanship, for when he left
the stage, people who had been hypnotised
by his chest and shoulders and his thunder-
ous upheaval of hair realised that, after all,
he did very little, though he said very much.
liis Kiama ghost is forgotten. His remark-
able land Speculations are forgotten. His
poems are forgotten, even the one about
how “On the Rocky Mountains’ height,
10,000 feet above the sea, love, my Stella
kissed me in the night, and none were there
but you and me, love” ; and the other which
declared, “I would not give my Ragged Jane
for all the peacock ladies going; she’s the one
to soothe your pain ; what she don’t know is
not. worth knowing.” They are almost as
perished as that ode of his political successor
which declared :
The advantages of co-operation were fully understood
By that best of all princes, Albert the Good.
And Parkes’s “Fifty Tears in the Making of
Australian History” had hardly been on the
market three months before it had slid down
into the cheap remnant basket in the second-
hand emporiums. This world may owe every
man board and lodging while he is alive, but
it doesn’t necessarily owe him a memory and
tribute and garland when he is defunct. In
the grave there is free competition, and the
fittest ghost survives, and the law of de-
mand and supply runs the show. Of course
the fittest ghost that survives is sometimes
the best advertised ghost—not the most de-
serving one. But if a ghost which shouted
on the top rung of the ladder of advertisement
during a whole generation drops promptly
out of sight in the tomb the inference is
that he was not great, but only resembled
greatness.
* * * *
Agar Wynne’s stepson, Charles Dudgeon,
has no luck with step-pa’s motor-car. Some
time ago, during repairs to the bridge which
carries Chapel-street over Melbourne’s larg-
est drain, Dudgeon and the car somehow
got through the contractor’s guard-rail, and
landed on the bottom, with all parties some-
what broken up, including the motor-car.
Now Agar Wynne’s vehicle has hurled the
young man and itself into an electric tram-
car, smashing the conductor’s right leg. If
Dudgeon is ever again permitted to sit in a
motor-car in Melbourne, it will be more
than his luck with such vehicles appears to
warrant.
LOVE BELOW GROUND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
St. Hilda Council, having lit up all the upper
beach, and allowed the building of a score of roaring
shows that throw a fierce, white light over two-thirds
of the foreshore, has now decided to effectively light
the last remaining few acres of sands within its
jurisdiction. The Aon advises other seaside munici-
palities to carry* on the good work.
We picture Cupid with a torch,
Which to the poet’s soul conveys,
Not that sweet Love is meant to scorch,
But that the tender, rosy rays
That from his tiny band proceed
Give all the light that lovers need.
But councillors are dull old scamps,
And they no longer understand.
They turn their incandescent lamps
To light the long, soft, kindly sand ;
And cursing on the paths above,
The lovers find no place for Love.
Grim Science, cold and heartless, too,
Will light the whole world’s surface soon,
And night, so long to lovers true,
Will glare in angry glow of noon.
Then burrowing, to pay its toll,
Poor Love must emulate the mole.
victoria.
EDDYSON.

Once again the law (by the mouth of
Judge Wasley, in the Vic. County Court)
has put in a word for the missus, and maybe
the kids. Percy Mitchell’s creditors had
seized the family furniture. Thereupon
Mary Ellen, wife unto Percy, claimed that
she had bought the furniture with savings
screwed out of the housekeeping money, and
therefore it was hers. Judge Wasley slapped
her kindly on the back and told her to keep
the furniture. After reading this case the
average citizen would hand over all his
screw to his wife, if it weren’t so d—d hard
to borrow back a couple of shillings when
wanted. Some wives are flintier than judg-
ment creditors.
‘Cook intervenes,” shouted one Sydney
morning paper after the harassed little man
had come back from more or less settling
the strike for reasonably pure water at the
attempted Federal Capital of Canbilly. “In-
tervenes,” one of the most dignified words
in the language, suggests some overpowering
personality coming between two smaller
combatants, who are ready to fling them-
selves at each other’s throats over some
question involving the national honor. And
this was merely a small, worried, bald,
spectacled politician with a precarious hold
on his job persuading a few thirsty and re-
bellious workers not to mind the taste of
dead rabbit in the water. That isn’t inter-
vening ; or, if it is, a suit of old clo’ is a
cathedral.
NOT QUITE EXHAUSTED.
HE: "You are tired; shall we sit out our
dance and talk?”
SHE: “ Oh! I'm not so tired as that."
It used to he decayed and obsolete war-
ships—now it’s little tin gods on wheels.
Said that Munro-Ferguson, successor of G.-
G. Denman, is being shunted to Australia
by the Asquith Government because he is an
obstinate creature who might make trouble
over Lloyd-George’s new land legislation.
Well, if the figurehead for the time being is
chosen on this simple, shabby plan, White
Australia must he regarded in England as a
sane, easily-run place where Capital is still
pretty safe and a little, inexperienced man
can’t go very far wrong. Glance down the
lists of governing jobs.: Egypt (Kitchener) ;
India (Hardinge, later on perhaps Kitch-
ener) ; Canada (Duke of Connaught) ;
South Africa —very tough just now (Glad-
stone retiring, Buxton just nominated as
successor) ; Australia —(What is the fel-
low’s name again—Mo-Ferg or something
like that?).
Each Victorian municipality has its
officer of health —several of him in some
cases, where the territory is big. The anti-
quated Health Act requires him to furnish
the Board of Health, through the council,
with an annual report. Usually it is a very
mechanical and insipid article (the better
he knows his business the more insipid), and
mostly is a dull and dreary repetition of it-
self. Should he have a keen scent and a
fluent pen, he will most assuredly reap his
reward; for. his immediate boss is the coun-
cil, and although it is a one-mouse concern
in other respects it has the power of remov-
ing him, and promptly uses it if he
makes himself obnoxious. But he’s
generally a canny medico, who rea-
lises how a fool and his perfunctorily-
earned guinea are soon parted, so he
just pursues the line of least resistance,
lets things slide, and hangs on to the nice
little sinecure, with its allied benefits, till re-
moved by natural causes. Dr. Ley, however,
recently threw discretion to the polluted
winds of Rodney, where the irrigation scan-
dals came from, and gave the local council a
candid account of his stewardship. For
example:—
Some time ago he was asked to report on slaughter-
yards, in conjunction with the inspector of nuisances.
They did so, and condemned one of the yards as a
disgrace to the town. He did not condemn the other
one, which was almost equally bad. The council was
not honest with him. He found the council only
made a tool of him to get the yard he condemned
shifted “so that one of their number could build his
house close to the site and inhale the sweet breezes
that blow over the lake that has been purified by
some 30 years’ collection of pig manure.” They
shifted the slaughter-yard in question to where
“three-fourths of all the breezes that blow take the
smell almost, into the' very heart of the place.”
As the Board of Health remarked, the re-
port showed a “great want of tact”; and
“tact” being ever so much more important
than truth, it was decided that the report be
not received, and that the services of the
“tactless” doctor be dispensed with.
The latest “permanent and repro-
ductive” work to mop up the loan
money of Uncle Abrahambenjamin-
moses in Maoriland is a new track to
Milford Sound. The present path, 31
miles long, which is alleged to be the
finest walk in the world, runs from
Lake Te Anau, up the Clinton Valley,
over the McKinnon Pass, and along-
side the Sutherland Falls to the Sound.
Tlje new track, about 45 miles long,
over the lately discovered Grave Pass,
will run from Lake Wakatipu, through
magnificent alpine and
lake scenery to the
Sound. It will be
called the Grave and
Talbot track, after the
Government guides
who blazed it. The
chief advantage of the
new route,is that it
provides a round trip
to Maoriland’s famous
Sound; so in future
the haughty tourist
won’t be able to curse
about having to retrace
his steps. The trouble
with “the finest walk
in the world” is that
the energetic tourist
has to come back the
way he went; though
that isn’t so very much of a griev-
ance, If you go early in the
tourist season, when the avalanche
is just waking up and the land-
slide is just settling down, you may
find 24 waterfalls on the way
home which weren’t there on the
way out. Also you may find quite
a different mass of ice on the track,
and six new creeks broken loose,
and a heap of other changes. A
Bulletin man made the mistake
once of being the first tourist over the
road when the season opened. He did it
through ignorance—not heroism; the fact
was that he didn’t know there was any
special season. On the way to the Sound
his hair turned grey, but on the way back
the aspect of affairs was so much changed
that it resumed its natural color. Then he
went to look at Mount Cook and became
bald. He has remained that way ever since.

The grief of the Oldest Inhabitant: —
The license of the Royal Hotel, George-street, Syd-
ney, has been cancelled because of the old pub’s
persistent disrepair. It is one of the most ancient
hotels standing in the city, having been built in
1840 on the site of another Royal, which was burned
down, with the Theatre Royal, on St. Patrick’s night
in the same year. The original house was built about
1830, and had a mill on the roof. As the sails used
to frighten horses, Governor Bourke ordered their
removal. The hotel’s “long room” was first used
as a theatre, but subsequently the home of drama
became independent of the hotel, and was known as
the Theatre Royal. In those days Barnet Levy was
owner of both hotel and theatre. The Victoria,
opened in 1838, smashed the Royal. Levy was dead
by that time, and the widow tried to carry on, and
failed. The fire, which took rise in a stable on an
adjoining property, extended to the hotel, and thus
the end came. Joe Wyatt, who built the Victoria
Theatre (1838) and the Prince of Wales Opera
House (1855), was owner at the time of the Are, the
lessee being John Sparke, who had kept the Aus-
tralian Inn—originally the home of Isaac Nichols
and the first post office —in Lower George-street. He
was father of the late W. E. Sparke, who left half a
million to relatives a few years ago. John Terry
Hughes got possession of the site, and built the
house just delicensed. Apparently he was his own
architect. John Sparke again became the tenant, and
died there in 1851. Amongst notable later landlords
was one of the Wiltshire family, and O. C. Skarratt,
a blue-blood amongst hotelkeepers. Years later he
matle a pile in Mount Morgans. Now, after a life
of threequarters of a century, the old place is doomed
—as it might have been years ago, but for the fact
that staunch buildings on either side held it up.

the Sydney dailies not to mention the un-
pleasing fact, there is little doubt that at time
of writing something of a drought exists in
parts of the Ma State. When you find

effect that there lias
been no rain of any
“beneficial” character
since September, 1913,
and that “the tanks
have given out weeks
ago” ; also that even
the oldest romancers
in the district disre-
member anything so
dry, while the springs
where grandpa used
to wash his sore toe
when a lad are “dry-
ing up”—well, there
is only one conclusion
to he drawn. At
Kiama. (the place
where ' old Parkes
used to keep his
■'“ghosts,V and where
ex-Treasprer Ca n n
keeps his quarries)
the dreary old farce
of “prayers for rain”
has been revived. In
addition, when the
Telegraph, which
last Christmas swelled
out its chest and
proudly announced
that the “estimate”
of the “department”
was for a “record
wheat yield esti-
mated at something

els” —when you find
that inaccurate and
hasty old party print-
ing tales of wheat
without ears, and
20-bushel “estimated”
crops round Barmed-
man or Narromine
actually panning out
at a bare nine bushels,
again, well! The guess iS now down to
about 35,000,000 bushels, and it may drop
lower.

That grand American business motto,
“Rob Him Now!” has evidently been taken
to heart by at least one Sydney person. The
other evening a ferryboat ran down an un-
lighted launch, and while the shrieks were
at their loudest and the confusion at its
maddest, some Samaritan threw off some of
his clothes preparatory to diving in to the
rescue of any splashing shadow he might
catch sight of. later on, he turned
to resume his garments, he found that his
watch had been taken. ■ This incident gives
Sydney a new Mean Champion. The former
holder won the dishonor by stealing the rug
off a sick horse.

SNAPDRAGONS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They are women with a secret, these snapdragons,
That is why they shut their mouths so tight
That every man may know
By the haughty way they go
They have something worth the telling to a wight!
Tenderly I prise them open —find the tongue is all of
gold,
Surely, ’tis the tongue of poets, mellow in a story
old,
Some fair runs of morning meadows, lit with many
golden flowers
That the wizard sun has made us from the silver of
the showers! • .
See them in their velvet bonnets, very modest and
demure,
But I hold their shy demeanor just a cunning form
of lure—
\ onder bloom is striped and fluffy as the skirts of a
Pierette
Lifted on a curve of beauty sly Pierrot will not
forget!
Here is one all soft and creamy as a bride in lan-
gorous hours—
They are women... .they are poets.... and they, best
of all, are flowers!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.

IRRIGATION
COMMIS-
SIONER.
The last full-blooded Tasmanian black
died years ago, and it is alleged now, on
what looks like good authority, that only
one half-caste is left. Till a month ago
there was another, Harry Beaton; but he
died at the age of about 75 on Cape Barren
Island, Bass Straits. His father was an
educated Englishman, who dropped his old
name when he took Mrs. Binghi to be his
blushing bride.
“Martin”: Various scribes have heaved the brick
of scorn at Randolph Bedford’s eulogy of James
Tyson, but the old chap possessed horse-sense all
right. He was watching a farmer struggling with a
bag of chaff near Warwick Downs (Queensland) one
day. The. farmer had nearly filled the bag, and was
ramming in enough to complete the parcel, when it
tore with a loud rip. “You chaps will never save
money by buying inferior bags,” he said. “You lose
time and money by so doing.” There are “Sack
Snecialists” in Sydney, namely, T. SMITH’S, LTD.
Get into touch with them, and save time, temper and
cash.*
In case the recent presentation of the
Koyal Shipwreck Relief Society’s gold medal
to George Reid, per Mrs. Reid, should be
misunderstood, The Bulletin hastens to add
that the High Conditioner has not been
throwing himself into the sea or dragging
anyone out of it. The hero’s reward was
apparently conferred on George because he
had eaten for the Society, and the gold medal
was the only thing the grateful Society had
about it. So George arrives gloriously
amongst the astonished marine heroes with
the usual napkin tucked under his chin, and
his knife and fork firmly grasped in either
hand.
Quinton McKinnon, the Maoriland ex-
plorer who in 1888 discovered the famous
McKinnon Pass that makes possible the over-
land trip to Milford Sound, is to get a brick
put up to his memory. The Gaelic Society
an( l iA e Rugby Union, assisted by
the M.L. Government, are arranging to
place on the summit of McKinnon Pass a
memorial cairn built of rough-hewn stone,
topped by a cross and fronted at the base
by a memorial slab of granite. McKinnon,
who was a lone-hand explorer, was drowned
in Lake Te Anau through his boat capsizing
in 1892 —four years after he blazed the
famous track. He did it all for sheer love
of the game.
AMERICA AND JAPAN.
With the constantly improving means of communi-
cation between Eastern Asia and the Pacific Coast,
the Yellow Peril comes closer and closer every day,
and within a few years the Asiatic problem in the
West might become even more serious than the
negro problem in the South.— Sydney Telegraph.
The Intruder : “ And you, too Mrs. Tele-
graph ! ”
C. A. Schultz, dead in Adelaide at 52, had
been all the working years of his life in the
A.M.P. Society.. He started as a small boy
in Sydney, was in the Melbourne office, then
accountant in Wellington (M.L.), and for
nearly 10 years had been manager in Ade-
laide. The end came with apoplexy, paraly-
sis and a general break-up—mercifully
pretty speedy.
By the Religious Editor: —
The Sydney police ran the spectacled Chidley to
earth again last week, and overpowered him with
blither. Then they haled the desperately mild old
gentleman to court on a charge that would fix any
possessor of a brightly improper picture postcard, and
got him sentenced to two months’ gaol, sentence to
be suspended on Chidley entering into a bond of £2O
to be of good behavior for 12 months. And this is
all so useful and so necessary, because, as everybody
but the unpolished copper knows, Chidley is one of
the best behaved men in Sydney—in fact, he is a
lot better behaved than most policemen. But it was
a great capture. It is useless for Chidley to disguise
himself in a classic hygienic costume unlike anything
else in town, and to try and avert suspicion by walk-
ing about in company with a brown bag. The Law
is dead-sure that he is a dangerous man, because his
clothes can be washed, and it has slowly and pain-
fully learnt those clothes, so that, as long as Chidley
remains in them, the Law will know him at once.
Finally—and this is most important—every Hop in
town has mastered the spelling of his simple name.
So Chidley can rely on being captured and summoned
quite frequently. But, anyhow, why shouldn’t he be
captured and summoned ? A man with so much hair
on his legs arouses suspicion.
Alban Gee, of the Sydney Meat Preserving
Works, is now the only one left of the trin-
ity that started the business of meat-packing
in Australia. The originator of the idea
was C. G. Tindal, who died in England only
a few weeks ago. He was squatting at
Ramornie, on the N.S.W. North Coast, and,
like many other stockowners., had been boil-
ing down beasts for the tallow. The shock-
ing waste of good meat worried Tindal, who
whipped up his influential friends in London
(his father was a governor of the Bank of
England), and raised capital for preserving
works. Thomas Cordingley came out as
chemist; Alban Gee as packer. Things,
however, didn’t go too well, and both were
soon out of a job. Gee struck a clever idea
and established the Sydney Meat Preserving
Co.; kindly and courteous Tom Cordingley,
who died four or five years ago, moved pre-
sently up to Townsville, and there set up
meat works, which the Trust lately took
over. Tindal stuck to Ramornie, and even-
tually the whole thing fell into his hands. It
is much the oldest beefery in Australia, and
of late years has specialised in beef extract,
hundreds of gallons of which are understood
to go into the innards of the French army.
Apparently the newly-appointed superfluity
has to open his head and say something when
greatness is shoved upon him. Munro-Fergu-
son. the new foreign G.-G. of Australia,
speaking grandiloquently to some of his con-
stituents at Leith (they must have been
deeply uninterested) declared that “Australia
was more British than Canada and that it”
(that is to say, Australia) “was the brightest
of all the Dominions.” What does Munro-
Ferguson know of Australia, and how is it
“the brightest of all the Dominions”? Does
he mean that it is the brightest borrower?
“X.P.D.” adds a few remarks:—
Ex-Governor Fuller’s kinsman, the Rev. Wyndham
S. Heathcote, who has got the billet of chief priest
to the Melbourne Unitarians, is likely to raise fury
among’ the belltoppers of Victoria. His fatal gift of
wit brought him big congregations in Bundaberg
(Q.) when he was rector of the Anglican Church
there. The wind of his eloquence was not tempered
to the front pews, and a certain rich man was so
wild that he left the church loudly and presented
it 100 to the local R.C. priest. Heathcote’s sins
against the Right Thinking section of Bundaberg
were many, and included a lecture on Socialism,
delivered in a theatre; remarks to the effect that
the Sermon on the Mount didn’t mean something
quite different; and remarks made in a public place
(to wit, “that man Fisher’s’’ meeting) about the Bib-
lical assertion that “The evil thing shall be no more
called Liberal.’’ Heathcote will have big congrega-
tions. Some will come to learn, some to laugh with
him, some to get mad, some to feed on intellect and
oratory. But they’ll come.
From an old gallery hand :
The atrabilious Wm. Forster, for a time Jack
Robertson’s Treasurer, had a bitter hatred of Parkes
and his proteges. For this reason Dan O’Connor
used to be often severely tongue-lashed. One night
when Forster was speaking, Dan made a personal
interjection, and Forster, in a tone which was as
caustic as the words, said: “Mr. Speaker, I would
not presume to ape the hon. member. For me to try
to assume the Chesterfieldian airs of politeness and
the Addisonian elegance of diction which are so
characteristic of the hon. member would be to
attempt the impossible. It has been my misfortune
not to have had opportunity to acquire those more
than gentlemanly and cultured instincts which are
associated with the noble profession of pig-sticking.”
Dan had been fined for slaughtering a lamb in a
back room of his home on a Sunday morning. The
charge was denied, Dan asserting that at the time
of the alleged offence he was at Mass. No one
doubted that he had been to Mass, but the magistrate
believed that he had killed the lamb before he went.
The incident gave the irascible Forster his chance,
and he said: “The hon. member presents the truly
edifying, Christian-like _ spectacle of sanctifying
God’s holy day by shedding the blood of an innocent
lamb, and then with the blood on his hands and the
reek of it in his beard, going to God’s holy edifice,
and there, with devout genuflections, humbling him-
self before the Image of the Lord.”

just outside the entrance to Port Jackson,
56 years ago-

Ex-hotelkeeper “Geebung” Kelly’s death
in Sydney recalls one of Henry* Parkos’s
early jobs in Australia. When Kelly landed
in 1839 he found work in P. N. Russell's
foundry, then in what is now Dalley-street;
and Parkes was one of his early shop-mates.
Elliot Smith, son of a N. S. Wales public
school teacher, has been selected vice-presi-
dent of the Royal Society of Great Britain.
He graduated at Sydney in 1893 after a
brilliant course, and was marked as a special-
ist in the structure of the brain. Journey-
ing to England, he climbed one rung after
another until he reached Cairo as
professor of anatomy. To-day he is recog-
nised as the authority on the comparative
anatomy of the brain, and is also boss ex-
pert on the bones and intelligence of the
ancient Egyptian mummy. A highly-cultured
Australian can always depend upon honor-
able attention in a foreign country.
Paul Foelsche, Inspector of Police in the
Northern Territory from the end of 1869 to
the end of 1903, was so content with the
local climate and his hobbies of Freemasonrv,
dentistry, rifle-making and photography that
he took only two regular holidays in his long
official career, which lasted till he was 73
years old.
SENATOR RUSSELL (Vic.).
A page of Kelly Gang history:—
Passed out at Wangaratta, on February 9, aged
75, ex-Sergeant Arthur Loftus Maule Steele, who
brought down Ned Kelly with a charge of buckshot
in the legs, on June 28, 1880. After shooting ■ the
informer, Aaron Sherritt, in the hills near Beech-
worth, the four members of the gang took charge of
the Glenrowan Hotel, and packed all the residents
therein. The prisoners included the local constable.
They made the railway ganger tear up the line on a
steep embankment on the Beechworth side of Glen-
rowan, and returned to the pub to await develop-
ments. A special train loaded with police was
despatched to Beechworth as soon as the news reached
Melbourne of the murder of Sherritt, and the lot
would have come to grief at the embankment but for
the act of schoolmaster Gurnow (still at Ballarat),
who, on the plea of visiting his sick child, persuaded
Ned to allow him to leave the hotel late at night.
Gurnow ran down the line in the darkness and
stopped the train. The police then surrounded the
hotel and commenced pouring lead into its thin
walls, where men, women and children were huddled,
with the result that at least two innocent people
were killed. Some of the residents of Glenrowan are
still drawing - pensions from the Government for
bullets which they are carrying in their bodies.
Steele was patrolling near Wangaratta when one of
his men heard the firing at Glenrowan. The patrol
hurried to the scene. “Just at the break of day,”
Steele told a Bulletin man, “we were lying in the
timber at the rear of the hotel when behind us in
the fog came a solitary figure enveloped in what
appeared to be a long cloak. I thought for the
moment it was a local black with a blanket wrapped
round him; we had not the faintest idea that any
of the gang had escaped from the house. Suddenly
the figure raised its arm and fired several revolver
shots. Ned’s bad shooting can only be accounted
for by the weight of his armor and the difficulty he
had in the bad light of seeing through the slit in
the helmet. We returned the fire and saw him
stagger as our bullets struck his body, but he still
came on. I could hear the bullets hit him with a
ring, and grasping the fact that he was armored,
discarding my revolver, grabbed my gun, fired at his
legs with a charge of buckshot, and brought him
down. I rushed at him as he struggled to his feet
to take aim, and grabbed his revolver arm. He was
all out and gave no more trouble.” Steele has been
living in retirement for many years at Wangaratta
with his wife and his grown-up family and his
roses. The aged mother of the Keilys is, by the way,
still living—a hospitable old lady whose face is full
of sorrow.
“Red Tape”: James Killian, who waited
on successive N. S. Wales Lands Ministers
for quarter of a century has, after death,
panned out at £9913, a sum more than equal
to the estates of half a dozen Ministers who
can easily be remembered. Killian was
head messenger in the Department, and his
financial stability was an envious word
among the clerical staff. He came down
real handsome for his mother church, and
thereby leaves an inspiring example to an
age that more and more inclines to blew its
substance on frail vanities and unsubstan-
tial froth.
THE VICE-REGAL SUPERMAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The coining of two Governors has imposed an ex-
hausting task upon Melbourne dailies. They have
been put to the necessity of representing two dis-
tinct individuals as special creations, each the best
possible. After a sunburst of laudation extending
over two weeks, we are left with the impression that
the two new figureheads are not only superior to all
other men in virtue and attainments, but each is
superior to both of them.
Behold, how glorious they grow !
How wise and worshipful they are!
What wondrous loads of things they know !
Each intellect is like a star
That, through the darkness and the mire,
Fills this mean world with heavenly fire—-
Both are a beacon and a goal
'To him who hath a hopeful soul.
As poor Australians born, we know
We may not scale the dizzy I'rf~hts
Where pure Imperial Governors glow ;
But still our weak, aspiring flights
Unto that sweet perfection lift
Us from the dregs above the drift.
And that is all the Gover -nor
By Heaven is created for.
From out the wastes of nothingness
He’s wrested like a splendid pearl ,
Snatched from the depths. Then journals stress
His gifts, his hair’s entrancing curl,
The perfect turn of calf and lip,
His glorious works of statesmanship ;
With what hard days and sleepless nights
He’s saved his country at sham fights.
Her Ladyship—consider her !
Her splendid style, her winning grace,
The tact that’s never known to err,
The subtle glory of her face.
The fit and lovely mate is she
For that most perfect Excellency,
Reviewing which we hardly can
Believe there’s been a fall of man.
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

Thus in any four months since 1910 the profits
have exceeded the total amount of capital
put into the undertaking! Had the State built the
line, and required (say) 6 p.c. on the capital to
provide interest and sinking fund, Private Enterprise,
in the shape of the Broken Hill cos. and the
townspeople, would have saved something like
£400,000 during the past three years. State Social-
ism may be a tiger; but, when it gets a chance,
Private Enterprise knows far better how to rend the
lamb and suck its blood.
Of course, officially, the business isn’t put in the
foregoing manner. The management (in its way a
very good management indeed) says the capital is
£250,000, and the dividends for the year conse-
quently a paltry 50 p.c. The discrepancy is explained
this way:—
1886. —Co. registered; paid capital £46,800, plus
£16,000 in promoters’ shares.
1888. —First stock watering: £41,860 capitalised
(two shares for every three).
1889. —Second stock watering; £17,433 (one in six);
Third stock watering, £15,273 (one in
eight).
1890. —Fourth stock watering, £27,473 (one in five).
1891. —Fifth stock watering, £32,967 (one in five).
1910.—Sixth stock watering, £52,194 (one in four).
So you get this: —
Capital actually subscribed .. .. £46,800
Promoters’ little lot . . . . . . 16,000
Profits capitalised . . . . . . 187,200
Total alleged capital . . . . £250,000
The promoters’ little lot would now represent about
£64,000 of capital, carrying a div. for last year of
£32,000. Probably nobody knows now what became
of that £16,000 in shares. The late W. P. McGre-
gor, who went into the N.S.W. Parliament to put
the Bill through, and retired as soon as he had done
the job, is dead ; and most of the secret went with
him. Certainly he did say that he could have got a
concession to build a railway to the moon if he could
offer enough “inducement.” Those were the good
days when payment of members was unknown, and
the House consisted of patriots who were above
sordid considerations.
Get rid of it ? It is quite easy. The Act, passed
is aforesaid, provides a way. All the State has to
:lo is to pay the co. 21 times the average annual
orofit for the preceding seven years. So the State
-an have this scrap of cheap line, running through
30 miles of saltbush plain, for a mere £2,525,000
ar so. And how much better off would either the
State or Broken Hill be then? If it bought the line
and got a return of 6 p.c. to pay interest and sink-
ing fund, the State would itself have to collect an
average of over £150,000 in excess of working exes.
That is to say, instead of reducing fares and freights
it would have to increase them. And perhaps even
6 p.c. wouldn’t allow for a sufficient sinking fund,
because the line will be of no value when Broken
Hill is worked out; and vast as the Broken Hill ore
body is, there is an end to it somewhere. As far as
one can see, it won’t come to an end with a sudden
jerk; but it will tail off to nothing. The chances
are that these are the years of its heaviest tonnages ;
not because at least three mines, and probably five,
won’t go on producing as much ore for the next
dozen years as they are producing now. but because
the present production is largely inflated by the
using-up of tailings dumps which went on growing
for 15 years until the zinc-flotation process was
developed. There will be very little of those dumns
left in five years from now, given reasonable markets
for zinc and lead during that period. One way and
another, about one-third of the co.’s freight is due
to the treatment of these stored products. A further
possibility is the construction of a Government line
from Condobelin or elsewhere to Broken Hill, and
thence on to the S. Aus. border. The Condobolin-
Broken Hill work has been passed by Parliament;
all it awaits now is the loosening of Cohen’s purse.
But, unless the line connects with the S. Aus. svs-
tem at Cockburn, it won’t cut very deeplv into this
co.’s traffic. The bulk of the stuff will still go on to
Port Pirie, where a port can be reached in little less
than half the distance.
The co.’s capital (as watered) is in 250,000 shares.
Fixed assets, including rolling stock ( £71,864),
amount to £215,009 ; and there was on December
31 approximately £233,000 of other assets net, nearly
all public securities and cash.

Hooray! That is better almost than The Bulletin
expected. Twelve months ago, in noting that the
directors proposed to transfer £150,000 to capital,
making the £lO shares paid to £3 instead of £2,
this paper hoped that the Board wouldn’t depart
from its old policy of strengthening the reserves to
keep pace with the growing business. In other
words, that it wouldn’t jujmp up the dividend to
£67,500 (=l5 p.c. on the increased capital).. As
will be seen by the table, the directors actually don’t
pay any more, in the aggregate, than they did the
previous year. The result is a magnificent addition
of £70,097 to reserves, after handing £5OOO over to
the Officers’ Provident Fund and writing £2773 off
investments. The interest earnings for the year were
£43,863, so that the whole of the underwriting
profit, less £1137, was available to strengthen the
co. and give greater security to the policyholders.
As a matter of fact, the progress of the co. is so
rapid—which is another way of saying that the
risks are increasing so fast —-that there are only two
things to be done; either the shareholders must put
more capital into the business or they must leave a
very large proportion of their profits untouched.
Consider the growth of the risks, as measured by pre-
miums, and compare them with the funds (capital
and reserves combined) :
Funds in
Proportion to

So it will be seen that, in spite of the fact that the
directors have hung on to so much of the profits,
the risks are not even now as well covered as they
used to be; in other words, the risks have increased
more rapidly than the funds. Moreover, though
there has been a steady improvement in the figures
since 1909, the funds in proportion to premiums are
even ifow only 137 p.c., which is less than the
British average and much less than the Australian.
As deposits grow and a bank takes the risk of work-
ing on them, that class of financial concern invariably
gets in more capital from its shareholders, and doesn’t
depend solely on the accumulation of reserved profits.
An insurance office might reasonably do the same
thing. This co. ought to have now another £150,000
at least of capital; and the directors ought to get it
in. Even then the co.’s capital and reserves would
amount to less than £l6O for every £lOO of pre-
miums, which would still be not quite as good as the
average of the 20 best British offices and considerably
below the Australian average. To quite deserve it's
brilliant. ac’'ievement in more than doubling the
premium income in 13 years the co. should increase
its capital to £600,000. If it won’t do that, the
directors should sit tight and not raise the dividend
a single point until capital and reserves combined
are brought up to where they can challenge com-
parison with the figures of other great offices.

The expense rate is considerably below and The loss
