offices and other properties, on the whole they seem a
satisfactory and profitable lot; though, seeing that
nearly half-a-million is in public securities, it is
very doubtful whether enough has been provided to
write these assets down to the low values at which
most of them now stand. So long as the co. doesn’t
have to realise on them it doesn’t matter much,
though, what the market quotation is, since that
doesn’t affect the interest return; wherefore the best
the. co. can hope for is that there won’t be another
Frisco disaster to call for a heap of money in a hurry
until, at any rate, public securities command a better
figure than they do to-day. Any ordinary trouble
can be met out of the till and from fixed deposits;
between branch balances and cash in other forms
there was a trifle of £190,000 to pay out with at
the end of November.

In 1904, a drought having hit the eastern part, of
Australia, the devil was sick and a saint would be.
This co. therefore decided to write down its capitai
by £73,384. At that time there were 183,460 issued
£1 shares paid to 18s. These were reduced to 12s.
shares paid to 10s., which meant that £73,384 dis-
appeared from the capital account. Things soon
improved—probably they never were as bad as they
looked —and a couple of years ago 6s. per share was
restored. Since then the shares have been nominally
18s., with 16s. paid. Now it has been decided to
restore the other 2s. ; they will thus for the future
be 205., paid to 18s. The div. at the old rate is
paid for the past half-year on the whole 18s. The
co.’s progress has justified the restoration of capital
supposedly lost. Look at this comparison:—
Dec., ’O3. Dec., ’l2. June, ’l3. Dec., ’l3
Liquid assets. . £49,495 £105,201 £109,899 £ll9 073
Liabilities .. 43,146 Z 6,194 22,503 25,495
Surplus ... £6,349 £79,007 £87,396 £93,578
Thus in 10 years the surplus of assets, exclusive of
freeholds, plant and fixed property, has grown by
£87,229. Part of this growth may have been due to
the sale of property and plant; but there is no trace
of anything of that sort in the accounts. On the
contrary, judging by the steady, movement, term after
term, the increase has been due simply to the accu-
mulation of profit in excess of the amount required to
pay the dividend and keep the plant in order. In
most cases the best a co. can do is to put the surplus
into the business. Here, £70,000 is in deposits or
public securities; and meantime, while this nice
little lot has been scraped together, the requirements
of the business haven’t been neglected, judging by
the growth of the profits. In fact, frequent refer-
ences have been made in the directors’ reports to
plant-additions. Just at present the largest refri-
gerating machine in the southern hemisphere is going
in ; and extensions of the cold storage accommodation
are 1 aing considered. No doubt, when these
improvements are made, the book value of the fixed
assets will jump; but, on past experience, it will
soon be reduced again by amounts written off profits
to cover depreciation. Altogether, then, it seems that
it can safely be assumed that more than enough has
been spent on plant out of profits to cover deprecia-
tion ; and if that is so, then the accumulation of liquid
assets, mentioned above, represents real profit in ex-
cess of the dividends. The 10 years’ record, in that
case, is very fine indeed. Certainly most things have
favored the co. The _ State hasn’t had a general
drought since 1904; in fact, seasons have mostly
been very good, and the demand for cold-storage, etc.,
exceptional. Also there have been conditions of in-
flated prosperity,Vlue to heavy State borrowing, from
which the co. has derived great benefit, first through
the cutting-out of competition in (say) the ice busi-
ness, there being enough boom trade to go
round, and then through unusual numbers of people
being able to afford to buy the co.’s cooked foods, or
being driven to do it because of the scarcity of Mary
Ann, Mary Ann’s father being so affluent that the
lady herself isn’t cooking or washing up for any
other party just at present. Conditions, in short,
have been abnormal, and this co. has been so placed
that it has derived an uncommon amount of benefit
from the abnormality.

If this co. had a motto, like some other trustee cos.
and Peter Doody, it would be “Least said, soonest
mended.’’ it is the most silent concern in the busi-
ness. It doesn’t even say the value of the estates in
hand, so one can’t trace its progress in that respect.
Just about all that one can do is to dig out the
figures given in the foregoing table, and add these:—
Dec., 1903. Dec., 1913.
Liquid assets .. .. £14,439 £36,878
Other assets . . .. 21,506 28,238
Liabilities . . .. 50 Nil
Liquid assets consist of Victorian Government stock
and Board of Works debentures; the other assets
include the co.’s Queen-street premises (£15,962)
and sundry debtors. In 10 years the co. has thus
added £22,439 to its liquid assets; and they are
hard stuff, whatever the unspecified other assets may
be. As it has done this on a paid capital of £25,000,
and after paying a dividend ranging from 8 p.c. to
30 p.c., the Equity wouldn’t be guilty of empty
boasting _ if it said that it was doing pretty well.
The co. is in 50,000 shares of £2 10s., each paid.to
10s., and the last time there was a sale of them, a
few days ago, the market said they were worth 30s.
Capital and reserves combined are worth! 255. (assum-
ing that book values of assets are real values), which
means that the market adds ss. for goodwill. Evi-
dently the market doesn’t reckon that there are going
to be any more 8 or 10 p.c. dividends. And it is
probably right, seeing that, . even if the business
didn’t earn more than its exes., 4 p.c. interest on the
co.’s funds would just about pay a 10 p.c. div. on
the capital.

Though there is nominally a good margin left over
after paying the 8 p.c. dividend, it is doubtful
whether the co. writes anything off for general wear
and tear. Hence the margin of true profits is pro-
bably very small. And when the building and recon-
struction boom eases down, the true profits will
almost certainly be affected. The shrinkage thusfar
in the visible profits is probably due to two causes:
(1) the keen competition of electric lifts; (2) the
increasing expense of maintenance. As to the former,
this table of gross revenue for the latter half of each
of the past eight years is significant:—
1906 .. £12,975 | 1910 .. £11,852

The amounts shown in the first table as written off
during the past two years are debited to Plant Re-
newal and Contingency Account; previous to that
all renewals and contingencies had been charged
against profits before the latter were shown. The
new practice, of course, puts the best possible face
on the current profits as shown in the first table. At
December, 1908, the co. had liquid assets totalling
£9975 and liabilities of £1292. a surplus of £8683 ;
as at December, 1913, the liquid assets were £BBB2
and liabilities £3199, a surplus of £5683. So that
during the five years the surplus of assets (apart
from land, plant and water rights) has shrunk by
£3OOO. And though in the same period the co. has
spent £14,275 on property, plant, etc., the unsatis-
factory feature is that the outlay hasn’t produced
any extra revenue; on the contrary, the revenue has
pretty steadily decreased,

The “A” and “B” shares are entitled to 10 p.c., if
the co. can pay it and still charge only 3s. 6d. per
1000 ft. for gas; and the limit for the “C” shares
is 7 p.c. unless the charge for gas is under 3s. 6d.
The “A” and “B” shares represent the old capital,
including reserves (visible and internal) capitalised
under the Gas Act of 1912. They amount to £275
for everv £lOO of old capital, so that even an 8
p.c. dividend is as good now as a 22 p.c. dividend
would have been prior to the changes caused by
recent legislation. But the shareholders are not
drawing quite 22 p.c. on the amount actually paid
by them, because part of the reserves that were
capitalised consisted of premiums paid for shares.
Giving that in, the return is remarkably good. For
the future, however, the only hope worth very much
is that the dividends on “A” and “B” shares may
be put up to 10 p.c., and on “C’s” to 7 p.c. They
can go beyond that, but to pay an extra \ p.c. the
price of gas must come down Id. below 3s. 6d. The
latter standard may, it is true, be raised if it can
be proved that Wages Boards’ awards have sufficiently
affected the cost of production. At present the
margin in excess of the dividend now paid is so
small that one wouldn’t expect an early rise in
divs.; on the other hand, it must be remembered
that, as most gas cos. are applying to raise the
standard price, the natural inclination would be to
show as small a profit as possible under existing con-
ditions. Apropos the accounts, this co. hasn’t yet
given the shareholders the advantage of the full in-
formation which it has to supply to the Department;
in that respect it differs from the Sydney co., which
in its published accounts now follows the schedule to
the Act. Dealing with the Sydney co.’s accounts
the other day The Bulletin remarked that for the
past half-year the management had charged £5OOO
to a Depreciation Fund, and it was questioned whether
there was any authority in the Act for establishing
such a reserve. There still doesn’t appear to be any
such authority in the Act (though there should be) ;
but in schedule 5 (“Revenue account’’) such an item
is provided for. Whether Act and schedule are in
conflict somebody else may fight out.

Besides the reserves mentioned above, there is
£15,471, to the credit of a “Depreciation Insurance
Fund,” whose purpose is obscure. This reserve is
fed partly on the profits shown in the table as written
off, and partly on manna which drops from Heaven
or other ill-defined places: £7OOO fell into the
account in the early half of last year without men-
tioning what it was or where it came from. This
may have been special profit; on the other hand, the
whole of this reserve may be a mere set-off against
actual depreciation. Anyway, depreciation is not
apparently provided for otherwise, though, of course,
maintenance is charged to working ekes. Still, you
can’t “maintain” a pub or a brewery for ever; the
time comes when you can’t patch any longer, but
must have new pants. Hence, there ought to be
a depreciation (or, better still, a replacement) fund,
built up out of profits. This depreciation fund may
be that kind of thing, or it may be a leasehold
amortisation fund. It is too vague. What the
assets of the co. are like The Bulletin doesn’t
know, and therefore it can’t say anything as to the
security of any principal invested in this concern:
the trouble is that almost all the assets are thrown
into a heap, and how much of the £108,069 is re-
presented by intangible goodwill and water right and
how much by freehold properties is quite uncertain.
So far as profits are concerned, if they are as good
as they look they are exceedingly good. This paper
confesses that it doesn’t quite know how the thing
has been done; but, according to the published
accounts, the value of “freehold and leasehold pro-
perties, plant and machinery, rolling stock, goodwill,
water right and supply, etc.,” has increased from
£77,228 to £108,069 in 12 months, and, meantime,
no new share capital has been got in, and borrowed
capital has increased only from £5855 to £20,912.
If the balance has been provided out of ordinary
surplus profits in 12 months, then this co. is on
a good thing all right.

In 1904 the Board of Health required certain struc-
tural alterations, and the co. made the best of a
bad bargain by remodelling the whole place at a
cost of about £17.000. That meant an overdraft
and ether liabilities of £13,038 as at December,
1904, against which, however, there was £2718 in
cash and deposits. To-day, nine years later, though
no new capital has been got in, the liabilities (apart
from a few pounds of rent paid in advance) total
only £44, while cash and deposits amount to £2093.
Thus, though but lately a further £2OOO has been
spent in re-upholstering, etc., the cash position has
improved* by £12,413 in nine years. Unfortunately
the co. can’t regard that as surplus profit. The
trouble is that this co. only holds the bulk of the
property on lease; and though the latter runs well
past the middle of the century, the capital repre-
sented by leasehold and improvements has to be
restored out of profits within the period, or it is
liable to be lost for ever. There ought, of course, to
be a fund for restoring this capital; but so far the
money that might reasonably have been available
for the purpose has been mopped up by the improve-
ments and renovations mentioned above. From this
onward things should, however, improve, because
under the terms of the sub-lease to J. C. Williamson,
Ltd., which runs to 1928. this Theatre Royal Prop.
Co. had to spend the £2OOO on the renovation men-
tioned above, and a further small sum on extras, but
thereafter all repairs, renewals and decoration, apart
from structural alterations, have to be paid for by
the sub-lessees; and the rent goes up after 1918.
At present the leasehold and leasehold buildings stand
in the books at £34,200; and a sinking fund of
£lOOO a year, invested at 4 p.c., would wipe that
right out in 22 years. So, even providing for the
extinction of the capital debt on the leasehold in 22
years, a net revenue of £3OOO a year would pro-
vide the sinking fund and a 4 p.c. div. (which is
11 p.c. in excess of the present div.), leaving any-
thing in excess of £3OOO for contingencies. This
assumes that the ground rent will go on at the
£lBOO a year which has been paid hitherto. On
that point The Bulletin has no information.

in the distant past can be no guide to the future,
the circumstances having changed completely. The
co. secured an area alongside the Stockton, partly
under the beach, partly under the ocean. The
expense of opening the mine and of keep-
ing it open was so great that, though the
coal was first-class and the output fair, the
dividends were usually only 5 Or 6 p.c. The two
shafts, which had to be “tubbed,” alone cost as much
to sink as should open up a mine. And then the

That shows an apparent improvement of nearly

a lot of leeway to make up ; the proper thing will
lie to go on writing down the old property out of

lated to pay off the debenture debt. When that is
done the co. will have the moderate capital of
£ 123,665 represented by the Bellbird property, the
Hetton plant, and the more or less derelict old mine ;
and with the capital unencumbered, very fair divi-
dends should then be maintained —better, anyway,
than when the co. had a quicksand to drop its
money into. #
Vegetable Creek (N.S.W.) has struck
4ft. of good wash in a working 577 ft. east of Allen’s
shaft, which is between the Rock shaft and the
upper boundary. At the end of January this work-
ing was in 18in. of wash worth 401 b., so it has
changed in 20ft. or so from poverty to comparative
riches. The increased output from the present block-
ing-out campaign had already put more heart into
this speculation, which has been debt-ridden from
the start ; but disappointments have been so persistent
that the market will not budge.
Another change takes place at Lloyd’s,
the N.S.W. copper mine which holds out promises,
hut never cashes them. Manager J. O. Armstrong
has retired, and Stanley Low takes charge.
* * * *
The London mining director is a well-
greased guinea-pig. In fees the Rt. Hon. Earl who
is chairman of the Sulphide Corporation cuts up
£2500 a year with his four colleagues. But that
doesn’t satisfy them. “In accordance with the
articles of association” the directors draw a percentage
on dividends, and last year this put another £7500
into the five pockets, giving them £2OOO a .year
apiece. Payment on dividends is better, no doubt,
than payment on general expenditure; still, it is a
bad plan, inasmuch as directors may have far more
solid work to do under adverse conditions, when pro-
fits are nil or thereabouts, than when the concern
is in a patch of clover and a bit of mismanagement
or a whole lot of neglect doesn’t matter.
Hawkins Hill (N.S.W.), which, by the
way, sent its reserve shares to market some little
time ago, is still pegging along on its main level to
reach the cross-course; and 10 weeks or thereabouts
should decide whether the expected rich gold is there
or not. Long before then the battery—ls head of it,
anyway—should be going regularly on the 200 ft. of
reef which the drive has so far opened up.
* » * *
Lake View South (W.A.) tells its share-
holders from London that the work done on its
Ivalgoorlie property during the past 18 months was
on a very small scale. Tributors are still in charge,
and the Board proposes to let them stay there.
Meantime, the co.’s affairs are being conducted at
what is termed a minimum of expense ; the directors
are even doing without their fees. The only other
asset the co. has are a few debentures in a concern
holding a half-share in a Klondyke venture which
has so far recovered £81,048.
Backed up by Government assistance and
the hank’s prayers. Phillips River (IV.A.) is fighting
hard for another lease of life. About 1000 tons of
ore have latelv been dumped at the smelters, and as
“splendid bodies of rich ore” are “known to exist in
other mines,” the place is tipped by its supporters to
go ahead. Undoubtedly there is a good deal of ore
showing, hut it is in the usual bunches, so far as
one can judge, and a continuous smelting campaign
is out of the question.
* * * *
Associated Northern (W.A.) has got
another attack of the creeps, and is obliged to give
up stoping at No. 4 level for the present. lhe
ground is “quiet” enough, but increased pressure
requires expensive timbering, and this under exist-
ing conditions makes too big a hole in the profits.
Onerations will, therefore, be suspended in the sul-
phide zone until a plant is installed. This means
12 months’ delay.
* * *
Last financial year North Kalgurli (W.A.),
yielded £22,300 from the treatment of tributors
ore, and the co. got £4OOO in royalties. In the
last six or seven years £145,000 has been dug out,
of which the co. has received £23,000 So far,
shareholders have not received a cent,. The co. has
enough cash in hand to pay a small div. ; but r ‘
ently its policy is to hoard up a bit more and then
splash it up on some foreign venture.
* * *
In the West the Great Boulder (W.A.) is
being growled at for going to Victoria in search ot
a new property. Still it has hunted round in the \\es„
for a long while, and the Stawell group sampled nearer
the owners’ estimate than anything it has ever tested in
Groperland. On the old mine things are no better.
Some deep boring recently done from the adjoining
Ivanhoe workings proved disappointing. The drill
met with nothing in the shape of lode-stuff, and
boring was stopped at 3232 ft. from the surface.
* * * *
A Melbourne crowd has acquired and
floated the Queen Ann lease at Southern Cross (W.A.).
Two large lode formations are visible, and sundry
parcels of ore taken from these bodies and treated
locally indicate that they are worth developing. The
purchase price was £lOO and a tenth interest in a
co. of 60,000 shares.
* * * *
Lancefield (W.A.) has been re-christened
Beria Consols, and will in future be operated in
conjunction with the other industrial enterprises. ot
the new owners, the Kalgoorlie and Boulder Fire-
wood Co. New plant, including a big gas-producer,
has been ordered. #
Siberia Consols (W.A.) has got an exten-
sion of time for the payment of the £4OOO balance
due to vendors. Its due date was June 1 of this
year, but a new agreement just entered into pro-
vides for the payment of £lOOO at the end of 1914
and the remainder, as profits allow, by June, 1916.
Three months ago the co. had nearly £6OOO in cash,
so that if there is any gold left in the mine at all
the wiping off of its debt to the vendors should be
dead easy, and with ample funds in hand the sys-
tematic prospecting of the property made easier
still. Up to date the results have been disappointing.
Hannan’s Reward North (W.A.) is get-
ting talked about after a long silence. Nearly £5OOO
has been extracted from a new body ot ore within
the last two or three months, and, according to the
present owner, a good deal more wealth remains to
be taken out. This show was once owned by a
small weakly-financed Adelaide co., which made calls
and finally reconstructed itself out of existence.
Since then it has passed through many hands.
* ■* *
Burma Mines, Ltd., in which a lot of
good Lake View money was sunk, is at it again.
This time it is “considering the creation of £35,000
worth of 6 p.c. prior lien debenture stock. in
August last it got an advance of £35,000 by a first
prior lien mortgage on the slags at the mine,
agreed to repay the loan out of the first load sales.
The co. now wants to pledge its whole undertaking,
instead of the slags alone, and to postpone repay-
ment until the end of next year. The consent of
the stockholders has to be obtained, but this, it is
stated, has already been arranged.
* * *
A London opinion of Great Fingall’s posi-
tion: “A profit in sight of £50,000 or 4s. per
share. A cash balance of ss. i d. per share. A
prospective value of 6s. sd. per share. In other words,
with the market standing at 165., the value of shares
is represented by 36 p.c. cash assets 25 p.c. profit in
sight (at 10s. net per ton), and 40 p.c. prospective
value. On the basis of 10s. profit per ton, this
would presume the existence of another 160,000 tons
below the 18th level, pr a continuance of the size
and quality of the new ore shoot for another seven
levels. While the above estir ate assumes ani ore
reserve of 100,000 tons of 40s. ore above No. 18 level,
the official ere reserve estimate published six months
ago mentioned 50,000 tons only as actually in sight.
Worked out on this conservative basis, the continu-
ance of the new shoot would have to be assumed
for more than 10 levels below the 18th. Moreover, if
the plant were to run to its full capacity as in the
palmy days of the last decade, or at the rate of
20,000 tons per month, the ore reserve would be
exhausted in a very short time. Bmally, the fact
should not be overlooked that the new /olTveUhe
-s already out of the new shoot at the 18th level, the
shoot pitching farther away from it. ’ Word comes
from the West that the co. has taken a 12 months
option on the Big Bell group which the Great
Boulder turned down a few months-ago. #

The first crushing this year from the
Vulcan (N.Q.) was 682 tons stone for 20 tons con-
centrates valued at £1558. Last year the mine
crushed 8863 tons for 332 tons concentrates worth
£28.694; average, £3 4s. 9d. per ton °r stone.
The Vulcan is being prospected at 1050 ft., but there
the drive shows only odd patches of tin worm
5 p.c.; the ore going to the mill comes from the
stones between 350 ft. and 900 ft., and the falling off
in the return is due mostly to the 350 ft. stope having
become lower grade. #
Charters Towers grabs now at almost any
straw on account of the poor results in the deep
levels. The biggest share-market turnover has lately
been in Clarks. This co. is driving at the 426 ft.
level on a small reef of. good quality. The last cut
is reported to have sin. of nice stone, and, as usual,
a specimen or two are shown around town to whoop
things up a bit. # *
At last Mount Oxide’s London deal looks
like completion, the weak and foolish Australians
who found the mine and developed it having been
mostly squeezed out. Oxide is another trumpet-
tongued argument against the bowelless thing called
British capital, especially when it has a curly nose
behind it. _ „
* * *
At Gympie the East Oriental and Glan-
mire is going to provide a little gamble. Slate fl° ors
below 2154 ft. are dipping to the east, and the coun-
try is more mineralised and favorable for gold. It is
a peculiarity of this field that the same reef is often
barren in one formation and rich in another, so a
favorable change in the country rock is frequently
accompanied by a rise in shares. A big formatio
carrying quartz leaders and a snnnkling of go 1 u
previously put the E. Oriental and Glanmire on the
dividend list ; hence the interest taken in the present
development. # *
The closing-down of Cbillagoe smelters
will give North Queensland a nasty knock. One way
and another 700 men will have to take the traek.
The co. tried to come at the Government for a loan
of £30,000, presumably to help with the line to the
Mulligan coal, and the construction of coke ovens,
etc ' but it had no luck this time. The story is
that’ coke and fuel are too expensive till Mulligan is
connected. Mulligan may help ; but the most serious
trouble is the scarcity of cheaply-won ore, and Ein-
asleigh is the only mine that holds out much hope
for the future.
* * * *
Great Fitzroy (Q.) goes further and
further to the bad. December’s loss was £l5OO ;
January’s £l6lO. For January the furnaces ate
1631 tons of ore, showing 2.81 p.c. copper and 2.61
dwt. gold, and 1426 tons concentrates, showing
9.51 p.c. copper and 6.87 dwt. gold; and though
there was £12,492 in the lot, the expenses climbed
still higher and reached £14,102.
Fat names appear on the buff-colored
balance-sheets of the Cock’s Pioneer Gold and Tin
Mines (Vic.), which represents the resurrected elec-
tric-barge show once run by Jack Dane. Two
Baillieus, one Robinson, J. L. Wharton, Gordon
Lyon and Hector McDonald now stand beside Smith,
of Wangaratta, who, despite his name, has plenty
of oof to back a mining fancy. Cock’s lies at Eldor-
ado, and the drift to be sluiced is said to carry gold
and tin worth 9d. per cub. yard. A. H. P. Moline,
brought down from Y Water, Emmaville, is in con-
trol, and has the job of cutting a new channel for
the old creek that swamped out Jack Dane’s big
paddock. The duty of the new plant is to sluice
600,000 cub. yards of material, to lift it to the boxes
and to stack the tailings. Electricity is to do every-
thing except drive the power plant, where steam
power will rule. A bill of £23,000 will have to
be footed before the wheels go round. When the plant
is working, the big face of drift on which the old
barge is now doddering will melt away like snow
before the sun, for a huge output means solid pro-
fits. The project is a hobby of E. S. Robinson, jun.,
brother to Lionel; and when he next patronises this
side of the globe he will be able to tip the mine as
a promising beginner.
* * * *
New Chum Goldfields, Bendigo, knocks
the bears out with Is. div. on top of a short run of
6d. payments. Mining pressmen of the Golden City
didn’t like the little mine too well, and, as they are
an outspoken lot, they let the world know their
opinions. The best gold has been got off a big spur
that junctions with the east leg of the saddle, lhe
stranger was picked up in the intermediate level
below 1076 ft. What the bears rely on is that the
leg will get down to its old 3 dwt. value once the
stopes go beyond the influence of the spur. If so, it
will be bad luck for Bendigo, for gold in an outside
show like the Goldfields brings in fresh interests to
a weary crowd of eallpayers.
* * *
Great Poseidon Alluvial (Vic.) mustn’t
be confounded with Poseidon Alluvial. The latter
has a shaft, the former hasn’t. Great Poseidon has
been trying to freeze its way down, but cash hasn t
been too plentiful for the expensive operation. How-
ever, the circle of bores is down, the brine has in it
all the sodium chloride reauired, the coolers and con-
densers are up, and the refrigerating plant is ready to
be shoved along at ton speed. A tangle therefore has
been gradually straightened out, so that, with th_
funds available, the co. should before long freeze the
ground, sink the shaft, and then hey for the lead.
After that, what will be got when the lead
been reached? That point may before then b
answered by work at the Poseidon Alluvial next
door, where, however, the water problem is still a
tough one. # *
Toole, at the New Langi (Vic.), didn’t
get into the gutter from his incline dijive off 'the
top of the rise, but had to go south to let the lead
weep a few tears over his cautious wooing, rrom
the inches of bottom showing at the top laths he
snicked a grab sample of the wash that gave iclwt.
gold. If this be a representative test of the gutter,
the Madame Berry would be a lay figure beside the
New Langi. Bill Toole meanwhile is content to
guess that the gutter should yield at least 2oz. per
fathom. If it does, Victoria is in for another deep-
lead boom, with plenty of spare cash for the flutter.
The weak point is that the gutter where the few inches
were pouched is 1000 ft. wide, and the rise went up
between two specially good bores. If 2oz. dirt hadn t
been got, the sound of the fall of scrip would have
been heard in Ulster. * #
At present there are 200 osmiridium
miners on the west coast of Tasmania, and most of
them are said to be doing very well. The market
price, which was 10s. an ounce for years, has rise
rapidly to £ll. The demand increases faster than
the output, although the biggest specimen of pure
osmiridium in the world, weighing loz. ITdwt.was
only lately discovered. This is in the Passion of
TT "B Silberberg and Co., of Melbourne, along witn
seven" other pieces ranging from ISdwt. These came
from the Bald Hills district (Tas.). Tw ° of ,
smaller specimens have been bought by local
museums, but the prize packet, now at Fairfax and
Roberts’s (Sydney), will probably go to some Euro-
pean collector. Scientific and surgical instruments
are tipped with osmiridium. Likewise fountain pens.
It serves to harden platinum. _ An acid
the metal is used in microscopical apparatus. Reports
as to the metal being used for tipping projectile ,
bearings of flying machines and so forth are obvi
ously absurd. The metal will have to be found in
cwts , rather than grains, before it can be* sho V®“*
of s-uns Tasmania is just now the chief osmin
dium country, the Russian output having slumped
badly. t t

S r ,f P ?rtiSS: PIS-e
wire has been sent by the Association to the Minister
for External Affairs: “You are reported in the JN.I.
Times as - having told a press representative that
the proposed battery for Maranboy is in abeyance,
pending inquiries and reports. Sufficient payable
stone is in sight to keep the battery going for years.
In the opinion of experienced tin-miners, Maranboy

and will do, more to attract genuine workers to the
Territory than any Government farming or settling
policy costing thousands. Your Government, m
delaying mining assistance with the battery, is break-
ing faith with Territory miners.” #
The vaguest prospectus that ever was
issued asks the public to put up the money for 1000
shares in the Islands Gold Dredging and Prospecting
Syndicate. The promoters collar 4000. The vendors
have nothing to sell except a misty belief that on
some unnamed river in some unspecified island there
is gold to be got. Two unmentioned mining engineers
are said to have reported that there is gold there
(not payable gold or gold in quantities, but just
gold), and the promoters merely ask for four-fifths
of the substance that is in the bag—it may be a
pig’ or it may be a delusion. Company-promoters
faith in the confidence of the public is very touching.

Star lady Mary Proctor has slipped her
moorings as far as concerns this portion of
the earth s surface. She landed here from
-LU.D. one day last week, and hurried up the
? a ngwa.y of an outgoing mailboat for
London. Little Miss Proctor may fairly
claim to have so stirred the curiosity of
Maorilanders re other worlds than ours that
ner persistent attacks dragged a solar obser-
vatory out of thin air. Astronomer Ever-
shed reports favorably on the Nelson site,
and considers the Dominion more intent on
sky or star culture than Australia. Our
continent, by the way, has several million
miles of sod to break up before it ploughs
the atmosphere for new gold specks. Besides,
™w a r ia h * s . P ee , n on the astronomical
racket for a fairly long time, and has looked
eiirnesUy through several huge telescopes,
and has discovered little save blank spaces
and an occasional dry, fussy comet. It is
old and tired at the game, for it started
before white men began to settle in Maori-
land. The young fresh isles still find astron-
omy a new thrill, and are hoping to locate
Heaven if they look at the sky long enough.
A rumor that one of the Falk girls, of
this Camlet, has joined a high-toned sister-
hood of Melbourne dressmakers is given here
for what it may be worth. The sisterhood
doesnt care to plunge its scissors into any-
thing in the way of a rag that won’t mount up
to a 17 ‘ .or 18-guinea total. If you go to a
show of its goods—which are worth looking
a t y°u mustn’t linger too long or wear too
intense an expression. Copyists and per-
sons who devour details are not encouraged
on the premises.
Mrs. Rose-Soley, hon. organising sec. of
the Lyceum Club International (Sydney
branch) is. rounding up a welcome to Mrs.
Maude Knight. The lady, who is a visitor
here, is a member of the London Lyceum
Llub. The fatted cream puff is being
slaughtered in honor of the guest.
Glancing through the reports of the Medi-
cal Congress, one notices that our sex—in
the person of Dr. Jane Greig—puts a learned
thumb on the weak place in juvenile morals.
An absence of handkerchief, and conspicuous
carelessness about the necessary tooth-
’ are Puiuts in school hygiene which
Medico Jane brought before the mighty Pill
assembly Though she only got three parts
of an inch of printers’ ink (being a woman),
there is a. whole chapter of common sense in
the Janian suggestion of “handkerchief and
toothbrush drill.” '
Horsehair wigs are being battened down
? n to the perspiring heads of Supreme Court
justices. The long summer holiday term —
about 10 weeks—closed with a snap on
Saturday, and Judges have been toddling
back to town for days as fast as their waist-
measurements and dignity permitted.
Sydney, like Melbourne, is taking the
lango so seriously—you’ve got to take it
seriously, for it is hard labor—that schools
of the cult are beginning to bud in various
suburbs. The first few lessons leave the
victim a limp heap of collapse, with a dis-
jointed feeling about the hips, and the gen-
eral appearance of a rag doll with its stuffing
knocked out. When the patient feels better
he or she settles down to slog steadily at
steps as complicated as the manoeuvres of
an old fleet of battleships in a head wind.
The Meat Strike or beef famine is in very
delicate health. It seems to me that the
flesh insurrection is likely to come to a
sudden end. The proposition of switching
off every meat supply from a great city, and
a tremendously meat-eating city at that
even for three or four days, should cause a
movement of this kind to break down with
the weight of its own carcases. The animal-
hood habit has got the butcher-men just as
it has their customers, and when a confirmed
consumer of chops cuts off his own pro-
vender to spite his face, he presently becomes
angry with himself.
The Nightingales’ referendum on the sub-
ject of raising trained nurses’ fees to three
g r 1 ?oo S fl a eek has . result ed in a majority
of 400 for the uprising of an income which
at present averages £B5 a year. This is the
figure computed by a nursing authority of
*e l°ng experience of Miss Helen Gould.
With the increase a nurse who has spent
tour years_ (at a nominal wage), learning
her profession may hope to gather up £l2O t
year. This allows for the weeks she is liable
to be out of work, or too knocked out to
hump her basket from one sick-bed to
another.
, Sydney nurses complain that they have
had the big dailies against them in this
matter, though they only ask for a wage
that bungs them a little nearer to the
weekly earnings of an unskilled manual lab-
orer. Most nurses are working for their
livmg. They cannot afford to be philan-
thropists any more than the people who pre-
vent disease by digging drains, or the folk
W j , ? ave u ? • rom the germ of ignorance
and the mephitic vapor of misunderstanding
by running morning papers. The legal as-
pect of this claim for a wage in keeping with
the increased cost of living has been sup-
ported by heavyweight legal guns like Min-
ter, Simpson and Co., and Stephen, Jacques
and Stephen.
Minister Carmichael is stamping along the
right track in giving the local artist the
chance of a hanging ground for his wares,
outside the circle of internecine warfare,
ihe prospect of two galleries properly lighted
and so ventilated that the SDectator need not
hght for his breath is alluring. The new
Education Building is to be the Place of
Art. It is hurriedly emerging from the
stay-bodice epoch, and is putting on its best
trock. When the builders add the last
polonaise, part of a top floor will house
the frequent art show.
( Marthe Troly-Curtin. who wrote
I hrynette and London,” jots down in The
Sketch a racy impression of a woman’s
meeting in London. The object was the
betterment of woman’s lot. Mrs. Sidney
Webb, the lecturer, was prodding at the
mistaken kind of education that girls have
always received “which tends to teach them
to make themselves attractive to men.” For
example, the hampered waist of years ago.
and the hampered feet of to-day, are some of
the mistakes mentioned.
Says Marthe, “Does not Mrs. Sidney
Webb know that it is not woman, but man.
who invented and rendered possible the
hampered waist and tight skirt? Men sang
of wasps’ waists, of willowy slenderness,
never of beautiful broad-flanks. The magni-
ficent back of the Yenus de Milo would not
appeal ,to one man in a thousand at the
present time, nor would her strong and noble
neck, her serene face, her restful body, her
large and perfect feet. In truth, man - loves
beauty no longer. It has to be disguised if
not destroyed before it can be acceptable to
him. Why then hint at his superiority?”
Actors’ Carnival is tearing round the
White City to-day (Monday). It is in aid
ot the benevolent funds of the association.
I his gives the mummer who falls by the
way or shares the common lot of growing
old, or the common ill-luck of being knocked
down by a motor, or trampled on by a friend
or a tram, a little cash in hand. Actors are
always m the thick of the charity matindo
tray for other mendicants. Once a year they
pass round the hat for themselves, and the
public in consequence is doing a bit to
help.
H. N. Southwell is putting in the few odd
days with his sister, in Melbourne, before
he catches the Zieten.
The Aerial League of Australia (N.S.W.
section) is going to fill aviator Henry
Hawker with good things at lunch on Feb-
ruary 18. Flying Hawker is the Australian
who won the Daily Mail consolation prize
tor an aerial spin round Britain.
Paling’s are selling a new song of the
softly sentimental ballad order. It is called
Why Should We Part for Ever?”
The words are by T. Schmohl, and the
tuneful melody by Guiseppe Loti, leader of
the vice-regal string band in Adelaide. The
cover is in sympathy with the words. It
illustrates one conclusive reason why they
should not part for ever, by showing Him
in the act of kissing Her eyelashes.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.
“Binnacle”: Yet another Newcastle coal-
laden ship has fled off the surface of human
ken without the slightest trace. That is the
German barque Seestern, which left N. S.
Wales some months ago for an American
port. Whether the dangerous cargo caught
hie or shifted will never be known
until the sea gives up its debris and
its souls. Fire or founder—it is all the
same now, and it is doubtful if it will be
any better later on. The name Seestern is
unlucky. Another steamer of that name, a
beautiful yacht used for German Govern-
ment purposes, left Sydney for German
iapua three years ago. It is still at large
somewhere.
Another horror on the Vic. railways. Re-
cently a girl of 16 was sitting placidly in a
Brighton train when a human severed hand
flew in at the window and splashed in her
face. It had an instant before been the
property of a small girl 10 years old who
had been waving it from the window of an
adjacent carriage. A swinging door on a
passing train did the rest. The younger
girl died an hour later, and perhaps her
luck was the better. The shock to the other
was such that it is doubtful if she will ever
be_ a normal, sane woman. The coroner
said, “Accidental death.” The Railway De-
partment points to regulation Z 1174, which
forbids passengers pushing their extremities
through apertures in the carriages. So far
the swinging door has made no remarks.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, February 16, 1914.
My Dear Yarhie, —
A lord or a duke or something has been
wandering round Melbourne prosaically buy-
ing wool and avoiding the title-worshippers
whose animals grow it. He suffers from
gout (which may or may not be his reason
tor avoiding our alleged Society), and the
other morning he limped into the wool-
buyers’ temple, leaning heavily on a stick
and wearing one foot in a red slipper. He
subsided gratefully into the seat of a young
man who buys for somebody in America,
and when the rightful owner came along
there was a sharp and furious argument, for
there is a very strict unwritten law which
forbids trespassing. However, the gouty one
exhibited the sick foot pathetically, anil the
other withdrew as gracefully as possible.
The swearing-in (or whatever it is) of
the new State Excellency Stanley (his first
name is Lymph, I think) happens on Mon-
day, February 23, when a lot of carefully-
selected unemployed people will be on hand
to watch. There will be a crush, of course.
Even a well-worn and familiar cocked hat
draws a crowd ; but with a brand-new poten-
tate to gaze on the crowd is certain to be
immense and feathered beyond all reason.
The A.N.A. educational exhibition was
opened in the same old way on Saturday
afternoon by the little G.-G., who looked
sick, or perhaps he was just hot and bored,
llis lady and the whole of the house party
were enjoying themselves at breezy Caui-
field, and naturally that’s where he longed
to be also. Chairman Embling made some
superfluous remarks for which Denman later
thanked them all in a hesitating way. He
also thanked them for dragging him away
from Caulfield and making him tired by
showing him fifty thousand things in a heap.
Sometimes he spoke like a man whose very
soul was yawning, and Help Curwen, sitting
close by in a uniform which was far too
tightly laced with gold braid, had no sugges-
tions to make. But his Ex. still struggled
along gamely, even when, for want of some-
thing else to say, he had to drag at his fin-
gers and confess that he “wasn’t much good
at education.” The audience was given to
understand that he had the ruins of his edu-
cation packed away somewhere at home, but
it really wasn’t worth calling round to in-
spect it. Madden, sitting alongside, looked
sympathetic, and seemed to be wondering
how any human being could be at such a
loss for mere words. John himself always
has a plate of buttered ones ready. Gaud
Mayor Hennessy smiled Perpetually, and a
handful of women (one woman is a handful
for most men) tiptoed in in time to drink
George V.’s health in lukewarm gingerale,
while a pretty but belated waitress rushed
towards them with ice. The Creswell dame,
drooping wearily in black, after her hard
work in the Arts and Crafts gallery, was
accompanied by the usually icebergy' Mary
Chomley, who seemed to have been melted
by the heat. Chairman Emblitig’s wife was
a fair, cool vision in white muslin, and three
Embling children sat properly with their
nurse and refrained from violence or fidget-
ing. The thing lasted a fearful time, and
it is doubtful if the poor G.-G. even saw the
last race.
Caulfield races, on Saturday last, were
not officially on the Best People’s honor list,
but the dresses and plug hats turned up
m battalions and made it one of the fashion-
able events of the young year. The vice-
regal enthusiasm is good for the horse-
racing game, and snobs hurry along to all
sorts of minor meetings. The Denman
dame, whose little G.-G. was sweltering
dutifuHy at the A.N.A. show, deserted the
official crate most of the afternoon and
walked about the paddock with a cool touch
ot moss green in her costume and a train of
devoted Gentleman Helps. Burnham, how-
ever, was free to roam with Margaret Niall
and drink squashes with her in the shade.
(Comedian Will Whitburn used to sing a
mock sentimental song which began: “The
prettiest girl that ever I saw was sucking cider
through a straw.” I remembered it on Satur-
day). There was such a rush on cool, teetotal
drinks that the outcast flat had to be appealed
to for extra lemons. Sep Miller’s horse
Laheen bolted riderless almost round the
course, and when brought back started off as
fresh as ever and won his race. Mrs. Sep
simply danced with delight, and, as she is
young and a good dresser, the performance
was very popular with those who saw it.
Her dancing costume was topped by a simple
Panama hat, with schoolgirl tails of black
velvet hanging down the back. Mrs. Alan
Lurrie and Mrs. Jim Purves exchanged con-
fidences in a shady corner of the stand. The
wife of Dr. Joyce looked like a pink ice-
cream—-and I was very grateful to her for it.
i simply hated another woman who looked
like a round of beef. She looked terrible be-
side a woman friend garbed in cool white,
who closely resembled a dish of junket. Then
there-was a raspberry-tart girl—but enough !
I an Dango Macka.v went down to the bird-
cage, and natted Radnor, her husband’s horse,
on its distinguished nose, but the nag
couldn t manage to win even with that bit of

wedding of the month—the Burnham-Niall
affair. I hen, before Society has had time

Plump and serious-minded Ivy Clarke is
taking a kind of hereditary interest in the
City Newsboys, of whose society the late
Janet Mrs. Clarke was one of the first
presidents. During the week, Ivy “shouted”
the youngsters a picnic to Rye, whither thev
journeyed by the Lady Loch. A luncheon
to the committee and guests was given on
board. Edith Onians, the secretary of the
society, for whom the slightly-rolling deep
hath no charms, made the trip to Rye by
motor with Mrs. John Murray. This lady’s
more completely exhausted half could not
come owing to having overslept himself or
something.
Civilisation’s latest, the afternoon Tango
lea at half a crown a head, reached us at
last on Ihursday, and the struggle to get in
was memorable. Curiosity is the best draw
ot all. A heated management declined to
hold seats after curtain-rise, and the stam-
pede ot the Great Unbooked into the vacant
places made the ushers look like dazed tally-
men at shearing time. The dilatory moto-
cracy undulated up the stairs later, only to
lind their seats crammed full of staring
strangers. It was no use protesting either.
i r *u w ? s no shifting the claim-jumpers,
ancl the late arrivals had either to retreat
downstairs or stand. The sensational item
or the afternoon was the parade of girls in
undergear constructed mostlv of silk, mull-
muslin and Valenciennes. When it came to
tlie top-dressing, the new clothes were seen
to be bunchy. A Tango dress needs width
and heaps of gathers, but tulle and chiffon
(both perishable stuffs) are the only possible
coverings one can treat so and stili be fash-
ionably underclad. Tea and ices were rushed
round the auditorium by flustered waitresses
in the intervals, while a bald individual with
massive trousers- announced the new items.
Ihe demonstrating Tango maiden was tall,
and shewed off some beautiful garments. A
black and white arrangement was practically
nothing but fringe and a wisp of tulle. Then
she showed a neat tan tailor-made, out of
which she thrust one silk-stockinged limb bv
means of a slit almost waist-high. Black tights
were glimpsed underneath. The house sat
and_ stewed patiently through the afternoon,
hoping all the time for Something Worse

bourne a few more of the alleged 72 steps.

The Palais de Danse is steadily gaining
popukrity amongst the Very Best People
who followed like sheep on the early and
daring heels of the Barttelots and Help
Burnham. They dance in gloves now!”
murmured a recent female spectator, wlio
was too absorbed to choose her words care-
tuily. Ihese evenings there is just the
faintest hint of tango in the two-step, and it
goes unchecked by the management, which at
first had to be more careful than the average
suburban vestry. The Best are now hoping
for the Worst—though the blessed dance has
been so carefully conventionalised that there
is hardly any Worst in most versions of it
Every evening small expeditionary parties
from ioorak arrive to praise the floor, gyrate
and see how much further the regulations
have relaxed.

Walter Hackett, one of Adelaide’s rich men, died
a day or two ago, aged 87. He made his money out
ot seeds and flowers—had a nursery at Marrvattville
which is built all round nowadays, and a quiet sea-
side home with a half-mile avenue of big gums at
Brighton. There are sons to carry on the business ;
but as long as he could turn over a ledger or value
a sample of grain—which was until the other day—
the silent old man kept his grip on the affairs of a
firm which is one of the ancient institutions of South
Aus.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

“.Ssculapius”: In ancient Greece they used to
erect temples over natural springs and give to each
a titular diety. We moderns are no less appreciative
of the healthful virtues of a mineral water —only our
methods differ somewhat. For instance, up at the
famous Helidon Spring, the water is secured just as
it bubbles up from the earth, and hermetically sealed
until the process of aeration and bottling is com-
plete. You don’t need a Greek fineness of palate to
appreciate the cool charm of Helidon Spa—it has
the same delicious refreshingness to Billjim as to the
Earl of Hopetoun. As a blender with wines and
spirits, too, it gives just that added snap and
sparkle that counts so much in the sum of pleasant-
ness. You’ll be wise to always make it a practice to
Figaro : Read a par. here the other week about
Nature planting the giant stinging nettle in N.Q.,
but close beside it the tree whose leaves serve as an
antidote to its sting. This provision of Nature is
in no way unique, for, just as she curses us with
indigestion so she blesses us with Helidon Spa.
After dining out you’ll have no next morning head-
ache if you take a bottle of “Helidon” before turning
ln this delicious mineral water is Nature’s gift to
those who have dined not wisely but too well. A single
glass will dissolve the undigested food and at the
same time ensure a perfect night’s rest. Quite apart
from its health aspect, “Helidon” greatly enhances
the flavor of wines and spirits—so truly the way of
wisdom is always to
SPLIT IT WITH
HELIDON.

Vigilans : I see another authority, with
MD. after his name, has arisen to remark that a
man to make a do of things in the Northern Terri-
tory must be constitutionally like a procession of
lions and elephants ; he must be in perfect health ; his
habits must be tremendous; he must be an absolute
devil on morality ; and he must be so temperate that
he eats nothing and drinks less, and never sits down.
He must, in short, be a picked man. To which I
say bandicoots.” I know a dozen men who went
to the North because they could not live in the
South. Men with slight consumption, asthma, bron-
chitis and weak chests. Are they dead? Not much
They are very alive. As for drink, there is more
square gin consumed up there for the population
than anywhere else in the universe. Perfect habits!
Men get wet through three or four times a day and
dry in the sun. A bark humpy is the usual abode or
a roughly-pitched fly. Old Blaire lived years under
a bark roof with no sides. Personally, I never had
better health than during my four years wandering
in the North. I rarely slept in a bed ; most of the
time I lay on the hard ground with a saddle for a
pillow. My habits were quite imperfect, but they
made me strong and healthy. The wandering, hard
life of the North changed me from a city dude to a
man. I even became a bloke. I walked from Derby
to Wyndham. Got bushed twice; was three days
without water and five without food. I walked up
the coast from Broome to Beagle Bay with only a
singlet and dungarees on me; I must have swam 30
creeks, was always more or less wet, and was half-
devoured by mosquitoes. I rode by myself from Pine
Creek to Charleville (Queensland) with two horses.
I slept anywhere, lived hard on johnnie-cakes, salt-
junk and post-and-rail tea. Drank any kind of out-
back poison when it was there. Was moral occa-
sionally. And I’m still going strong.

“T.H.” : Somebody was asking about black-
fcHows ovens recently. Back in the old days along
the Murray and Loddon rivers, where for miles no
stones could be found, our primitive brother cooked
his rations thuswise: a hole was made in the ground •
it was about 9ft. in circumference and about 9in’
b!n P ’ W 'L u loP J ng Sides ‘ Then a su PP J y mud
balls, each about as big as a man’s fist, was prepared,
sun-dned and well heated on a fire. These were
rolled into the excavation until it was paved. Upon
them were laid ’possum, fish, or whatever was in
season, and covered with a layer of grass, a layer of
leaves and a layer of hot ashes. I’ve never seen
better results from a white man’s oven. The mud
balls are used only once, and I have seen piles of
them Bft. high along the rivers mentioned.
* * * .
A suggestion that a white man trained to
the game can beat any black-tracker has called out
a host of contradictions in Adelaide. Captain
White, the naturalist, says that, on his recent
expedition, the blackboys tracked camels over the
gibber country, where the stones for miles are
pressed down like mosaic, and the spongy foot of
the camel passed over it without making the least
impression.” As for tracking ants—an idea which
had been represented as comic—White found that his
followers accepted the job as a matter of course
when he told them he wanted different breeds
of the insect. South, the S. Aus. Protector of Abor-
igines, was formerly a trooper in charge of black
trackers, and hands them a big testimonial. They
have given an escaped prisoner 13 days’ start, and
then run him down; even his changes of horses
were at once clear to them. They have followed
hoofmarks through a perfect maze made by a mob.
Other blackfellows gave them the most trouble.
They can track the scuttling lizard and describe it
before they see it; in fact, South thinks some of
the most brilliant work is done by piccaninnies,
when they are out after food. From 25 to 30 is
however,, the best age, as a rule, and the real
tracker is the chap from Outside, who distrusts even
Alice Springs as a gay metropolis, and gets uneasy
when asked to stay anywhere for a month.

Masarcus : Don’t know who started this
slow-train competition, but I reckon I’ll finish it
when I nominate the Northern Murchison line
(W.A.). From Midland Junction to Geraldton,
travelling by the steam hearse is bad enough, but
from Geraldton on to Meekatharra the boiler fire is
put out and the train is drawn by cows and lame
dogs. Over the distance of 320 miles the Flying
Backslider is scheduled to rage in about 21 hours;
but mostly it limps in between four and eight hours
late. Nevertheless, there are some compensations
to the passenger. Supposing, for instance, the train
has pulled up at some small
station on which there is no
refreshment booth. All the
thirsty one has to do is to
ask the engine-driver to wait
half an hour while he mean-
ders over to the hotel. That
is done at Nannine nearly
every trip. Then again, if the
traveller wants a shot at a
wild turkey along the track,
he borrows a rifle from the
guard, and the procession is
suspended while he stalks his
prey. Sometimes the engineer
joins the hunt. Only once has
the Meekatharra - Geraldton
train been known to be on
time. That was when the
District Superintendent made
a trip. On that occasion resi-
dents of the various towns
along the line were seriously
inconvenienced. They had got
into the habit of setting their
clocks by the arrival of the train, generally reckon-
ing on her being three hours late. Next morning
many of them got up at four o’clock under the
impression it was seven or thereabouts.

Some gets their joy in chemists’ shops, but none o’
that for me!
I don’t want bottles full o’ muck, like wot you
gets at sea 1 *
It don’t do me no good to take a mug o’ rotten tack
An feel as if I’d et a storm! No, when I’m feelin’’
slack
An’ orf me grub, I goes along to where there’s lig-ht
an’ cheer, 6
An’ gets me reglar medicine—a pot o’ foamin’ beer!
You can’t stuff me with, pills and things that costs
a bob an’ more,
An’ isn’t fit ter feed to pigs! There’s lots o’ pubs
ashore,
An’ ev’ry pub ’as beer enough t’ cure th’ blessed
town.
When I feels that I’m not meself, I puts a few
pints down.
When I’m like that, th’ blamed old beer soaks in an’
makes me well;
An’ when I’m right, it gets ter work an’ makes me
feel like H —l!
These chemists blokes will never give ver something
yer can swill,
No matter ’ow yer feel—their muck is guaranteed

An’ when yer well, yer chuck it out! That’s w’y I

Th chemists’ tack is only fit ter give away at sea !

BEALE PIANOS.
The facff that to-day we are the largest piano firm doing business in the Commonwealth is diie solely to the
quality of our instruments.
No effort has been spared in the manufacture of Beale Pianos to reach perfection of tone and ensure
durability. To-day our instruments stand absolutely unrivalled in those respects.
Beale Pianos are specially designed and made to meet all the exigencies of the Australian climate. They
embody every improvement known in piano construction, and are made under the direct supervision of an expert who
has made scientific piano manufacture a life study. In addition, they possess special features which you cannot get in
other pianos ; such as our permanent steel tuning-system, hexagonal bass-strings, patent cast-in hitch-pins, and our
patent method of building up the casework.
The Beale Permanent Steel Tuning System renders what was hitherto the weakest part of a piano the strongest.
In every Beale Piano the wrest-plank is made of solid iron. The wrest-plank is that part of the piano into which are
driven the tuning pins to which are attached the wires (or strings ). In every impoited piano the wrest-plank is
made of wood, which has to bear the strain (about 20 tons) of all the wires when the piano is in tune. So that in every
piano that has a wooden wrest-plank there is a probability of the wrest-plank splitting and the piano becoming untunable
in consequence. And in every piano but the Beale the wrest-plank is made of wood.
In all Beale Pianos the wrest-plank is solid iron, a part of the iron frame itself.
Write for our special booklets explaining this matter in detail.
We have recently put on the market the Beale Player-Piano. The Beale Player-Piano possesses the finest
mechanism it is possible to produce, fitted in a Beale high-grade piano of handsome appearance. The artistic possi-
bilities of the interpretation of a roll on the Beale Player-Piano are limited only by the artistic temperament of the
performer. The mechanism is so delicately regulated and every control so perfecff that it is easier to play than any other
piano-player.
FREE LIFE INSURANCE : We supply, when requested, a Free Life Insurance Policy, without any charge
whatever, to buyers of Beale Pianos. This covers the life of the customer during the course of hire-purchase, and
insures a valuable and saleable asset to the family without further payment in case of bereavement. The Policy is a
purely free gift from Beale and Co., Ltd., so that no other person may claim under it than the person named by the
customer when accepted by us.
All Beale Pianos carry our written guarantee for Twenty-five Years.
A Beale Piano is lower in price, quality for quality, than any other piano you can buy, and in after cost it is
also cheaper, because it requires far less tuning and less frequent expert attention in every way.
PARTS : Should you by accident damage your Beale Piano we can always replace any injured or broken
parts at a very low cost to you, because we are the actual makers, with our fadlory facilities here in Australia. But if
you buy an imported piano, and it gets damaged, you will find it expensive to repair, because the agents have no factory
facilities here and they do not carry stocks of spare parts.

“Te Babarantiko” : I can endorse “Lagani
Namo’s” assertion (B. 29/l/’l4) that a 20ft. dive
is not much to skite about. All through the Islands
you find excellent divers among the natives. On the
phosphate islands in the Central Pacific, where there
is a good deal of rule-of-thumb work about the
moorings, I have had natives go down six fathoms—
and work, too. Four fathoms is considered a mod-
erate depth to work in. And I have known white
men beat the niggers at the game. The native diver
takes no header to gain impetus—he just describes
a semicircle on the surface, after swallowing a huge
gulp of air, pinches his nostrils between two fingers,
and works his way down with powerful strokes of
arms and legs. Some claim to be able to go down
10 fathoms, but I have never seen it done; a good
diver is too valuable for that kind of thing, anyway.
The. deep-diving Kanaka always becomes hard of
hearing and suffers from headaches. He also insists
that it is easier to dive in shallow water than to
dive to the same depth in very deep water —-which
seems feasible enough. On the Rio Santos, South
America, I have seen Indians go down along a bamboo
pole stuck into the mud in peasoup water 30ft. deep,
and grope about the bottom for lumps of coal or
anything else dropped from vessels discharging cargo.
The diver who is on this lay takes a little basket,
which is hauled up by his mate and taken down
again when the diver has had his blow. About the
Malacca Straits, and well into the Malay Archi-
pelago, you can contract with naked divers to scrape
the marine growth off your vessel’s bottom, and they
make a good job of it, too.
For thousands of years mankind has allowed its
bread to become stale regarding staleness as unavoid-
able. It now appears however that staleness is due
to exposing bread to varying temperatures. Dr.
Katz’s experiments (see Scientific Australian) show
that if stored at 60 degrees Cent, bread will keep
new for 40 hours while at 30 or 40 it becomes only
"half stale.” He recommends the keeping of new
bread at 50 degrees. An exciting problem, day
baking, may thus be solved. Phillips Ormonde & Co.,
17 Queen Street, Melbourne.*

“Hamel’’': Australian snake-expert Fox is
looking for new worms to conquer, and his search
has taken him to India, where the deadly cobra lives.
While in the Malay Settlements Fox is alleged to
have had 1 a battle With a cobra, and to have been
bitten by the animal; but his “cure” is said to
have proved efficacious. Anyhow, he is still alive.
‘lt speaks a great deal for his confidence in his anti-
dote that he is willing to tackle a proposition like
the cobra; and if there isn’t a dead Fox in India
shortly, he is. likely to get more advertisement,
revenue and profit in a few weeks than he secured
in Australia in many years.

“Inabit”: “Sarto” (B. 15/1/’l4), having
been so kind as to tell “Kodak” the name of the
strange bird he saw, will perhaps oblige
me in the same way. I have only seen
my bird twice, and each time it was late
at night. I first met it on a lonely road.
It was perched on the bough of a tree,
using a crosscut saw. I applauded its
efforts, whereupon it pressed a button
and the ship lit up with red and green
lights. Rockets went up at intervals.
Pressing another button the squadron of
giraffes withered away altogether, and the
sky lighted up. Fiery dragons, angels
with flaming swords, black-winged imps,
green scorpions and a host of mis-shapen
things danced the stately minuet. One
couple, a fantod and a winged snake,
started the Tango ; but a policeman with
white whiskers grabbed them, and they
all went into the night in a heap,
dragging the furniture with them. Bill
Jones was with me at the time. When I
next saw the bird I was at Murphy’s,
and it dew out from Hogan’s pint. It
hung in the air for about a second ; then
with a soar it flew on to the beer-pump
damsel’s head, where it spat out blue
sparks, which turned into eggs and
hatched out millions of centipedes.
Each centipede had a vaccination mark on
its left front leg. As I know “Sarto” is interested
in these matters, I’ve bought a trap and will send
the bird along as soon as I’ve caught it. Only let
me have it back, as I want it to show to Bill Jones.
“Jimmie Pannikin”: Please let me butt
into this cackle about the busbman’s literary appe-
tite. I am on “Gouger’s” side when he asserts that
it is utter rubbish to call the bushman “a literary
degenerate.” Many of the bushman’s creaking and
croaking critics suffer from fatty degeneration of
the intellect. These critics creak because their mental
machinery wants oiling with the lubricant of com-
mon-sense. They croak because, like frogs, they live
in the swamps of swill and swipes. Sane and sober
men do not make such absolutely untenable charges.
It was an Outback bushman whom I first heard use
the ruthlessly right phrase: “The piffle of Charlie
Garbage.” It ought to be sold in sealed tins, for
obvious reasons. I have often said and written
that the majority of even the old-age pensioners
camped on the banks of the Darling prefer good
reading matter and baccy to firewater and chain-
lightning as gifts. The number of outbackers who
read the “Red Page” regularly and digestively aston-
ished me. I’d like to shake “Gouger” by the hand.
Some of the other writers in this controversy I would
cheerfully shake by the neck.
* * * *
“Pick”: If “Tarago” (B. 22/1/ i 4) under-
stands what happens when a cyclist is following a
pacemaker, he should also know why a gale invari-
ablj' follows a bush fire instead of setting in towards
it from all points of the compass. Until the flare
has assumed considerable proportions, sufficient local
heat to cause an inset of colder air is not created ;
but, meantime, driven onward by any" • prevailing
breeze, or led by the upward slope of the country,
the fire leaps forward, driving the atmosphere with
it, and thus causing an ever-increasing suction from
behind. Moreover, any inset of colder air would
tumble in behind, inasmuch as ahead of the disturb-
ance things are only beginning to warm up, while
rearward the blazing debris cooks the surrounding
atmosphere to a cinder. In connection with bush
fires, there is one thing, however, that puzzles me.
How do insects and small bush creatures learn of
the coming trouble? I have seen droves of them
scrambling away from a coming fire, when, to me,
the only indication of its approach was a smoke
rising over the range.
“5.11. G.”: Mudgee’s troubles with wom-
bats (B. 4/12/’l3) are not over yet. When the
lOs.-a-head bonus was offered, 1500 wombat corpses
were presented. The Pastures Protection Board
emptied its cashbox and still owed £5lO. It tried
to compromise at 6s. a head, but the offer was treated
with shouts of derision, and the Board had to take
up another hole in its belt. The rabbit-inspector
was sacked, stock-inspectors’ wages were reduced by
£3O a year and clerks’ by £25, and the directors
themselves had their own fees cut down by one-half ;
at the same time, rates on stock were jumped up
from 2s. Id. per 100 sheep to 3s. 6d. and from lid.
to 3d. a head for larger stock. But The Bulletin
was out when it suggested that in the event of a
plague of tigers the Board would scalp anyone who
suggested a bonus of id. per head ; for when it had
finished pruning everyone’s salary, it decided to give
a £l-a-head bonus for dingoes, because they were
getting very plentiful. Some people are whales for
trouble.
“Gordon G.”: The “true adventures” of
one Matthew Tracey, of the Amurrikan Fleet, as pub-
lished in H’Yark:—
On my arrival I was presented by Cardinal Moran
to one of the grandest men in Australia, Timothy
McGrath, of Ballarat, who has grown up with the
country, and raised millions of sheep, and is worth
a million dollars. Mr. McGrath has nary a son to
leave his acres of sheep ranch at Ballarat to, and he
is very anxious that I should marry his only daughter,
and become heir to his millions. I said I would have
to finish my period of service with Uncle Sam, and
that then I would go back to the sheep ranch at
Ballarat and claim my lovely bride.
The story is profusely illustrated with pictures show-
ing Timothy, his town house, his ranch and his
emus and kangaroos. Tracey is expected here shortly
to claim all these things—unless, of course, his
own country’s police need him.
“Cleveland”: How’s this for the casual
stakes? I was loafing in a deck-chair on board of
an East-bound ship which was lying. in Townsville
(N.Q.) harbor, when a man hove in sight who had
obviously looked on the wine when it was brimming.
Presently he hailed one of the wharfhands: “Shay,
d’remember two coves I was with —no, I mean was
with me?” The lumper looked stonily at the man
of beer, who proceeded: “The little fat bloke (hie)
is the bloke I mean,” he said. “Wot about him,
anyhow?” asked the other. The unstable one col-
lected his ideas. “He’s in the flaming water; fell
over the blitherin’ wharf, and er shark’ll get ’im,
sure. Gotta bloke lars’ week, ’n’ Bill’l make two,
an’ then there’ll be another one soon. Always goes
in threes,” he concluded prophetically. The toiler
then asked where the other pal was. “ ’E’s sittin’
on the wharf watchin’ Bill duckin’ round er pile,”
said the prophet. “Rotten things, piles; all over
barnacles an’ copper. Bill’l soon go now. Wouldn’t
be s’prised if er shark ’ad ’im before I get back.”
He turned and stumbled cursefully up the jetty. Five
minutes he was back again, hurrying excitedly.
“Wot’d I tell you erbout three ’aving to go?” he
yelled triumphantly. “Jim’s over the side now.
’E was leanin’ over to lissen to wot Bill was bawlin’
erbout, an’ fell in ’imself. ’E makes the three
orlright. Only for that cove lars’ week goin’ over,
Bill an’ Jim’d be ’ere now.” I waited to see if
the wharf operator would become interested, but he
didn’t; so I hurriedly investigated. It was even as
the little boozer had stated, Bill and Jim were
hanging on to a stringer, hollering alternately for
pledge-cards and a rope.
“Billabong”: A Victorian judge has given
a decision on a question which affects the cocky all
over Australia. Two Vic. farmers lived on opposite
sides of a deep creek. One drained cows and the
other grew corn. A cow, swimming across the creek,
got into the crop, and etc. The corngrower alleged
trespass, and sued for damages. Defendant raised
the point that the creek frontage was not fenced, and
that damages could only be claimed for trespass on
enclosed land. The plaintiff then took a hand and
argued that a creek was a fence according to the
law made and provided, and a County Court Judge
upheld the contention. It appears that the Eng-
lish Fencing Acts, from which the Victorian Acts are
copied, define a fence to include a ditch, stream or
watercourse. The only chance defendant has of getting
even is to lie in wait to catch the crops crossing the
creek to devour the cows. Then the agriculturist
will wish he had never been born.
“S.T.S.”: I should like to think that
“Camp” really found a corpse in a buggy, as he
describes in B. l/l/’lt, and tipped it out, taking its
place between the two live occupants—and so on.
But digging up bodies for dissection purposes sounds
much more like England 70 years back than any
“bush township” within “Camp’s” recollection. Any-
way, it was one of the pet stories of Sketchley (real
name Rose), author of the immensely popular “Mrs.
Brown.” He was in Australia in the early ’Bo’s, and
it was then I heard the yarn. He finished it very
neatly, too. The grave-robbers drove on, with the
supposed corpse squeezed between them, till finally
a dreadful suspicion became a certainty. “ ‘Bill!’
‘What is it?’ ‘Bill, he’s —warm!’ ‘Warm,’ I said,
in a hollow voice; ‘you'd be warm if you’d been in
Hell as long as I have!’ Then they gave a yell and
tumbled out on either side and bolted, and I was
left with a very nice horse and trap for my trouble.”
“Wandi”: A conundrum often propounded
in camp and hut is why kangaroos and wallabies do
not inter-breed, when the only generic distinction
between them is that the nostril interspace is naked
in the kangaroo and hairy in the wallaby. The
bushman, as a rule, can tell one from the other at
a glance without looking to see if it has hair on
its nose. This is largely due to his knowledge of
their haunts. Asked to explain the difference, h 4
will usually refer to the relative sizes of the animals,
and a miscroscopic distinction in the shape of the
ears. But place a big wallaby in open country, and
a small kangaroo in a scrub, and without some dis-
tinctive mark to guide him, the bushman will be
hopelessly mixed. Size, of course, is no criterion;
but taking the common species, a full-grown wallaby
is only the size of a half-grown kangaroo. Some of
the jerboaroos are just as difficult to distinguish in
the bush from some of the bandicoots. Generally
speaking, the jerboaroos have short, rounded ears,
short noses and white bellies ; whereas the bandicoot
has long ears and an elongated snout. The smallest
marsupial that hops is the jerboa pouched mouse,
which was a body length of about three inches and a
five-inch tail, with a dark brush. Between this mite and
the grey boomer there are more living grades and
variations than there are inches between their
respective sizes.
LIQUID MEASURE.
“Say, Boss, how far am I from Boolabillalong?”
“Oh, about two pubs, I think!”
“Tarago”: “Gip” (B. 8/l/’l4) asks:
“Are foxes snake exterminators?” I can’t say
whether Reynard deals with the enemy of man first-
hand ; but I can offer circumstantial evidence that
he dines off it. I have happened on a number of
his habitations with the front garden strewn with
the remains of defunct reptiles. The imported gent.
may make the kill, but I doubt it. For the matter
of that, very few of the native or imported inhabi-
tants of the bush kill snakes either for recreation
or for other purposes, if you except the common tike.
As for “snakes growing beautifully less where foxes
are thick,” I dunno; but if it came to a question
of extermination, my lucre would go on the snake.
He is more numerous, and holds the punch that outs,
whereas Reynard must miss sometimes.
