[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SOO-NAN had lost more than he could
afford, and the last shreds of his temper
and self-control were following his van-
ished taels. So, when a strange, dirty, long-
taloned hand shot out and took to itself Soo-
nan’s only winning stake and gains, the
despoiled owner went the Chinese equivalent
for Baresark.
He followed up a whistling Cantonese
curse with a carved stool, a broken-handled
knife, the heavy brass dice-box, and sundries.
The 21b. of dice-box took the unknown in the
small of the back and he collapsed, arms and
pigtail outspread, with his head nuzzling into
the feet of the great carven Joss of Chance.
He was quite dead when they turned him
over. An enlarged spleen and a flying lump
of metal will “do for” most ’rickshaw coolies.
The indescribable, meaningless chatter that
every low-class coolie raises on every possible
occasion died down as Soo-nan was hustled
into the street. A barefooted policeman, in
a little, conical, plaited straw helmet,
prodded him through Canton’s four-foot-wide
streets to the Yamen.
For the consideration of two dollars from
the prisoner—and the possibilities of a big
“squeeze” from the old man—the straw-
helmeted policeman sauntered to the shop of
Soo-wat, a rattan worker on the Bund, and
the father of Soo-nan.
Here, in many words, he told of the fate
of Soo-nan, and enlarged on the chance of
him being one of the interested parties in the
next execution.
Soo-wat thought regretfully of the many
pleasant Sundays he had spent with his son
by the river, watching the public executions
in the old Tan Tock square. How they had
shouted as the heads rolled and jumped!
How they had laughed when the heavy, two-
handed sword made a misshit, and clove a
skull instead of taking the countenance off
clean at the neck.
And it was entertaining and amusing to
note how the expression changed on each
lined face as the swinging sword came nearer
and nearer! But to see his own son one of
that kneeling, doomed line—he must buy a
substitute quickly.
Old Soo-wat sighed as he drew the savings
of ten years from their hiding-place in the
well under the courtyard, and hurried to the
mandarin at the Yamen.
That was a big “squeeze.”
He told the tearful Soon-nan the news
through the grimy bars, and hobbled on his
way. From the reeking, stinking prison it is
but a step to the Kow Chee Hock —the guild
whose members sell themselves for punish-
ment or death.
Soo-wat saw Ah Young. To be exact, he
saw as much of the original Ah Young as
was publishable.
Ah Young had crawled in from the lower
provinces to die in Canton City: but he
would not take less than 200 taels for dying
in four days at the end of a two-handed
sword.
Soo-wat, with tears in his voice, pointed
out that 200 taels was robbery when asked
by a leper that would most probably drop
to pieces in a month. He begged him to
consider the easy, quick finish in one light-
ning stroke: he implored him to think of the
luxury to be bought with —say, 70 taels.
They compromised on 150, paid into Ah
Young’s half a hand, in the presence of
many witnesses.
Ah Young lived at the rate of a thousand
a year—for three days. He bought himself
sham-su, and was gloriously drunk for 12
hours; he bought opium, pork—and more
sham-su.
Never did Chinaman have such a heaven
on earth.
* * * *
Three days in the Yamen had not im-
proved Soo-nan. His almond eyes were red
and sunken. The prison slime marked his
yellow cheeks. The cold, chill dawn of the
fourth day found him yammering at the
bars.
His proxy was due.
An hour passed, another dragged.
He screamed and grovelled in the filth on
the floor as he saw the hopeless look on his
father’s face when the old man approached.
Soo-wat yelled the news at him above the
din. Ah Young had been knifed in an opium
den overnight!
Cocos.
Oimai” : The capture of the whole gang
of Papuans who murdered and probably ate
prospector Warner a few months ago con-
stitutes a record for these affairs in Papua.
The whole job of going and coming between
Samarai and the scene of the murder occu-
pied only two months. The country is ter-
ribly wild and mountainous, and to capture
one lot the R.M. and his police had to ci’oss
the backbone of the island. Considering the
people had never seen “the Government”
before and that there were no “friendlies”
to aid, it was a fine feat. But, then, the
R.M. in question was not a junior clerk from
Melbourne.
“Jay”: The more I see of N. S. Wales Law
the less I like the animal. It is a fool and a
lingering ass at that. Regarded as a dilatory
and impracticable neddy it is the limit. It
has no regard for time. It never heard of
time, or eternity either. A case is impend-
ing, and the parties have to attend the court
and hang round for, perhaps, several days
before their turn is reached. That is bad
enough when there is something to be argued.
But in one case in which I was involved a
settlement had been arrived at, and two
days running I had to travel 30 miles and
waste the best part of a day, to get the
wretched thing scraped off the list. I would
get up and ask that the thing be struck
out, and the other bloke would say he con-
sented, and then the Judge would explain
that we were speaking out of our turn. We
nearly got committed for contempt or dis-
temper or something. Now imagine wasting
the time of a Judge over such a trifle, to say
nothing of my time. The Judge is paid
£l5OO per annum, and 30s. per day exes to
do that sort of work. Why could we not
have walked into the office of the Registrar,
and fixed the thing up in five minutes?
“Harrison O.”: I prophesy that something
sudden will one of these nights happen to
the policeman who, out of deference to
pious clamor, has lately been told off for
searchlight duty in the Fitzroy (Melb.)
Gardens. This forerunner of destiny wan-
ders around, armed with a lantern, seeking
whom he may ascertain. On the soft lawns
even the megatherium-like feet of the Law
cannot be heard, and their owner stirs up
bushes and flashes his lantern suddenly on
to the poet and his poetess, scrutinising them
at length, until satisfied that they are in no
was infringing the Statute of Limitations,
the Hackney Carriages Act, or any other old
regulation. About the only “unwritten
law” recognised by the Australian is the
right of a free-born citizen to slay the quiet-
footed, slow and sudden stranger who looks
round bushes in the parks. In fact, there is
a sort of impression abroad that unless the
slayer boasts of the deed in a pub or a
crowded railway carriage the police will not
race themselves to death in their efforts to
bring him before the Court of Petty Sessions.
But a detective looks like anybody else in a
night surprise, and poets are a hasty crowd
who shoot without consideration. So I don’t
want to be that officer.
From Robert Brothers, war correspondent:
This is about a battle of big hats, and the foolish-
ness of Sydney tram seats, which are placed back to
back without making provision for big hats. When
we sat down the big hat in which I am interested
accidentally scraped against the big hat of the lady
who sat in the seat exactly behind her. The lady in
the back seat was a lady, so she did not turn; but,
being much annoyed, and not intending that her hat
should be scraped by any other big hat in creation,
she gave her head a jerk so as to catch the wearer
of the other big hat on the neck. Now, when the
wearer of a big hat comes into conflict with the
wearer of another big hat, she does not need to turn
to know that the gage of war has been thrown
nothine T w t’L™ u” 'y hl< ; h tfw interestcd sf lcl
notning:, out 1 knew by the look that came into her
eyes that she had accepted the challenge, and in-
stantly there began such a scraping of hats that you
could, hear it some seats away when the tram was
not moving. But no one knew what an earnest con-
teS r t hat , s w f s b . ei f£ waged.
belone-ed 8 tn 6 ™l y . lnte | ested - Neither of the hats
neionged to me, but I have a pecuniary interest in
one of them. The idea on both sides was to get at
the back of the neck, and, failing that, to do as much
damage as possible to the garnishing of the glorified
lids. No regular rules were followed, except that
both contestants refrained from looking round. Apart
trom a hard look in each pair of eyes, each remained
pleasantly ladylike. The lady at the back wore a
lacy halo with several big roses in it. My lady wore
a black tulley affair with little dog roses round the
brim. It was surprising how one read the intention
of the other, and avoided each jab. Like the birds in
a cockfight, each hat strove to get on top and lacer-
ate the plumage of the other. Now and again a rip
would sound above the creaking of the “founda-
tions,’’ and one knew that a deadly slash had been
driven home. I waited in dread for either to turn
and frigidly begin a warfare of words, but neither
did so. For five streets the wrecking of the big
hats went on. I was beginning to get frightened at
the damage that was being done, till at length the
big hat in the other compartment rose and alighted
with a damaged look about it, and went off without a
glance at what was under the other hat. We rode

retrimmed, anyway.”
Though the morning papers of Sydney are
bitterly hostile to any proposal to let other
deep-water harbors in the State be used for

great applause when Britain repudiates the

The Labor proposal to flood the N.S.W.
Legislative Council, with the object of pre-
sently obliterating it, recalls a former at-
tempt to swamp it in order to pass the
Robertson Land Bill. John Robertson had
resigned his seat for the Upper Hunter, and
had gone into the Council to pilot the Bill
through. The Opposition was, however, too
strong, so he decided on getting in another
batch of supporters. The Council had been
appointed on May 13, 1856, for five years. On
May 10, 1861 —three days before the Council
was doomed to expire—Cowper appointed
21 new members, the only notable man in the
list being W. B. Dailey. The President,
W. W. Burton, took the Chair as usual, but
immediately rose and announced that he had
resigned the Chair and his seat in the House.
Captain “Bobbv” Towns got on his legs and
exclaimed “Follow your leader, boys,” and
the Opposition walked out in a body, leaving
John Robertson and his 16 supporters help-
less ; and two days later the Council’s little
life ran out. The next Council was appointed
on June 24, 1861, and members were then for
the first time sent in for the term of their
natural lives.
Hartley Williams, retired Judge of the
Victorian Supreme Court, receives four sovs.
a day from his grateful country—and spends
the money in England. The departure of the
ex-Judge was partly accounted for by his
second marriage. The lady in that case
was an English connection, and her home-
sickness led her to urge a flitting when the
time seemed ripe. The Williams dwelling
at Malvern was a decent place, and calcu-
lated to make a placid soul quite satisfied
with Australia. After the pensioner’s re-
moval the property passed into the hands
of Stratford Strettle, a well-known Mel-
bourne stock and station agent, since de-
ceased. Later, it was bought by widow
Lawrence, relict of a Williamstown pilot
whose ship came home with a goodly cash
cargo. Between times Melba leased the
establishment for a term. While these some-
what lengthy transactions were taking olace,
every blessed day four sovs. of good Aus-
tralian money were going to a never-very-
important person, engaged in passing the
time on the other side of the globe.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The S. M. Herald, referring to the offer
of the Hoskins firm to sell the Lithgow iron-
works to the N.S.W. Government, says it is
the first effect of syndicalism in Australia.
Presumably the Broken Hill Prop.’s modern
works at Newcastle, now rapidly approach-
ing completion, are the second effect.
GRANNY IS ALARMED.
Our statesmen have, however, always recognised
the possibility of these trusts obtaining a foothold
in Australia, and the -legislation passed by both the
Commonwealth and the States has been directed to
meet this danger if, and when, it arrives. When the
Referendum proposals were placed before the country
we repeatedly expressed the view that the powers in
this regard were ample to deal with any real attack
on the interests of the community.—S. M. Herald.
S.M.H. : “Here, my dear, the time is now
ripe.”
Australia: “What! Fight it with that,
thing?”
A Government like that of Westralia
should refuse subsidies to public bodies
which fail to carry out the settled policy of
the country. Perth City Council is a case
in view. Recently, a caretaker being wanted
for the municipal baths, the council adver-
tised for a “married couple without encum-
brance,” and all the while the State rang
with an outcry about its want of population.
It is time some politician proposed a law
under which the person who calls a child
an “encumbrance” shall get three months
for certain. And if the person is connected
with any church he should get six months.
There were two things that the Saviour
didn't say: (1) “Suffer little encumbrances
to come unto Me”; (2) “Thou hast hid
these things from the wise and prudent,
and revealed them unto encumbrances.”
The Victorian Government lias announced
that it will offer another sixpence a day to
its police force from July 1. It was a matter
of raising the honorarium or reducing the
physical standard; but the latter has been
reduced so often, and the former raised so
seldom, that Watt’s crowd thought it had
better give the cash and get brawn, even if,
on the pay offered, it is utterly hopeless to
expect brain.
Sydney D.T. (ominous initials those!)
drew itself up to its full height in its occa-
sionally financial column, on 11/2/T4, wiped
the froth off its moustache, and gave an ex-
A HE COW OF A TIME.
THE BIG INTRUDER: "Ahem! After you,
old chap!”
hibition of gentlemanly fury, marred only by
a few hiccups. To put the matter briefly, the
Investors’ Review had accused Australasia
of being loan-drunk, and the B.T. (again
those shaky initials!) took the quarrel on it-
self, it being a dear old friend of ’Stralia and
jealous of its reputation as a moderate
drinker. When the worst of its rage had
been worked off it started triumphantly to
prove that ’Stralia was perfectly sober by
strewing an array of figures on the bar
counter. Why, in four years, while ’Stralia
was drinking a mere £30,000,000, Canada had
swallowed £169,000,000, Argentina had reeled
out with £72,000,000 in its bottle, and Brazil
was dozing comfortably in a corner, after
having disposed of £60,000,000. “Shurely,”
gulped the D.T., tipping its hat over its
bloodshot eyes and tightly gripping the edge
of Ae counter —“shurely they are far more
glarin’ exshamplsh!” There is the crystal-
lised logic of the drunk. Because Smith and
Brown are drunker’n / am, therefore I am
perfectly sober!
According to a witness before
the Inter-State Commission last
week, the Holman Government,
which is kept in power by. the
votes of N.S.W. Laborites and
Labor sympathisers, lately gave
a contract for strawboard to. a
Japanese firm, the sole consid-
eration apparently being the
price. Perhaps Holman ruled
out the question of patriotism
on account of the competing
Australian paper mill being
located in Victoria. To such a
confirmed parish pumner the 1
Victorian is as much a foreigner
as the Jap. But Labor in
N.S.W. should remember it.
smainst Holman; in fact, it is
morally bound to remember it.
Loyalty to the Labor Cause (if
not to. Australia) should not
be merely a matter of wages
and working conditions and
irritating strikes. A strike
against Holman would be a sort of inspira-
tion just now.
From the uttermost north of Australia: —
In a Melbourne Age which has drifted up this far
I read:—
“We, in Australia, completely surrounded by sea,
with a barren and inhospitable coast, and several
weeks’ sail from' any potential enemy . .”
The Age either doesn’t know geography or has weird
ideas of what constitutes a “potential enemy.’’ Just
seven days’ journey for a fighting ship from this
writer’s address, which is in Australia, is Japan, with
a large modern navy and an over-populous country.
These two factors are generally accepted- as the things
chiefly necessary to turn a nation into the “potential
enemy” of a large empty country with a congenial
climate and an inadequate defence. If the Age were
to shift its printery from the comparative safety of
Melbourne to (say) the north of Queensland, it would
discover that the “several weeks” had boiled down to
a few days, and that, so far as the North is con-
cerned, the “barren and inhospitable coast” only
exists in infrequent patches. The invader will not be
foolish enough to steam all the way to Smellbourne
and its dangerous harbor to do his invading, when,
by dropping in on the far handier North, he can be
ashore and have the billy boiled before we can call
round to ask Whaffor ? We may be able to turn
him out; and, again, we may not. But it does seem
easier ,to have the argument with the intruder at
. the garden-gate instead of trying to fire him out of
the parlor.
Martin’s Pills are sold by all Chemists and
Stores in Australasia. Ladies recommend
them. Be sure you get the genuine with the
signature, Martin, Chemist, Southampton.*
The struggle to move the wheat that is
waiting for export, to the one port in N.S.W.
which is allowed to deal with the business,
grows really strenuous. At February 7,.
1911, there were 2;949,000 bags clamoring
for trucks at N.S.W. country railway sta-
tions. In 1912 (same date) there were
3.488-000 bags. In 1913 the arrears consisted
of 3.034,000 bags. Now in 1914 they amount
to 5,190,000 bags. At 13 different country
stations more than 100,000 bags are bogged
or stranded, while over 251.000 bags have
been on one day blotting out the sun on the
wharf at Darling Harbor, Sydney.
Land is becoming so scarce in artificially
congested Sydney that greedy eyes are now
faxed on cemetery re-
serves. The Ryde muni-
cipality is approaching
the Minister for Lands
and begging him to revoke
the dedication of 160
acres of land that have
been awaiting the first
North Shore corpse and
gravedigger ■ for 12 long
years. Also, owing
doubtless to the high
price of meat, reverent,
undertakerish sportsmen
have lately been shooting
rabbits in the Necropolis.
Sydney is growing very
crowded, and if some of
the closed ports are not
opened and the popula-
tion pressure thereby lessened, the Sydney
dead of to-morrow may have to be buried in
trees.
Henry Hawker, the Australian flying man,
has offered some remarks about this coun-
try’s aerial defence fleet which should make
the Cook Government sit up and think. By
reason of the importing craze the sky-ships
were ordered in England, and it was 12
months before they arrived. Then because-
the somnolent Department didn’t quite know
what to do with them, and hadn’t yet im-
ported men to manage them—being a somno-
lent importing Department it didn’t dream
of giving local men a show and letting them
train other local men—the goods have fes-
tered in retirement for another 12 months.
And as a greatly improved model comes out
about twice a year the contraptions are
already antiquated. The country might have
been busy learning how to build and manage
air-craft.' Thanks to the importing disease
it has only been looking at the outsides of a
lot of ancient packing-cases.

By the Religious Editor: —
“The news of the appointment of the Hon. Munro-
Ferguson, as Governor-General of Australia in succes-
sion to Lord Denman, lias given universal satisfac-
tion.” So clamors one of the feverish little papers
that do their best to be a sort of Court Journal to
the suburbs. And the obscure Mo-Ferg knows so
little about the country whose bazaars he is to open
that the cable reports him as “devouring all the
books upon Australia that he can get hold of.” That
and some letters from ex-Governor Carmichael, of
Victoria, seem to be his equipment. Yet the panting
little rag is dead sure that his appointment has “given
universal satisfaction” to the people he doesn’t know,
and who certainly don’t know him. Hell is draped
with this sort of cow's innards.
An ex-Han sard man
writes: —
Dan O’Connor was invariably a
“fat turn.” A hundred words of
his grandiloquent, sesquipedalian
phraseology, when judiciously con-
densed to essentials, would repre-
sent about five lines. One night
he made a long speech on the
land question. It was my lot to
start with him on a 20min.
“turn” ; but the Chief, well know-
ing what a rambling, repetitious
mass of verbiage it would be,
directed that I should take the
whole of it. I had over four
hours of Demos-
thenes. When the
report was issued
Dan leaped into the
Chief’s room in a
state of coat-tail
and belltopper and
fur}', and demanded
to know why his
speech had been so
much curtailed.
“Here you are, look
at this. Sir Hen-
ery made a speech
which occupied ex-
actly an hour, and
it filled so many
pages. My speech
lasted over four
hours, and I have
fewer pages.” The
explanation was
simple and unsatis-
factory. Dan had
obliged by sending
his “notes” for the
verification of quo-
tations. The “notes”
disclosed a masterly
speech, all pith and
point, and the
handwriting was
that of W. B. Dai-
ley. It would have
lasted half an hour.
That was the speech
we printed. Of
Daniel’s wander-
ings and sham-
blings and mean-
derings and rav-
ines and vast polvslyllabic perambulations we took
no notice at all. We told him this, and he was as
wild as an intoxicated Rechabite.

grapple, and his grip won’t become blue-
mouldy for want of use. .
THE HINDU.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There were two views of the Indian population in
Fiji. The Indians were easily governed and kept in
subjection. They were also a fecund race. The stop-
page of the supply of labor would be a heavy blow.
On the other hand, the Indian had in many places
ousted the small trader. Merchants regarded the
outlook with apprehension.— J. M. Iledstrom, Legis-
lative Councillor and trader from, Fiji, in S. M.
Herald (12/2/’l4).
Oh, let us have our good Hindu,
Our bland and mild Hindu,
Who doesn’t want to live like you,
Who never swanks like you,
A gross and meat-fed crew ;
Yes, let us keep our nice Hindu,
Our humble fellow-subject, who
Will labor all the long day through
For rice and stew—
Hooroo !
Oh, spare our diligent Hindu,
Our thrifty, strong Hindu, ,
Who would not go on strike like you,
Who dares not strike like you,
A rank, rebellious crew ;
Yes, grant us yet our cheap Hindu,
So gentle and so fecund, who
Will swiftly multiply by two
In inky hue —

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The conclusion of Sydney’s ninth round
of First-Grade Cricket was highly interesting. On
the previous Saturday rain had driven most of the
teams from the fields after less than an hour of
batting. Of course, in such circumstances, which
made it necessary fpr both sides to get through
then first innings on the last day of the round
drawn j games were bound to be numerous. But
Gordon s chances of making a draw with University
looked hue The Students had got loose on the
Gordon bowling, and gathered up 355 for four
wickets before the rain drove the teams from the
held. On Saturday last, instead of declaring right
away, as everybody expected, Uni. went on and
accumidated 407 for seven wickets. Then realising
that it had to get Gordon out to win, it hurriedly
closed and went out to rush Gordon through. But
jc Students had forgotten Gordon’s strong man
Macartney. And how did he rage! The heathen was
milk and water compared to him, and the Wowser
rnnl a H h ° lamb. He stood like solid
f l} dlnB \ afternoon, and smote and
bashed and battered and biffed till 227 appeared
te hl t ~m- For a tlme h is disheartened
colleagues faded away, man by man, but at last
Jordan came and made a stubborn stand, and toge-
ther they broke the heart and hope of University,
mu / th f, dusk was dosing in Macartney put his
leg before the wicket. S. Trumper took his place, and
he and Jordan played it out till the light went and
tile stumps were drawn, with eight wickets down for
. *’• n hat had looked like sure defeat was turned
into a drawn game. Middle Harbor had a similar
experience Sydney had collected 235 for six wickets
and closed. Middle Harbor had no earthly hope ex-
cept by keeping the sticks up till time ran out. This
* UCCC< '! I<,(I in doing, thanks to Randell (41), Lowe
(3o) and Anderson (17). But it was a close shave
—nine wickets had gone for V miserable 117 when
gloom ended the struggle. Paddington also played
clo p k - ° n the first day it had felled
Glebe wickets for 165 runs. On Saturday
Olobe lifted the score to 281, when it closed with
nine wickets down. Paddington got busy and wal-
loped with such energy that when the light vanished
only eight, wickets had fallen for 215, and Glebe
had missed its win. Waverley, which had 228 to its
credit, failed to put North Sydney through the mill,
the stubborn hillmen losing only four wickets at
knock-off time for 121. Balmain was unlucky. It
hit 196 off the Western Suburbs bowling, but the
Suburbs team responded with 213 for the loss of
only three wickets, Taylor, the not-out man, smit-
ing 101. So Balmain was down and but by 17 runs
on the first innnings. Redfern was a sad example of
what happens when you under-rate your antagonist.
Captain Goddard, having seen 196 smitten off Cen-
tral Cumberland for five wickets, closed; but he
struck quite unexpected scoring on the part of the
Parramatta rank and file. Best (40), Thomas (44),
Pye n.o. (41) and others lifted 201 on to the
board for the loss of five wickets, and so defeated
Goddard and his merry men by five runs.
Individually, Macartney, of course, was easily the best
with 227 off the University bowlers. Incidentally, it
might be mentioned that the Gordon warrior occupied
only 190 min. compiling that mammoth score. Next
to him came old Syd Gregory with 128 (n. 0.) for
Waverley; then the 102 of Emery for Redfern, who
also humped his timber at the end of the innings.
J. S. Taylor made 101 for Western Suburbs, and
practically won the match against Balmain. This
concluded the list of centuries. V. C. Robins, of
Glebe, carried his bat for 85 ; Lamrock, of Univer-
sity, made 77 ; Fusedale, of Sydney, carried his bat
for 59 ; Coffey, of Sydney, also made 55 : Moore, of
North Sydney, hit 54 ; and Dwyer, of Paddington,
was still on deck at the end of the innings for 51.
Among the bowlers the best was P. W. Dive, of West
ern Suburbs, who smashed six Balmain wickets fo,
39 runs. S. C. Freeman, of Glebe, who took five
Paddington wickets for 69 ; Shortland, of Gordon,
who got five University sticks for 145 ; and Best, of
Central Cumberland (otherwise Parramatta), who got
four Redfern wickets for 48; Hopkins, the bee-
farmer, took four Waverley wickets for North Sydney
at a cost of 51 runs; and Barbour, of University,
upset four Gordon wickets for 66. Altogether, it was
an exciting afternoon.
’ * * * *
This is from the Edmonton Bulletin,
published in Alberta, Canada: —“I doubt if the
Australian cricketers left more than 10 dollars’ worth
of information behind them in Winnipeg, and I
don’t see what good can come from another lavish
expenditure. If any money is to be spent it is in
the interests of cricket that it should be expended
on bringing from the Old Country a thoroughly
good professional. More can be learned from one
professional in one month than could be learned
from 12 annual consecutive visits from the Aus-
tralians. Never think for one moment this Aus-
tralian tour is a philanthropic one. It is a money-
making scheme got up by Mr. Benjamin, and the
promoter has doubtless done well as the result of
his enterprise. . . .Winnipeg got the Australians
cheaper than any other city, paying $l2OO for three
games. It was a terrific price, and money thrown
away. But for the tour now planned $lOOO per
match is asked.” The Australian Board of Con-
trol should hit this brand of boodle-cricket with an
axe, and hit it so hard that only one blow will be
necessary.
* * * *
The Australian XI in Maoriland struck a
snag at Wellington last week, when a local bowler
named Southall destroyed five wickets for 34 runs,
and a wight named Saunders created a painful im-
pression by getting away with three for 40. The
mighty Trumper was caught and bowled by Saunders
for a duck, and Southall clean bowled Armstrong for
the same round sign of zero. The whole side van-
ished for 124. However, Armstrong had his revenge
when the Maorilanders went to the sticks. He took
four of their wickets for two runs. Five wickets
fell for 32.
* * * *
The Sheffield Shield match at Adelaide
between South Australia and Victoria saw some
sensational bowling by Crawford and Mayne for the
Croweaters. The Victorians had disposed of the
South Australians for 274, but by nightfall they had
lost two wickets for 28. On Saturday Crawford and
Mayne broke loose and the former took five wickets
for 48 and Mayne three for six. Three Victorians
failed to score, and Baring’s 71 was the top figure.
Carroll (35), Fitzpatrick (40), and Ryder (n.o. 36)
were the other principal scorers, but the side van-
ished for 213. With a lead of 61 the South Aus-
tralians started on the second round, and although
there was no sensational scoring, it was so good all
round that they rattled up 349. Victoria, requiring
411 to win, started on a hopeless looking: task, and
bv nightfall had accumulated 96 for the loss of two
wickets. On Tuesday the score was lifted to 185 for
six wickets, and the case looked more hopeless than
-ver.
Australia is threatened with another
Yankee baseball raid, according to one “Jim” Brown,
He says it will eventuate in October next and fall
on the “gate” at Brisbane, Sydney, Melbourne and
Adelaide.
* * * #
Summer baseball has now entered on the
championship competition, and on Saturday the men
from Leichhardt came up against the men of Wav-
erley and overthrew them by eight runs to three. The
Waverley batting was indifferent compared to that of
Leichhardt, and it was not till the fifth session that
the losers managed to score. Leichhardt started well,
making two at the jump ; but it was in the last ses-
sion it laid Waverley waste by scoring a whole four.
* ' * * *
Died at Williamstown tMelb.) last week,
George Coop, whose name was well known to Aus-
tralia in the long ago. Coop made his mark on
several branches of sporting endeavor, and as a
wrestler he worked for some time with Professor
Miller when that big lump of a strong man was
young at the game.
* * * *
The bone of contention among the amateur
swimmers of Sydney on Saturday was the final for
the President’s Cup. This goblet goes to the swim-
mer who scores the most points in a competition of
four long-distance races: 880 Yards, 1320 Yards, One
Mile, and Three Miles. Saturday saw the Three
Miles battled through. The sun was fierce, but the
breeze was delightful, and the water was tepid in
the fervent heat. Longworth was on scratch and had
to wait 25min. while the distance man battled away.
In March, 1911, in that same Domain bath, he had
established the record by swimming the distance in
lhr. ISmin. 22 2-ssec. He was sure on Saturday that
he had no hope of winning, but he determined to do
his darnedest, and his darnedest was a fine effort. It
proved that, as a long-distance swimmer, he is as
mighty as ever he was. Although he failed to win
the race, and finished third, he broke his own record
by getting through the distance in lhr. 18min. 8 2-ssec.
Colton won the race, and by so doing scored 12
points in the competition. Longworth was second,
with 10 ; and Weilandt third, with nine. So Colton
takes the President’s goblet.
* * * *
Oscar Dickman picked up the 440 Yards
N.S.W. professional championship at the League of
Swimmers’ meeting at Leichhardt (Sydney) last
week. There were six other starters, but Dick-
man led the way from the beginning, and paddled
home an easy winner in smin. 52sec. Which was
deadly slow. At the same assembly Miss G.
Hooper decorated her costume with the Ladies’ 100
Yards Championship in lmin. 18 2-ssec.
IRONY.
Johnny Summers counted out in a prayerful
attitude in the first round of his return scrap
with Tom McCormick, at Sydney.
The first instalment of the Amateur
Athletic Championships of N.S.W. at Sydney Sports
Ground on Saturday drew a crowd of about three
thousand, composed mostly of ladies and old and
young men with clean-shaven visages. The patriots
prayed and hoped for the downfall of the Yankee
band which had picked up six championships at
Melbourne. But prayers were of no avail against
Yankee fleetness, and the Yankees got away with the
half-dozen events in which they competed. They were
heralded as the “cream” of athletic strength, and
proved to be it. The N.S.W. talent was not even
good buttermilk in comparison. The draw card
was G. L. Parker, of the U.S.A., and he filled his
part by annexing the 100 and 440 Yards champion-
ships. Parker does not strike the eye as what a
champion should be. There is none of the grace of
Apollo Belvedere about him. He is heavy and un-
gainly, and it is gigantic strength which propels him
at such speed. In the 100 Yards his pace at 20yds.
was as fast as when he smashed the tape, and to the
cold eye of the onlooker it seemed that had Bradley
of N.S.W. unwound his sprint in the final a little
earlier history might have told another tale than
the defeat of the Australian by 12in. The 440 Yards
provided a hurricane tussle for half way, but there-
after it was a procession with Parker leading the
way, galloping as though pursued by devils or wild
women. Taking time for it Parker is no great
shakes. A wind strong enough to blow over hurdles
was raging behind the runners, yet the 100 Yards
occupied 10 1-5 sec., and the 440 Yards usurped
51 4-5 sec. These times are far behind both Aus-
tralasian and World records. The finest spectacle
was the Mile Championship, where J. Power (U.S.A.)
defeated A. F. Thorpe and others in 4min. 33 3-ssec.
—lOsecs. behind the worst' figures in the “book.”
Power took the bit in his molars when starter Dick
Coombes pulled the trigger, and with a beautiful action
made for the tape as though the Peace Society were
after him. Thorpe sorely tried to burst his boiler
in the chase, but failed to do so by three yards.
Power’s evenness of movement is something to cheer
about. R. R. Templeton, a veritable india-rubber
ball of Yankee make, also picked up a double—the
120 Yards Hurdles from J. AV. Fraser in 15 4-ssec.
(the only N.S.W. record equalled in the running
events), and the pole vault, wherein he scaled a
miserable 10ft. Still the best N.S.W. effort was
a still more miserable 9ft., shared by C. T. Fergu-
son and R. A. Adams. The other limb of the Yankee
team picked up a championship trifle when the broad-
shouldered E. R. Caughey heaved with his left hand
161 b. of lead over a distance of 43ft.Olin. Local roan
J. O’Reilly, with 39ft. llfin., was the best Aus-
tralian effort. Afterwards Caughey heaved the
object 44ft. Sin.—s4in. over the N.S.W. record.
In the other events, left solely to N.S.w.
athletes, policeman A. W. Pickard had no
difficulty in outing the opposition in the Mile Walk
in 6min. 54sec., and “Jqck” Smith broad-jumped
opponents and won with a commendable 22ft. 3?in.
Thus ended the first feast-day for the American
athletes, and only an earthquake will stay a second
banquet the comjng Saturday. #
Germany is going to make an effort to
sweep the board at the next Olympiad. A commis-
sion has been sent to the United States to study
American methods, and bring back, regardless of
cost, the best traine: that can be discovered. He
is to train the German trainers, who will train the
Olympians. This individual is to be paid partly by
the Kingdom of Prussia arid partly by the Germanic
Empire. German athleticism is to be nationalised.
The country 1 is divided into districts, and each dis-
trict will select its best, and then the best in the
Empire will be selected from the best in the dis-
tricts. It ip a fine, comprehensive scheme.
The sculling championship of Maoriland
is an item that has not been wildly sought after of
late. But it is threatened with resurrection. Wil-
liam Fogwell, a busted N.S.W. championship hope,
migrated and picked up the honor which was lying
on the roadside, a couple of years ago, and has kept
it ever since to the indifference of the Shivery
Isles. Now, however, Paddy Hannan has thrown in
a challenge. If the match is arranged both scullers
will have to cross the Tasman Sea to fulfill the condi-
tion that the Maoriland championship can be raced
for only in Maorjland waters.
The racing at Caulfield last Saturday
knocked the bottom out of the idea that Traquette
and Woorak are 2-year-olds of the highest class.
Brilliant youngsters in the past have proved equal
to winning the Oakleigh Handicap from a field _ of
seasoned sprinters, but Woorak, a warm favorite,
after making all the rqnning up to the last turn,
was not in it with Pppinjay, Nuwara Eliya, Aleconner
and co. Previous tp the Oakleigh Plate race, Tra-
quette, with lOst. lib. up, had made a poor show
in the Federal Stakes. The top weight was going
nearly 3st. to the winner, but the “flying” filly
should at least have put up a fight.
On his Oakleigh Plate performance Popin-
jay has a fair chance to take the Futurity Stakes for
the second time in three tries. But there are others.
Some of the lighter weights must be dangerous in
the Futurity—which is worth £2OOO, and carries no
penalty for the winper—if they are to make any sort
of show in the Newmarket Handicap a week later.
Garnell, for instance. Thusfar, Mr. Baron Brown’s
imported mare has done nothing to speak of, but now
is the time for her to cut a dash if it is in her.
Carnell’s work on the track of late suggests the
possibility of a win next Saturday.
Something of a record! All six gee-gees
which poked their noses first past the winning post at
the V.A.T.C. meeting at Caulfield (Vic.) on Saturday
last were bred in New South Wales. The faith in
the Ma State brute is likely to rise somewhat over
such an happening.
Passed out at Bondi (Sydney) last week,
Charles Stanley, probably the greatest of “old time”
Australian jockeys. It was in 1868 Stanley was at
hip best, and kicked up a tremendous dust by win-
ning two V.R.C. Derbies (the only two Derbies ever
attempted in one State in one year), the St. Leger,
Sydney Cup, A.J.C. Metropolitan and Melbourne Cup.
It was a hurricane performance even for the bad old
days. # »
The mariners of Prince Alfred Yacht Club
were accused of neglecting their womenfolk, not
having had a ladies’ day since December 13; so, to
restore peace in a hundred homes, Saturday’s race
for the Albert Cup was made a social function as well
as a sporting contest. Afternoon tea and cakes were
added to the usual necessaries of life served out by
the club to the hardened seadogs. The Albert Cup
is an inside race, and the utensil itself is the gift
of the well-known mariner, Mr. F. Albert, who
donated it last year. The final owner will be the
yachtsman who wins it three times. Last year it
fell to Messrs. Wadsworth and Fleming in the
Aoma. This year, Rawhiti (scratch), Sayonara,
Culwulla 111., Petrel, Aoma, Ranee, Sunbeam and
Curlew all went after it. Curlew was the distance
boat, with 33min. handicap. It was never headed,
but led from start to finish, and when the Culwulla
111. made its splendid dash the handicap held good
apd brought the Albert Cup to Mr. A. Wilson.
The Sydney Flying Squadron had the light
north-east wind so well-beloved by the open-boat men
for the usual two heats and a final. The feature
of the first bout was the defeat of Chris Webb in
Australian. Sunny Sputh went off with a bound,
and the only boat that could keep pace with it was
N[ona. They raced together to Shark Island, where
Mona went overboard and left Sunny South undis-
puted winner. The breeze freshened nicely as the
second heat started, and Eunice, with three minutes
start, looked a sure thing. But Stewart’s Swastika
went to the front, apd led the way to the Taylor
Bay mark by a few seconds. Then Eunice let fly a
topsail and ballooner and gradually drew level.
Swastika, for some reason, did not fly any extras,
and Eunice 11. won, with Swastika and Livonia
second and third respectively. In the final, Sunny
South had four and a half minutes start to the
three minutes of Eunice 11. Again Sunny South led
from start to finish. Eunice was fourth on the list
for a long while, but gradually forced its way to
the tail of the leader, and came in second. Sunny
South, which had never been seriously threatened,
finished with a margin of 40sec. * #
Little Jimmy Hill, featherweight champion
of Australia, put up a good scrap in defence of his
title at the Olympia Stadium, Newtown, Sydney, on
Saturday night. He was matched with Sid Sullivan,
and Sid was just quarter of a pound lighter than
Hill, who weighed exactly Bst. 131 b. It was
easily the best and most brilliant fight the new
Stadium has witnessed. Four times the champion
was sent to the, floor, and four times he got up
again and resumed business at the old address. It
was a fine exhibition of pluck and determination.
But the loser made just as brave a show. During
the latter stages of the fight both his eyes were prac-
tically closed, and although he could only peer at his
elusive foe he fought like a wild cat to the finish.
The last time he knocked Hill down it was doubtful
if he knew he had done so. There was little between
them, but at the end the referee gave Hill the verdict
on points. #
At Brisbane, Frank Picato held converse
with the returned and much improved Ray Bronson,
who worried him with straight lefts and disparaged
his sad Italian face most grievously. Picato dealt
out staggering uppercuts which made Bronson often
regret he had ever seen the light of day. It was
not a spectacular fight, but a slogging match, in which
both boys showed Spartan endurance till the end,
when the referee declared it a ll .drawn battle. The
crowd made a hostile demonstration, apparently con-
dering Picato had won. In Melbourne, Frank Ellis,
of Maoriland, created a sensation by getting all oyer
'be promising local fighter, George Taylor. Taylor
did not reproduce the snap and force that had made
him so popular. The rapid evolutions of the Maori-
lander baffled him badly, and the uppercuts he landed
out made the Victorian wonder if the present centrali-
sation policy is all it might be. However, he weath-
ered the gale till the finish, and was defeated on
points only.
There is joy in Pugdom at the cabled
news that Beg Baker has managed to induce Jimmy
Clabby to return to Australia. Clabby is a mighty
warrior these days and his price is high; but the
Australian promoter fished with the right bait, and
James will leave for Australia early next month.
His first opponent here will probably be Jeff Smith,
and then McGoorty. He fought Jeff Smith in New
York in June, 1911, but as it was a 10-round no-
decision affair it does not shed much light on what
may happen in Australia.
THE CROWD
WATCHING
HAWKER
FLY.
Specially snapped
for
THE BULLETIN.
The falling of Johnny Summers on Satur-
day last at Sydney Stadium was bewildering, pic-
turesque and sensational. As the prophet Bruserius
sayeth in the Fug’s Acopolysse, “He who goeth
looking for a knock-out shall surely catch it —on the
jaw.” Ordinarily, there is no more cautious be-
ginner than Summers. He usually advances to the
fray, like Agag, walking delicately, even as a cat
walks on eggs. He feels his man before mixing it,
and if his man tries to mix it the man finds Johnny
an elusive sprite. But on this occasion Johnny was
in such a hurry that he forgot to touch the scapular
in his sock. He had evidently made up his mind
that McCormick would outbox him if it went 20
rounds, and had determined to overwhelm McC. in
short order. At the word he bounded across the
ring, and fell upon his enemy like a long street
full of tigers. He smote with both fists. He hurled
all his weight into the contract, and pounded the
hostile person right across the ring, with showers
of body blows, varied by a few to the head which
McCormick covered desperately with his gloves. It
was the ropes that stopped McCormick’s wild re-
treat. Bringing up against them he rebounded, and
strove to clinch. Summers swung him round, threw
him away, cast him ofF, implied by his manner that
he had no use for him, and followed up. McCormick
uncovered his head and tried to smite. He was
despised and rejected. He tried to hang on and he
might as well have tried to drive a nail into the
side of the east wind and suspend himself thereto.
But the fiercest cyclone must blow itself out, and
so did Johnny. And the moment he subsided into a
calm McCormick, angered by ill-usage and aroused
by the way the audience urged Johnny to knock his
head off and throw it through the window, assumed
the offensive. He met a right swing in the chest
that nearly knocked him down, but recovering he
sprang again, and let fly a double broadside as he
did. Then it was Summers who broke ground while
he gathered steam for another cyclone. He backed
away till the northern ropes blocked him, and
McCormick hurled himself at him amidst much
pandemonium. Summers is most dangerous on the
ropes, and some historic bashes were exchanged.
Dropping his fists he played for the body,
McCormick gasped as if a piano had fallen on him.
But all the same he never stopped hitting. Two
lefts landed on Summers’s mouth. Then McCormick
was standing in close, and seeing Johnny’s face un-
protected drove a right short-arm jolt right to the
point. The recipient pitched forward on to his
knees and gloves. He was knocked unconscious,
and still had enough in him to protect himself as he
fell. He came down and tried to stop on his gloves
and knees, but everything faded out and he again
pitched forward till his forehead touched the floor
a few inches from where Waldemar Holberg had
gone down, all out, before Hughie Mehegan. But
Holberg fell forward on his chin, Summers on his
forehead. The roar ceased. A silence crashed
down on the Stadium, and the voice of Scott calling
the count sounded something akin to a town hall
clock. Nobody believed that the mighty Summers,
the greatest ring general of them all, was out. He
was taking all the respite he could get, they said.
But when the voice of Scott boomed “eight they
roared that he had already taken a dashed lot of
respite and shouldn’t overdo it. “Nine came,
and the man on the floor was still taking heaps ot
respite. Even Scott looked amazed and hesitated
for the fraction of a second before he said “Out.
or whatever he did say, for his voice was drowned
in the multitude of the hoot. There are some awful
dingoes among the Stadium habitu&s. His seconds
dragged Summers to his feet and to his chair and doused
him with water, and then he opened two blue, bewil-
dered eyes and wondered what the riot was about.
It was a blow that would have felled a bullock, but
the hardy little ex-fireman was soon able to get up
and scramble through the ropes from the arena in
which he had scored so many brilliant victories.
From the moment Scott had said “Box on. till
Summers struck the floor was exactly 2mm 29sec.
But into that space of time had been crowded one ot
the most spectacular fights ever seen in the Stadium.
Country racegoers in Victoria are suffer-
ing something extra from a profitable but impudent
scheme pf “scaling.” Abraham O’Bellow lays the
odds, as per usual and when the last race has been
run coolly assures winning punters that he can
pay, having laid nothing else but the winner. Then
he hands bettors their stakes, and departs with
bulging pockets. Sometimes he gets jobbed ; some-
times, as at Shepparton, lately, he is chased mt
the committee room or the secretary s tent, and tnen
the Law guards him from assault and battery till
the swindled ones have departed. These are the
sort of facts which Ikey’s friend the pareon
ignores when someone points out that tne to
always pavs the mathematical odds in , f “ 11 ’ ess T A_
per cent., and never has to be protected bv the Lav
while infuriated punters chase it with blue m
and “langwidge.”

WHY THE HUMAN SYSTEM
NEEDS EXERCISE
Because it is a natural stimulant for replacing
the waste tissue of the human system.
Every effort you make causes the destruction of a
greater or less quantity of tissue in proportion to
the exertion involved.
This process is going on every minute of the day,
and, unless you exercise judiciously, the accumula-
tion of waste tissue will debilitate your system.
Of course, you may not notice the difference. It
is so gradual. But it certainly exists. It is ever
increasing, and always undermining your energy—
your health.
By performing a few simple, yet scientific, exer-
cises, such as those comprising the
Baker Course of

you immediately expel the destroyed or worn-out
tissue, and construct new tissue to take its place.

Secrets of Beauty
SELECTED RECIPES FROM HERE
AND THERE.—THINGS EVERY
WOMAN WANTS TO KNOW.
Getting Rid of
Female Moustaches.
“Practical Suggestions.”
To women who are annoyed by disfiguring downy
hair growths a method of. permanently eradicating
the same will come as a piece of good news. For
this purpose pure powdered pheminol may be used.
Almost any chemist should be able to supply an
ounce of this drug. The recommended treatment is
designed not only to remove the disfiguring growth
instantly, leaving no trace, but also to actually kill
the hair roots without irritating the skin. * * *
Objectionable body odors resulting from perspiration
and other causes may be instantly banished by
simply applying a little powdered (white) pergol to
the affected surface occasionally. * * * Smart
women are rapidly adopting the use of the natural
allacite of orange blossoms when the complexion is
inclined to be oily. It makes a capital greaseless
cream, holds the powder perfectly, and does not
encourage hair growths.
Naturally Wavy Hair.
“Home Chemistry.”
Good stallax not only makes the best possible
shampoo, but has the peculiar property of leaving
the hair with a pronounced natural “wave,” an
effect presumably desired by many ladies. A tea-
spoonful of the stallax granules, dissolved in a cup
of hot water is ample for a luxurious head wash,
and leaves the hair with a peculiar lustre and fluffi-
ness obtainable from nothing else as far as known.
It is perfectly harmless, and can be obtained from
most chemists, although since it has been so little
used for this purpose it comes only in sealed original
packages, enough to make up twenty-five or thirty
individual shampoos. * * * For an actual hair
grower nothing equals pure boranium. It is quite
harmless, and sets the hair roots tingling with new
life. * * * The use of rouge is almost always
obvious, but powdered colliandum gives a perfectly
natural color and defies detection.
Oxygen Removes a
Bad Complexion.
“Practical Suggestions.”
Oxygen is now used to clear the complexion. Its
peculiar property of destroying waste matter and not
injuring healthy tissue is well known. Bad com-
plexions are merely the accumulation of half-dead
waste matter on the skin surface. This accumulation
shows in the form of sallowness, moth patches and a
generally lifeless appearance. Smart women now
clear off these imperfections by getting some merco-
lised wax from the chemist’s, and applying it for a
few nights like cold cream. This wax contains
oxygen, which attacks and removes the disfiguring
waste matter. It is pleasant to use and perfectly
harmless.. The fresh, healthy skin which has been
covered up is soon revealed in all its beauty, and the
face so treated looks much younger and prettier as a
result. * * * To bring a natural red color
to the lips rub them with a soft stick of pro-
lactum. * * * For tired, hot or perspiring feet
use a teaspoonful of powdered onalite in a foot-
bath.
Home Beauty Aids.
“Household Hints.”
A persistently shiny nose or a dull, lifeless com-
plexion drives many a woman to cosmetics and con-
sequent despair. And all the time a simple remedy
lies at hand in the home. If you have no cleminite
in the house you need only get about an ounce from
your chemist and add just sufficient water to dis-
solve it. A little of this simple lotion is Nature’s
own beautifier. It is very good for the skin, and
instantly gives the complexion a soft, velvety, youth-
ful bloom that any woman might envy. It lasts all
day or evening, renders powdering entirely unneces-
sary, and absolutely defies detection. * * * To
make the eyelashes grow long, dark and curling,
apply a little mennaline with the finger tips occa-
sionally. It is absolutely harmless, and beautifies
the eyebrows as well. * * * Pilenta soap is the
most satisfactory for all complexions. It even works
well in cold or hard water.

The Inky Way.
“J.F.H.” (from London) : There have been journal-
istic high jinks here on the 80th birthday of Lord
Burnham, principal proprietor of the Daily Tele-
graph. Congratulatory addresses were piled upon
him from the journalists of the Empire, the staffs of
the D.T., and the congregation of the Christian
Church. Lord Northclilfe, head of the Harms-
worths, presented the general journalistic address
with a speech thickly coated with marmalade. Burn-
ham and Northcliffe at one time were at bitter war.
but they have now agreed to tolerate each other’s
presence in the universe. In those days of strife,
Northcliffe objected very strongly to Burnham's
proclamation of the first leading article that the
D.T. had the “largest circulation of any morning
paper in London” ; but peace was eventually secured
by Burnham’s rag inserting “penny” before “morn-
ing,” thereby practically conceding the champion
circulation to its pushful half-penny rival. Burn-
ham has spent 60 years in Fleet-street, London’s
great inky highway, and looks like going strong for
another 10. He is the working patriarch of the
profession. In a reminiscent speech he mentioned
that his first job was packing and tying up the
parcels in the publishing office of the London
Sunday Times.
* * * *
Papua has just witnessed the preliminary canter in
its very first libel action. The suit was brought by
Dr. W. M. Strong, lately Acting Chief Medical
Officer for the Possession, against the Papuan Times,
the lone but very vigorous representative of the Press
in the big island. The cause of the disturbance
was the smallpox epidemic in Sydney, the local
voice of the people making certain remarks on the
doctor’s work in administering the Quarantine Or-
dinance. Because of these remarks he howled for
£lOOO damages. Both parties were represented by
cost-pursuers, who are almost as rare as Great Auk’s
eggs in Papua, and amidst the unsuppressed excite-
ment of the inhabitants the doctor obtained a verdict
for £250. Defendant, who was formerly in the Papuan
public service and is no friend of Lieut-Governor
Murray, is appealing to the High Court of Australia
-—the first case to go there from Australia’s nigger
dependency.
The newest mine of any consequence opened up in
Maoriland is at Whangarei, and the treasure consists
of a faded sheet of manuscript that is alleged to be
either the original or a copy in the author’s writing
of Burns’s “Scots Wha Hae.” Photographs of the
manuscript were sent to the curator of the Burns
Museum at Ayr, in Scotland, for identification, and a
reply has come to hand stating that the goods are
genuine, but whether they are the original or merely
a copy in Burns’s handwriting cannot be ascertained.
Burns is known to have made several copies of his
shrill poem, one being purchased some years ago for
the Ayr Museum. At the top of the Whangarei
manuscript there is the inscription “To my sister-in-
law,” which may be the means of definitely tracing
its career later on. Anyhow, without more being
known about it, the expert sends out the glad news
that the scrap of paper is worth over £3OO.
“Plattypussy”: I can understand the indignation
of the Maoriland newspaper proprietors when their
scribes began forming themselves into unions and
registering in the Arbitration Court. The ordinary
staffman’s pay was previously bad enough (it is
still mean and paltry), but the free-lance writer is
sweated in a way that even M.L. newspaper pro-
prietors, with th r notoriously cheap-labor tradi-
tions, might be expected to be ashamed of. One
paper which, during the period of the late waterside
strike, loudly proclaimed itself on the side of “labor,”
offers its contributors 12s. 6d. per column for either
prose or verse, which pans out at about 7d. per inch.
It evidently believes that all laborers should be well
paid except those who labor for its columns.
Another pays for its matter (verse or prose) at the
rate of 7-id. per inch. Still another, when it does
accept fiction, pays or has paid me, at less than 3d.
per inch. Some of these sharks announce that all
accepted contributions are paid for, but unless you
hang to them like a bum bailiff no money material-
ises. Generally, applications for it are stalled by
the information that the verse, story, sketch or para-
graph w r as “not up to standard,” but was inserted
with the philanthropic desire to encourage the writer.
Some people call this sort of thing a harsh name,
but maybe they are prejudiced. Anyhow, the law
of libel prevents me discussing it.
John Plummer, probably the oldest inky-way man
in regular toil in N. S. Wales, had to seek the
shelter of the North Sydney Hospital a week or so
back. For most of his 35 years in Sydney he has
drawn his income chiefly from English papers with
which he was connected in earlier days. His most
prominent work in Sydney was on the old Illus-
trated News and Tribune, but the best he has
done of late has been for the Year Book op Aus-
tralia. He is now in his 83rd year.
* * * *
“Leo Lear”: Newspapers that pass out don’t al-
ways leave memorial stones in loving remembrance.
Such an one is the Portland (Vic.) Herald, about
which I can ferret out nothing; birth, disease, and
death are all forgotten. Yet that it existed I know
from a sheet preserved in an old ledger. Even J. R.
Kean, the proprietor of the Portland Guardian (a
paper that dates back without a break to 1842—a
record unequalled by any other Victorian print)
doesn’t remember anything about the Herald. My
copy is dated “Friday Evening, February 11, 1853,”
and consists largely of an elaborate exposition of
“An Act for the Prevention of the Disease Galled Scab
in Sheep.” Some of the advertisements are rather
ambiguous. For instance:—
“A Gentleman of Large Dimensions has taken from
a Poor Woman a £5 in mistake for a £1 note and
refuses to give it back.”
And there is at least one suffragette, who advertises,
cautioning all persons “against speaking prejudicial
to the undersigned without having just cause.” Also
one G. Lowe shows how the cost of living has risen
in the present decade by announcing that he is ready
to tear himself apart from 121 b. of beef in exchange
for Is. 6d.
* * * *
“Stamps”: The first record of penny postage in
N. S. Wales that I can find was in a series of post-
office charges published in Governor Macquarie’s
time, though probablv the system was in existence
earlier. The list sets out that “soldiers’ letters or
those addressed to their wives” shall be delivered for
one penny. English or foreign letters cost the civilian
Bd.; parcels under 201 b. cost Is. 6d. and, over 201b. f
Bs. Within N, S. Wales the letter postage was 4d.
THE MAKER OF SAINTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Concerning the recent Bulletin discussion re the
greatness or otherwise of Tyson, who died worth a
lot of money some years ago.
Simple Randolph met a Tyson
Going to the fair.
Said Simple Randolph to the Tyson:
“Why not brush your hair?”
Said the Tyson unto Randolph:
“’Cause I am No Dandy!'’’
And somehow that quaint view of it
Knocked simple Randolph bandy.
Then, hurrying away in a state of considerable moral
exhilaration,
lie hurriedly canonised Tyson for saving the nation —
As well as saving his money and being perfectly free
from all traces of affectation.
And when some unbelievers rose up and remarked
that his new saint was spurious,
Randolph tore off his three-acre hat and denounced
them in writing most furious.
N.S.W.

“O. C. Cabot” : A week or two ago you printed
some remarks (from the scene of dryness)
concerning the Swindle capital site at Yass-
Canberra. Here are some more. About a
year ago—maybe a little more —the Sydney
Telegraph published a huge picture en-
titled “Junction of the Molonglo and the
Murrumbidgee at the Federal Capital Site.”
The photo., which was taken as near after
the break-up of the (then) partial drought
as could be arranged, showed about 37 miles
of water rolling down like the Ashburton
(W.A.) in flood-time —in* fact, there was so
much water in the picture that only a tree
and two mosquitoes could be found room for
in the offing. On Monday last the Tele-
graph published a telegram (perhaps from
its truthful fishing-expert, who described the
famous Cotter as “a chain of waterholes”)
concerning the mighty Molonglo, which read:
The Government is confining work at the capital
to the construction of the Cotter River waterworks
and power station. The Molonglo Hirer has ceased
running at Canberra.
This is borne out by the picture (also re-
printed by the D.T.. August 26, 1912) by
Joseph Wolinski, of “The Molonglo at Yar >
Canberra.” The artist shows a veritable
“chain of waterholes,” with sandy bottom
and a few bulrushes, a rather rustic spot,
but absolutely insane as the source of the
water-supply of the capital of all Australia.
When will Cook and Kelly allow the truth
to come out? A quarter of a million has
been wasted on the present absurd “site,”
and now things are so bad tha t even the Can-
berra crowd thinks of abandoning the place.
To the surprise of many people who re-
garded William John Lyne as a chronic
pauper, his will has been proved at £17,862.
The governor of the Commonwealth Bank
(Denison Miller), Walter G. Parish (soli-
citor), and the widow are the executors.
The bulk of the estate represents shares in
public cos.
Newtown, one of the most breathless sub-
urbs of congested Sydney, has an old tumble-
down cemetery which someone or other
wants to have “converted into a quiet place
of rest for the citizens” —not the dead citi-
zens who are resting there already, but the
live rent-payers. The optimist reckons that
a “lung” will thereby be added to a very
stuffy area. So with a vast hinterland
simply aching for population, and empty
ports crying out for occupation, some of
Sydney’s citizens have to get into a grave-
yard to breathe. This is a mad woi’ld, but
parts of it are madder than others.
A Sydney policeman found a burglar the
other night and immediately conceived an
intense longing to make him his burglar.
Being a constable, he was a large man speci-
ally trained and equipped to catch burglars.
Like the rest of his class, he was even clad
in special burglar-catching clothes. He
wanted a burglar more than a drink, or an
enlarged photograph of his wife, or a free
pass for a picture show. Even had such a
transaction been in order, a benevolent third
party couldn’t have kept him from his right-
ful burglar with an offer of ss. and an en-
graving of the Battle of the Boyne. The
burglar, on the other hand, wanted to get
away from the officer more than anything in
life. His soul boiled within him at the very
sight of the cop, and he desired to retire and
spend a long evening with all his wife’s re-
lations, or a bag of venomous snakes —any-
thing rather than go with the peeler.
However, principally on account of
the bobby’s shark-like eagerness, these
two dissimilar individuals came together
with a thud, and the subsequent pro-
ceedings were dusty and vicious, because
they were both frightfully in, earnest and
wanted to do different things.’ The trap
won, and next day the brilliant evening
paper came out like this in a perfect rash
of head-lines : “BURGLAR AND POLICE-
MAN-DESPERATE STRUGGLE.” And
straightway it occurred to everybody that a
struggle—but more particularly a burglar-
policeman struggle—to be of any use at all,
must be desperate. It really isn’t worth
while taking part in if it is to be merely
suggestive of crewel work or Limerick lace
or Venetian glass or chess or soap-bubbles.
From an authority on town-planning:—
One of the worst specimens of municipal vandalism
is the new traffic road from Woolloomooloo Bay, Syd-
ney, to Park-street. This splits Cook and Phillip
Parks in halves and cuts a swathe off the Domain,
and thus takes a big slice off the not-too-extensive
recreation grounds of Sydney. The designers of this
scheme deliberately left the big area of slum houses
on the east of the route, and chose to cut a portion
of the Domain away on the west. Thus they spoiled
a portion of the Domain while carefully preserving
the slums. It is understood that this vandalism was
perpetrated by a combination of the Wade Govern-
ment and the much-boosted Allen Taylor regime at
the Town Hall. It was decided in the teeth of such
obvious arguments as these: (1) That the preserva-
tion intact of the open spaces of a city is a duty
every generation owes to its successors and itself;
(2) that a Royal Commission on the improvement of
Sydney reported against this route, and recommended
the widening and regrading of Cathedral-street and
the continuation of that street by tunnel to Rush-
cutter’s Bay, thus linking up the eastern foreshore
suburbs for tram and vehicular purposes and avoiding
the hill-climbing and bottle-necking at (fog. top of
William-street; (3) that a danger zone is created at
the intersection of Boomerang-street with the new
street. One can imagine the whole of the Eastern-
foreshore suburbs tram traffic being held up morning
after morning while the big lorry traffic from the
Woolloomooloo wharves lumbers up the hill across the
tram line with a painful cracking of whips, lashing
of horses and drivers’ objurgations. The same City
Council which was particeps criminis in this piece
of crude vandalism repeated the guilt and folly of a
bygone predecessor by giving another lease of a por-
tion of the park to a private bowling-club, to the ex-
clusion of the public, save a 8 spectators. It now pro-
poses to hand over the new golf links at Moore Park
to a private club —an unheard-of thing. Why, even
the most famous of the Scottish links is open to the
public on payment of a small fee! But my main
objection is to the filching of park spaces for public
uses. Let us get in now with a protest, or we will
have to face the Allen Taylor -project to cut a road
through Hyde Park from Market-street to St. Mary’s,
with the corollary of the present piece of vandalism,
viz., the widening of Park-street at the expense of
Hyde Park. I have no objection to the lowering of
Park-street, with an arched cover extending the park
and letting traffic go under the park surface, like the
fine Quirinale tunnel in Rome; but the queen spaces
of Sydney should be fought for to the last inch. The
present City Council wants close watching. It will
sell the park if we don’t look out.

Cleaning the teeth with
tooth powders or pastes can never, under any cir-
cumstances, preserve them from destruction.
That follows from the simple fact that the parts
most exposed to injury—the backs of the molars, the
interstices between the teeth, and the cavities in
them —are the very parts which remain untouched
by tooth powders and tooth pastes.
In consequence, mischief once commenced in
these places, advances undisturbed.
But Odol, being liquid, can penetrate the minutest
crevices, and, as it has a real antiseptic effect, lasting
for hours, arrests all bacterial and fermentation processes
which destroy the teeth.

IN
BANANA LAND
Old John Connolly was the centre to
which all things gravitated at the opening
of the Gayndah-Mundubberra railway a few
days ago. The old gentleman, who claims
to be 98, strolled amiably round conversing
with the. ladies —especially the beautiful
ones, which means that he conversed with
them all, for all ladies are beautiful up
there. The “father of Gayndali” intends to
see his century out, and will almost cer-
tainly be found cutting wood on the morning
of his hundredth birthday.
Passed out the other day in Brisbane
Hospital, Jasper Harvey, an old Liberal
battler, who ran more unsuccessful contests
than any other Tory politician in Banana-
land. Jasper had a wonderful knack of say-
ing the right thing in the wyong place; of
picking’ a gooj constituency at a bad time,
and of tacklipg a bad constituency at all
Sorts of times. He died, as fie had lived,
exhorting Liberalism against Labor, and pro-
foundly dissatisfied witfi tfie slackness of his
own party.
. R, R. Howard, the best Protector of Abo-
rigines that Queensland ever had, lias re-
signed, and the reason can be put into two
words, “Mornington Island.” Dr. Mjoberg,
the Swedish ethnologist (B. 12/2/T4),'refers
to the fact that the Presbyterian mission is
about to invade this last, lone stronghold
of the Australian Binglii, and bitterly de-
plores the intention. For years Howard has
been battling to prevent the evil missionary
thing, but the pull of the Wowser has proved
too strong for him. For this the blame lies
at the door of Home Sec. George Appel, who
has. some weird idea that Black Brudder has
a right to “salvation.” Unfortunately you
can’t “save” Black Brudder without first
“civilising” him —and “civilisation” is the
end of poor Black Brudder every time. He
isn’t of much value, but some of him should
be preserved as a curio. And the missionary
to end him even as a curio.
Old John Branston, who arrived in Bris-
bane in 1859 as private secretarv to Gov-
ernor Bowen, was lately found ‘doddering
about Wimbledon (Eng.), hale, hearty and
financially comfortable. He and Herbert,
the first Queensland Premier, were fellow-
students at Oxford, and, having much the
same tastes, were inseparable in Brisbane.
Bowen, indeed, relied on Branston and Her-
bert to enable him to set up Responsibe
Government. Both had civil-service apti-
tudes, but neither was much of a politician.
Branston, nevertheless, gave up his secre-
taryship, became member for the Burnett,
and was Attorney-General for a time. But
he was the sort of man who wants a steady
billet, hence he applied for the Attorney-
Generalship of Hong Kong, and got it and
took over an Acting-Judgeship. Even that
wasn’t steady enough, so he got a billet in
the Colonial Office in London and pigeon-
holed documents with such precision that in
time he was appointed Under-Secretary.
That just suited him, and he held the billet
for nearly a quarter of a century, retiring
on a pension. Ilis eminent services as
pigeon-holer brought him a knighthood. He
is now well past 80.
PROOF POSITIVE.
COUNTRY B UTCHER (to customer with a complaint): "What? Not lamb! Well, perhaps
yer'll believe me when I tell yer that that joint was a cut orf that pet lamb o'
Bob Smith's wot he ’ad for years and years,”
A Rockhampton man has just put up
what ought to be an Australian record by
being turned down for the sixth consecutive
time nt the local municipal elections. Even
Joshua, the son of Nun, had only to make
seven fries before he got the run of the
mayor’s parlor in the city of Jericho, and
that was regarded as something extra in the
way of persistence.
“X.P.D.”: The latest candidates offering
themselves in the anti-priekly-pear contest
are Rasmussen, of Melbourne, and Pawley,
of California. Rasmussen reckons he can
tear the terror from the ground with a
machine he has, and make jelly of the fruit
and other things of commercial value from
the leaf-fibre. Pawley puts his trust in a
novel kind of traction-engine to crush the
curse flat so that poison can be effectively
administered. Any one of the tried or pro-
posed methods would probably kill anything
but prickly-pear. That devil of desolation
defiantly waves its spikes at them all, and
nothing short of pulling it up by the roots,
mincing it fine, dipping it in molten
lava and smoking it in Slieol for 1400
years seems likely to seriously affect its
health. Meanwhile, it is spreading to the
extent of a million acres a year.
“Milky White”: The par. (B. 5/2/T4)
about the “finger-cherry” being a pest says
that the Government promises to eradicate
it—some day. Well, the finger-cherry
(Rliodomyrtus macrocarpa ), even though,
“proclaimed,” isn’t a pest; so there ! Its
fruit sometimes is infested by a fungus,
Gloeosporium periculosum, which is strongly
suspected of having caused the death of sev-
eral kids who overloaded their tummies with
diseased berries. On another charge more
than suspicion exists: it is certain that, even
as p-. : fy is to politicians, so is the fungus to
thus* who eat of infested cherry-berries—it
causes blindness. Still, the fungus doesn’t
work in the dark, so to speak ; it forms little
yello\v spots on the fruit and so renders the
diseased berries readily identifiable. If
Queenslanders are wise they’ll chase the
Government up until it gets Gloeosporium
periculosum into a dark corner and lets the
public debt fall upon it heavily. It isn’t
certain that G.p. will not attack other fruits,
and we don’t want a nation smitten with
physical as well as political blindness.
Home Sec. Appel, in many respects the
most liberal of Denham’s Ministers, has one
curious kink. The other day a Free Speech
deputation saw him, and got this amazing
remark thrown at it:—
It Joseph McCabe [McCabe is a science lecturer
of world-wide reputation] asked me to allow him
to speak on a Sunday, if I interfered at all it would
be to see that he did not get a permit.
Some day, during an outbreak of anti-Ger-
man hysteria, some narrow-minded zealot,
sitting in Appel’s place, will refuse to any
person with such a sauerkraut name as
Appel the right to get up and show that the
Germans in Queensland are good, peaceable
citizens; and when that happens the Appels
will see what it means to stifle free speech.
Meantime, while Appel is prepared to shut
the mouth of the scholarly scientist, he is
willing to let the most ignorant ranter in
the country talk endlessly, at all times and
places, concerning a great fish that swal-
lowed Jonah and delivered him none the
worse for wear after three days’ cold storage.
“Boro-Belle”: Mulgrave Central Mill,
Cairns, a farmers’ co-operative concern,
holds the belt for having paid, on the aboli-
tion of the sugar bounty, the highest price,
of any mill in Queensland, for cane last
season, the amount being 31s. a ton. Inci-
dentally, Bananaland’s sugar industry is!
bounding ahead at a greater pace than the
mills can keep up with. A large new mill
(John Drysdale’s) is nearing completion at
Inkerman, near Townsville, capable of turn*-
b’g out 10,000 tons of sugar in a season)
The Government is erecting another big
mill at Babinda, near Cairns, which will be
crushing next year, while a second Govern-
ment mill goes up at South Johnstone to
crush in 1916. At Cairns, extensive new
areas are being put under cane cultivation.
This remark also applies to Mackay, where
an agitation is being started to encourage
the erection of a huge mill, capable of turn-
ing out 60,000 tons of sugar in a season-
just 3000 tons more than Mackay’s output
from its 10 factories last season.
Nationalists and Ulstermen should dis-
solve their differences in an harmonious ses-
sion with the genial spirit of King George
IV. All discords fly before the mellow in-
fluence: of this peace-inspiring topnotch
whisky.*
Every Australian should read “Explora-
tions in Civilization.” by Randolph Bedford
—paper Is.; cloth 3s. 6d. All agents, or
direct from publisher. Syd Day, Bulletin
Building, Sydney. Add postage,*
