[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Solestry is the name of a new science which
teaches that character is mainly revealed by the
shape and markings of the feet.' The Countess of
S —, its leading exponent, lately examined the feet
of scores of criminals in the Paris prisons.
—Press item.
When the black drop in your vitals plays
the mischief with your soul,
And the very air is humming that you’ll find
a Sombre Goal,
Do not feel your bumps in wonder or invoke
your diaphragm,
Or go marv’l ling are you devil plain or
merely common man;
When you’re sick of your offendings,
Just look down about your endings,
And I’ll wager ev’ry dollar you’ll discover
that the seat
Of your ailings and your failings is the con-
tour of your feet.
Never mind the wrinkled forehead or the
crow’s feet round the eyes,
’Tisn’t near them—do not fear them—that
(the source of sorrow lies ;
But beware the corny surface of the corru-
gated sole,
Or the bunion that has battened till it rises
like a knoll;
If the toes are long and bony.
’Stead of short and fat and tony,
You may take it for a certainty—l solemnly
repeat—-
That the root of your backslidings is the
shaping of your feet.
Let the palmist, quaint and gifted, drop her
client’s lily hand,
With its curious indications that the wizards
understand ;
Let her gaze upon the instep and the ankle
and the toes,
Let her bid the callowed toe-joint toll its
sorry tale of woes ;
Let her drop her tricky wiling,
Her professional beguiling,
And reveal the Truth about us in a fashion
frank and neat
As ’tis written in the scribing of the Fates
about the feet.
Brother Pleeceman, do not for the.
forecast on thy sole; * *
Thou art planted fair and firmly like a big
and burly bole ;
And the scoffer who would jeer thee for thine
understandings great,
May yet learn “ He hath the footprints of
the Men who make the State!”
Though they chide his wits of lead
• There is valor in his tread ;
There is intellect a-bulging in his pace upon
the beat,
There is comfort if we trust him and the
lines upon his feet!
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, February 14, 1914.
1 My Dear Thargomindah,—
The walking delegate of the Australian
governors’ Unindustrious Union of Vice-regal
Sundowners informs me that the Macgregor
Bady Mac pulled into Brisbane on
lliursday after a fortnight spent in beating
up the available agricultural shows and hos-
pitable stations on the Darling Downs. After
untying the swags, his Ex.’s mate, Talbot,
who, like Henry Lawson’s old pal, Mitchell,
does the battling, went on to Sydney, where,
I understand, the going is Rood.
This afternoon Sir AVilliam, with Charles
Mackellar to the right and Lady Macgregor
to the left, a bodyguard of aldermen and their
skirted halves, and the popular private sec-
retary, Captain Foxton, one pace to the
rear, successfully brought into action a large
and formidable f&te in support of a local
School of Arts. The disquieting feature of
the show was a polite message, received by
each of the newspaper offices. The messen-
ger, who breathed the remarks per ’phone,
urged in roundabout but unmistakable lan-
guage that a large, hobnailed reporter should
be sent, as it: was no place for a female
reporter. This may have been a reflection
on the capacity of mere woman for liquids,,
but I saw nothing to bear out the suspicion..
Indeed, everybody was perfectly sedate and;
sober. Even the Governor walked respect-
ably, and if he had worn a crayfish in his.
buttonhole I would certainly have noticed i
it. I don’t know what the girls expected,,
but every scribe present was a lady.
Mrs. Ricardo, the handsome widow of the *
late Colonel Ricardo, is to be married next
Wednesday in your city to Mr. A. Street,,
an American with, a plentiful pyramid of
dollars. The lady has many friends here..
She is the daughter of that bluff
and well liked old medico, the late Dr. Jolini
Thomson. Dr. Jack Thomson, her brother,,
married Miss Elsie Forsyth, of Sydney, who>
is now numbered among the ranks of our-
best-dressed younger matrons.
Two other hitchings timed for Wednes*--
day will link Dolly Jardine (who has £&00i
a year of her own) to Donald Cameron, off
Kensington Downs; and Roy Murray,, som
of John Murray, M.L.C. (once Minister foir
Education), to Miss Moody, daughter of the*
manager of the Warwick branch, of the*
Q.N. Bank.
Miss Jardine is staying with the Cecil!
Palmers until after her wedding. The offi--
cial breakfast will be gnawed at the Oeeill
Palmer mansion.
There were two weddings this week. One;,
at St.. Stephen’s Cathedral, was a very, sky-
towering affair. Cecelia Beirne, the pretti-
est of T. C. Beirne’s five daughters, tools
for as much better as possible and as littlb*
worse as may be H. A. C. Douglas. The
little bride trailed soft white crepe de chine
draperies, and was cheered up by her sister
Eileen, who brightened the proceedings in
white crgpe de chine, topped off by a pretty
pink hat. The decorations were in pink and
white at the cathedral and at Hendra, where
the Beirnes live. The bridegroom’s father
was once Administrator of Thursday Island
and before that he was Premier of Queens-
land. The other knot-tying was squatter
Hunter, of Hythe station, to Doris Webster,
step-daughter of W. R. O. Hill.
Mrs. Hobbs, widow of the late Dr. Hobbs,
and mother of the two lawyers of that name,
died this week.
The Society for the Suppression of Savagese.
got cash and applause to-night, when Palace-
Gardens was re-opened by Charlie
liagen, champion wrestler and pastrycook. _
The takings of the night went to the sofiifty-
already mentioned. Ilis Ex. and Lady Mac, ,
the new Mayor and Mayoress of Brisbane, .
the Acting-Premier and Mrs. Barnes, and a
choice and varied assortment of Ministers ;
and members were there. Tessie Mulcahy
uplifted a contralto, and Fred Watson a
baritone, without any opposition from the -
audience calling for special remark.
The Amateur Swimming Championships ;
of Queensland were swum off to-night. Alice ■
Alexander again beat Elsie Vennings for the ■
Junior by a few inches, and the Senior •
Championship remained with Mabel Spring-
field, the holder.
Women committees are rather illogical. I.'
don’t believe even J. S. Mill could have been
logical in a whalebone environment and a
tango skirt, to say nothing of a hat covered
with fowls’ skins, ti-tree and other vege-
tables. Where was I, my dear? Oh, yes; at
a recent meeting of the State School Girls’
Amateur Swimming Association, it was
suggested in all seriousness that one girl who
had distinguished herself by winning a num-
ber of championships should be disqualified
because the other girls had no chance, against
her. Luckily, the suggestion was squashed,
otherwise an awkward precedent w r ould have
been established of eliminating winners until
the last lone water-sluggard beat herself by
a head and the pastime became a hack
number.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Buka: Following information is too late,
“The ceraining ef fire armes buy ‘Celia Lloyd’ in a
recent Bulletin gold uruch further than 12 years.”
Why didn’t you cable it?. . . .Nightowl: Not right.
....Nickols: Wouldn’t print it at advertising rates.
....Shareholder: Never heard of it... .G.M.8.: To
answer that question we would need the run of
the C.S.K. Co.’s books; and even the Common-
wealth Government hasn’t the power to call for such
an examination.... Lisbey: If the child was born
here he could return; if born in China, he could be
barred.... Constant Reader: Don’t know what the
Binghi words “Banyabba” and “Gundaroo” mean.
Possibly they don’t mean anything. Possibly there
are no such words Camoo: Fact, no doubt; but
where’s the point of it? F.A.W.: Don’t know.
The B. certainly never said he was the only judge
of the fine arts in Maoriland. .. . F.A.F.: The Exter-
nal Affairs Dept, has charge of the business of
naturalisation. There are five essentials. The suc-
cessful applicant (1) must not be an aboriginal
native of Asia, Africa or the Islands of the Pacific,
excepting M.L. ; (2) must have lived continuously
in the Commonwealth for two years; (3) must in-
tend to make this his home; (4) must be of good
repute; and (5) must take the oath or affirmation
of allegiance. No fee is charged for a certificate. .. .
Swamper: This is what A. 11. S. Lucas (ex-president
of the Linnean Society of N.S.W., of the Field
Naturalists’ Club of Victoria and of the Naturalists’
Club of N.S.W.), and W. It. Dudley Le Souef (direc-
tor of Melb. Zoo) say in their authoritative book,
“The Animals of Australia” (chapter, kangaroos and
wallabies) : “The large tail is employed as a third
limb to the tripod when the animal is sitting up at
rest and as a counterpoise to the forepart of the
body when in rapid advance and, at such times, does
not touch the ground.” In a paragraph dealing with
the common or great grey kangaroo the authors
state: “The tail does not touch the ground when
the animals are leaping, but when they are feeding
and moving slowly they rest their weight upon it while
they move their feet.” “When they are fighting,” says
Mr. Le Souef, “they stand on their toes and lean
back on the end of the tail; and when they kick for-
ward the whole of the weight of the body is sup-
ported by the tai1.”.... Ginger: The sub-editor whose
specialty was reading copy that had been written
on blotting-paper with an old thumb dipped in tar
is no longer on the staff. He found life too strenu-
ous, and forwarded his resignation to Father Time.

MEMORIES.
Dawn.
Dost remember, love of mine, though time slips
’neath our feet,
How Hope, our golden sun, arose, and all seem’d so
complete ?
Thy love for me, and mine for thee ; doth it remain
a sweet,
Fond memory?
Twilight.
Dost remember how the Shadows, length’ning fast
apace
Diffused, obscured like fading dreams from me thy
lovely face ?
Ah, dost thou sit and ponder, is the thought of Love’s
embrace
Foncl Memory ?
Eventide.
The sweetest rose that blooms must fade, pleasure
fraught with pain,
As to a thirsting flower the welcome drop of rain
Thy love for me, and mine for thee, for ever will
remain
Fond Memory.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CORRESPONDENTS.
Hawley H.: If you could draw and had
some bright ideas you might do well in black-and-
white work....A. Boultwood: The whatyoumaycallit
(it is perhaps meant for a picture) is of no use to us
.. . . Kangatong: A poor, barren, futile trifle. The
motorist is at least working, which the cigarette
fiend, who is jesting at his expense, isn’t... .D.L.H.:
Your sea-sickness picture is rotten, coarse and utterlv
disgusting. Besides, it isn’t a picture. And your
joke (which isn’t a joke) has been worked off by
aldermen and beadles and Bumbles and churchward-
ens and other bright intellects ever since the first pun
was invented P. J. Iloctor: No writing attached
to show what the pictures are about. Does that
mean that they are about nothing? They look about
the species of pictures that might be about something
of the sort. .. . J.D.C.: When you draw two loafers
at a bar you really ought to make one loafer say
something sparkling to the other. For two loafers
at a bar have been drawn so often. .. .Allan H.:
“Bank _ director” joke rejected, but not returned."
You might pass the aged jest on some other paper,
and get into gaol. People like you don’t seem to
realise that when you sell old literary matter with-
out intimating that it is old you are getting money
by false pretences, just as much as if you passed a
valueless cheque, and it is only the forbearance of
editors and the like which keeps you out of The “jug.”
..N.A. _ (V.): Your delight in mere distor-
tion is infantile. Look at Phil May’s series
“Things We See When We Come Out Without
Our Gun.” He got his humorous effects by skilful
exaggeration W.S. (W.A.) : Wash covers a multi-
tude of artistic sins. The end of the artist’s sleeve
(or perhaps it is the end of the critic’s sleeve) is
apparently woven into the body of his coat, and his
trousers are back to front. The alleged jest is point-
less, and neither of the characters looks the sort
of man who would say “Spare me day5.”....18 •
What is the precise use of a jokeless drawing of two
beautiful full-bosomed ladies in evening dress stand-
ing opposite each other and looking up in the air ?
Work is prettily feminine and quite colorless.

ROYAL AUSTRALIAN NAVY.
Tenders for Auxiliary Motor-scow for
Papua.
TENDERS will be received, subject to
the conditions of tendering, until 12
noon on Wednesday, 4th March, 1914, for
the supply of an AUXILIARY MOTOR-
SCOW for Papuan waters.
Plans and Specifications may be seen at
the Naval Staff Office, Sydney and Brisbane.
Copies can be obtained on payment of a
deposit of £5, which will be refunded on the
receipt of a bona-fide tender.
Tenders are to be endorsed “Tender for
Motor-scow” on top left-hand corner of
envelope, and addressed (if by post pre-
paid) to the Director of Navy Contracts,
care Naval Staff Office, Sydney or Brisbane,
as the case may be.
The lowest or any tender not necessarily
accepted.
Tenders to be in all cases on the printed
form, which can be obtained as above.
E. D. MILLEN,
Minister of State for Defence.
Legal Notices.
PURSUANT to an Order of the
Chancery Division of the High
Court of Justice made in the matter of tue
estate of Robert Townsend Hippisley de-
ceased Ilippisley v. Hippislev 1912 H. 1515
and dated the 12th October 1912 the follow-
ing inquiry is directed, viz.: An inquiry
whether CHARLES ERNEST HIPPISLEY
named in the will of Robert Townsend Hip-
pisley is living or dead and if dead when he
died and whether he left any and what will
and whether he was ever married and if so
when and to whom and whether there were
any children of the said marriage and who
are his legal personal representatives.
Notice is hereby given that the' said
Charles Ernest Hippisley and all persons
claiming through or by descent from him
are personally or by their solicitors on or
before the 4tli day of July 1914 to come in
and prove their claims at the Chambers of
Mr. Justice Joyce and Mr. Justice Eve,
Room 692 Royal Courts of Justice, Strand,

will be peremptorily excluded from the bene-

afternoon at the said Chambers is appointed
for hearing and adjudicating upon the

Note. —The above-named Charles Ernest
Hippisley who was a son .of the testator
Robert Townsend Hippisley a solicitor of
Bristol England is now if. alive about 51
years of age. He went to Australia in Feb-
ruary 1889 and was there employed by
Messrs. Robert Reid & Co., and afterwards
till May 1897 by Messrs. Sands & McDougall
(Limited). He resided at Rochester Lodge,
Collins-place, Melbourne. He left Mel-
bourne in May, 1897, by the Orient Steam-
ship Orizaba for London, where he stayed:
one or two months, and is believed to have
resided at 123 Fulliam-road, London. In
October, 1897, he left London,- and until
April, 1898, was employed by , Messrs. J.
Garlick & Co., of Alderley-street, Cape
Town, South Africa, and resided at 43 St.
John-street, Cape Town. He returned to
Melbourne in 1898, and expressed his inten-
tion of going to Queensland or Sydney.' He
is alleged to have been seen in London by an
old schoolfellow in 1908.
Should the above advertisement, which
has reference to benefits accruing to him
under his father’s will, meet the eye of the
said Charles Ernest Hippisley, or in the
event of his death that of his widow or
children, he is (or they are) earnestly re-
quested to communicate with the least pos-
sible delay with his brother Alfred Edward
Ilippisley, of 8 Herbert-crescent, London,
S.W., or with the said Messrs. Abbot, Pope
& Abbot, solicitors, Bristol.

“Gadsby”: The return of a brief by a
barrister, owing to his promotion to the
Bench, does not necessarily imply the re-
turn of the fee received with the brief. In
this connection a Victorian Supreme Court
Judge could tell a tale. On his promotion
he received a letter from a solicitor, who
had employed him on the very day of his
advancement, pointing out that his client
was an exceedingly poor man. Could the
new Judge see his way, etc., etc.? The
Judge replied in a letter redolent of sym-
pathy, but protesting that he dared not
commit such a breach of professional eti-
quette, much as his feelings, etc. “I knew
what your feelings in the matter would be,”
replied the solicitor, “and in order to allow
you to satisfy your sense of justice without
offending professional etiquette by returning
a fee, I had payment of the cheque stopped
before I wrote you.”

Business, Robbery, etc.
The cat’s-meat man has arrived in Sydney. For
many years he was an unknown institution, but
times change. So does meat. So do cats. In brighter,
younger days the pussy foraged for herself, but now
the city has grown so big that she has to travel 10
miles or so before she can run riot and caterwaul on
a gum tree, and she has almost forgotten what a gum
tree is like. Then, again, there was a time when
any old meat was the cat’s meat. Now, owing to
railway congestion, the Sydney-bound bullock is
liable to do a seven-days perish in the trucks with-
out water, and when it gets out, a fever-stricken
ghost, and is driven howling to the abattoirs, it isn’t
fit for any cat to eat. Also it is crazy enough to
eat any cat or any number of cats. So, by reason
of a number of circumstances, the Sydney feline
finds itself placed on a new basis. It has lost its
summer vacation in the bush, for the adjacent bush
is dead. There is no longer enough rat and mouse
to go round. The habit of having covers on milk
cans has cut off an old-established means of suste-
nance. So has the habit of having covers on garbage-
tins. The shingle roof, to which a cat could cling
easily, has given place to a slippery galvanised-iron
abomination, so the invigorating midnight promenade
is at an end. And the infant who carries the animal
round by the tail has increased just at a time when
the animal was least fitted to stand being carried
round by the tail. Something had to be done for
the cat, else it would have deserted the sinking ship
of civilisation.
* » * *
“Asbestos”: In his oration on Australian boots,
“0.K.” hits the nail on the head when he states (B.
29/l/’l4) that “the prejudice is accounted for nowa-
days firstly by the fact that most Australian boots
are not branded.” The same applies to felt hats and
many other items that are creditably manufactured
locally. The blame lies with the wholesale ware-
houseman. He will not allow the manufacturer to
stamp “Made in Australia” on the articles he buys
and distributes, but insists that they be branded
with his own warehouse trade-mark, which usually
consists of a raging lion or something equally un-
Australian. Consequently the public purchases Aus-
tralian goods believing them to be either British or
foreign, according to the way the trade-mark im-
presses the buyer’s fancy. The Commerce Act
rightly insists on all imported articles being branded
with the name of the country of origin, and this
gives the “made in Germany” mob a splendid ad-
vertisement. But, unfortunately, the Act stops there,
instead of also demanding that “made in Australia”
be stamped on the local products, so as to give the
same publicity to our own manufactures. Most man-
ufacturers wish to mark their goods “Made in Aus-
tralia,” but they are not individually strong
enough to risk displeasing the unpatriotic merchant
who is often their only customer. They probably
n :er will be able to obtain their rights in this
n iter until the Commerce Act is altered to compel
the Australian to do what the foreigner now so very
willingly does.
* * * »
“Coorey”: “Pick” (B. 29/l/’l4) produces a
bushel of misstatements re timber matters, which
would be all right if he didn’t attribute them to me.
Let me assure “Pick” that I did not even mention
the Forestry officials. Theirs is a different sphere
altogether from the timber inspector’s. The former
deals with the raw material, the latter with the
finished or partly-finished product of the bush. The
difference is that between the squatter and the cat’s-
meat salesman. The Departments of Works, Mines,
Agriculture, Telegraphs, Railways, etc., each have
their inspectorial staff, arid it was two of these I
had in mind when I stated (what I now repeat)
that the substitution of inferior and other timbers
for the specified material, is permitted. “Pick” asks
the cause, whether fraud, negligence ■or incapacity.
I dispense with the first, and in some cases acknow-
ledge the next two. Yet again there is another cause
which he does not seem to be aware of —a little tact.
In the wonderful examination set for admission to
the guardianship of the Bush, a pass constitutes in
the clerical part not less than 50 per cent, on any
one paper, nor less than 60 per cent, in the aggre-
gate. In addition, there is a practical test of but
little better percentage, but the thing which bumps
out so many budding foresters, even after they have
passed all the rest, is called by the Public Service
Board “failed in aptitude,” which is another way
of saying “want of tact.” At present, there are 21
candidates eligible for appointment, and I take it
that the one who exhibits the greatest tact, how-
ever expressed, has the brightest chance of the first
leg up the gum-tree.
* * * *
Harassed by the high cost of living, even the safe-
breaker is calling out for labor-saving devices. A
couple of him broke into a Sydney boot-polish factory
a few nights back, went through all the drawers,
made an attempt at the safe, and got the large sum
of nothing. Next morning, when the boss came to
work he found a typed note on his desk: “Why didn’t
you leave the safe open, you ?” But they lacked
business training, and didn’t leave any address for
the reply to be sent to.
* * » *
“J. 0.8.”: “Torrens’ title, Torrens’ title,” repeated
the land and estate agent. - “Who is this Torrens?”
asked the prospective buyer of the lithographed
allotment. The land and estate agent appeared
never to have been asked the question before, but
bri*'g p U t to it he pumped up the information that
Torrens’ title chokes off lawyers and is “as sound
as the Bank of England.” Poor old Torrens! He had
hard work getting Australia to understand his idea,
and particularly hard work in keeping solicitors at
bay while he was converting Parliament. He was a
County Cork man, Collector of Customs in Adelaide,
and then member of Parliament. He had but one
use for his shillelagh—to fight with it for his system
of land transfer. The old plan of relying wholly on
deeds fed lawyers, who had often to go back through
half a ton of transfers to see that the title was com-
plete. Torrens advocated registration in a book
kept by the Government as not only simpler, but in
comparison almost costless. When he had converted
the S.A. Parliament he travelled round Australia
expounding his Act and fighting lawyers in every capi-
tal. Actually he single-handed conquered Australia,
and when he left for England every Australian Par-
liament sent him its thanks for his achievement.
He became a member of the House of Commons and
introduced his bill there; but members were more
under the thumb of law, and gave him but half an
ear.
ON THE BUST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Oh, yes! You can get them for two-and-eleven-
pence at Rags Ltd!”— Terrible revelation by a lady.
To lay his head upon her breast
He thinks would be like Heaven.
Ah, there indeed he longs to rest
Upon that shapely, swelling breast
That cost Two-and-Eleven.
(I trust, dear friends, you’ll not divulge
The secret of that lovely bulge!)
It fills her blouse to such effect
That all men watch her keenly.
As she sweeps by in muslin decked,
Your peace of mind is straightway wrecked.
Like some young queen serenely,
Full-bosomed on her way she goes.
(Only the crafty draper knows!)
It’s business —and it’s Art as , well.
The youth so sentimental
Admires the curves that softly swell.
It is a sweet and lovely sell
That Nature in her gentle
Old way will supersede when she
Has taken on maternity.
Two-and-l'.leven buys the bust
That draws Man’s admiration !
No perfect gentleman, I trust —
(I speak, remember, of the bust)
Will curse the innovation!
It may be false —it may be true!
Don’t worry! All are good to view.
N.S.W.

day—
Jest moochin’ along, _
An’ I comes on a cove lookin’ down on Im-
self,
So I arsks wot was wrong.
An’ ’e sez ’e’s a himmijgrant lookin’ fer work,
An’ ’e ’adn’t a cent — _ (
An’ ’e guessed ’e’d go down by th’ river, an
sleep—•
An’ I knows wot ’e meant!
Fer ’e’d not ’ad a feed fer a day an’ a ’arf,
An’ ’is traps was orl popped ;
’E was fair on th’ rocks with th’ tide cornin’
in,

buck up,
An’ I give ’im a smoke —-
’E was ’omesiek, yer know, fer ’e ’adn’t no
friends.

an’ ’eap—•

The Double-bed Dialogues.
THE FIRST JEALOUSY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was in the first few months of my
miraculous marriage with Babette the
beauteous. Naturally I was proud of
Babette, though in those days, per-
haps, I considered her merely as a
piece of perfect statuary, of which,
by incredible good luck, I had acquired
possession. My marriage with her had
been like walking into an auction sale
and picking up for a trifle a genuine Manet.
1 liked taking her out; I liked her to dress
smartly; I hated her in the neutral colors
that she so instinctively knew were the
finest frame for her brilliant beauty. For
myself, I would have clothed her in a rain-
bow. _
I liked seeing other women shoot at her
that professional, all-comprehending glance
that absorbs in one instant every feature
and every curve, every tone and every detail
oj a beautiful woman and her attire. I had
seen Babette use that professional glance at
other women. One passing look at a woman
seen on the street, and Babette knew whether
that woman used lip-salve, whether she pen-
cilled her eyebrows, whether she padded, or
by what make of corset she contrived to
stow away (and where she contrived to stow
away) the excess of her superabundant
figure, how much she paid for that corset, at
what shop she got it and at which particular
•sale, whether her lace was hand-made or
imitation, whether her stockings were all
silk or had lisle feet and lisle tops, whether
she possessed six pairs of shoes or only two,
whether she was married, and, if so, what
income her husband possessed; and, particu-
larly, whether under any conceivable cir-
cumstances she might become dangerous. The
sole danger she feared, except, of course, the
horror every woman has that she might some
day Fok her age, was that Some Other
unman, might capture me from her. And in
flabette’s honeymoon every attractive woman
was that contingent Some Other Cat. Babette
had had considerable experience of her sex.
But not only was I proud if women scru-
tinised my wife, but I really liked men to
admire her. Their admiration was not
needed to convince me that I had acquired
something really valuable, but it served to
remind me. It was comforting to find that
jpy own opinion was so universally con-

About the middle of the first act I became

deliberately disclose as much of her figure
as the by-laws allow —and some of the
by-laws are dreadfully out of date. She
will dispense with that article of attire
which her sex began to wear only two cen-

attiact the roving eye of the strange male

she finds on returning home that a corset-
string has escaped a placquet hole, she weeps.
The principle a woman has formulated for
herself in these matters is that any male
may see any part of her, or of her attire,
that she means him to see, but if he sees
even the most innocent portion of her dress
that is not meant to be seen he is a Horrible
Brute. Thus if the adorable Babette learnt
that strange men could see more of her than
the absurdly liberal allowance she, out of
the fulness of her heart, gave them, she
would feel dreadfully immodest. And, curi-
ously enough, she would hate me for being
the innocent instrument for enlightening
her.
Since our marriage Babette had told me
of the Awful Catastrophe that had happened
to her in the middle of a crowded street
when an indispensable tape had broken,
and a strange woman had helped her
to the shelter of the nearest draper’s,
shop. Babette had felt no gratitude
to this unknown Samaritan, who had
prevented Babette from literally falling
by the wayside. On the contrary, she con-
fessed quite naturally that she hated that
woman, and would always hate her.
A strange man can accost any man and
inform him that he has a piece of fluff on
his shoulder, and quietly remove it for him;
and the fluffy man will be quite grateful. But
if one strange woman calls the attention of
another woman to the apparent fact that she
has forgotten, in the hurry of running for
the train, to button up the back of her blouse,
those women part enemies for life. If a
blundering man dared to even mention the
gaping fact he would be frozen with a stare.
As the two men made their way noisily
to their seats behind us, after having evi-
dently had more than one drink, Babette
gave them a fleeting glance, and quickly
dropped her eyes to her programme. Some-
thing had apparently disturbed her. The
orchestra was noisily busy, and around us
was the sustained hum of conversational
chatter, but through it all I heard the young
man behind who had the clearest view of
Babette’s shoulder remark :—-
“Bit of all right, eh?”
The other man cautiously laughed.
Luckily Babette had not heard, but shortly
afterwards she casually turned round in her
seat and glanced over the ro\»s of seats that
towered behind us. There was nothing in
her action that could fix on her any blame,
even to my jealous mind. I was sure that
she hadn’t heard —yet what man can tell
whether women do not possess an extra
sense, by means of which even the unspoken
and the unapparent admiration of any man
is subtly conveyed to them? Still, a wife
should not have turned round and stared in
that way.
I had hurriedly to make conversation with
Babette, to cover up any fuither remarks
made by those men behind. But my pre-
cautions were vain.
As the curtain ascended, I distinctly heard
the nearest man say to the other, “Bet you
I’ll make her turn round again!”
I saw red. Quickly I swung round in
my seat, but in the darkness saw only two
blurs of white where faces should have been.
What could I do? The man had evidently
had the one glass too many, and any protest
of mine would only lead to a scene. And
Babette would be the centre of that scene.
Miserably and impotently I turned again to
the stage.
A quick glance showed me that Babette
had not heard. Yet she was curiously rest-
less. In the semi-darkness I noticed her
fiddling with the little bit of indispensable
lace that had got stranded in the “V.” Still,
a Woman’s principal occupation is to fiddle
with her attire, to pat her hair, or send ex-
ploring fingers on voyages of discovery up
and down her back.
“Babette,” I whispered, “I want you to
do something for me, something important.”
“Important?” she said in surprise. “What
is it?”
“Don’t look round at those ihen behind
you again.” ,
Babette never moved. She had become a
piece of beautiful statuary. “What on earth
for?” she asked at last.
“Because I ask you,” I said, sternly.
“But,” she began, “1.... 1.... Why should
I want to look x-ound at those men? And
how do I know there are any men there?
And why shouldn’t I. if I like?”
“Simply because your husband asks you,
Babette.”
“But this is extraordinary,” she breath--,
lessly began. “I don’t know those men —if
there are men behind me. Henry, you must'
tell me why you don’t want me to do such an
ordinary thing.”
Whenever Babette calls me Hem-y I know
she is in earnest. She doesn’t often call me
Henry. She has her own name for me, but
that name will never appear in print. A
man has some dignity to keep up—even a
mari-ied man.
“I can’t possibly tell you now,” I wliis-
pei-ed back.
“Sux-ely!” Babette spoke hotly. “You
make a perfectly ridiculous request, without
the vestige of a reason. And you’ve simply
made me curious. I hadn’t the least desire >
to turn round before you spoke, but now I
simply must.”
“Listen to me.” I sternly said. “I can’t

must trust me, Babette, dear. I’m your hus-
band, and it is for your own sake that I am
speaking. And I mean it.”
It was the first time that Babette had seen
her husband in me. So far, she had consid-
ered me as a rather delightful child, to be
cajoled and humored. It took her some
minutes to absorb the shock. When she
spoke it was in a sobered mood. “Very
well, husband,” she said. “Of course, I
know the whole thing is absurd on your
part, but I’ll be a good little girl since you
tell me to so sternly,”
And her hand stole into mine and gave it
a delightful squeeze,
“Thanks,” I said.
But as I bent to her I looked down the
white slope of her bosom and noticed that
the rag of lace had disappeared. Since I had
assisted her in fixing her corset that evening
I knew that the lace, whose function was
to disguise—or was it to emphasise?—the
“V,” could not have slipped down. There
was nowhere for it to slip to, since all below
the “V” was Babette, corset and bosom.
“Why, Babette,” I whispered, “what’s
become of that bit of lace?”
“Oh,” she airily answered, “I took it out.
I felt so hot. Wouldn’t you think that on
a night like this the theatre would be better
ventilated?”
What could I say? AVhat was there to
be said? But all the rest of that evening
I was miserably wondering whom it was
that I had married. Only the next day did
I discover that I had married —a woman.
As soon as the curtain fell, I hurried the
bareness of Babette into her cloak, and
rushed her out. She made not the slightest
attempt to look round. She kent her eyes
down. On the street she looked up to me
for my explanation, but I shook my head.
The explanation could not come then and
there. And when we got home Babette de-
liberately sulked. She sulks adorably. I
had to remind myself that I was angry with
her, else I couldn’t have refrained from
kissing her. And I had the feeling that to
kiss her then would be about as much use as
Moses striking the wrong rock.
In silence I untied and unpinned her, and
in silence I sucked my pricked finger.
But when we got to bed I told her what
that man had said.
“No!” she hotly protested. “That man
couldn’t have said a thing like that.”
“But I distinctly heard him. So did his
friend.”
Babette considered. “And he looked such
a _ nice man, too. So clean-looking, and
with such interesting eyes. And now all
the romance has gone out of it. I liked
him. I liked him to look at me.... but to
make a vulgar bet about me! It makes me
feel so—so cheap !”
“Exactly,” I sternly said.
u “Still,” she went on more cheerfully,
“though he was so common, he did admire
me. And I liked that. I could feel his ad-
miration all the time through my shoulders.”
“There wasn’t much on them to prevent
it,” I said.
“But surely,” she asked open-eyed, “you
like me I o look smart ?”
I was beginning to wonder whether I did
like it. “Not for the amusement of bound-
ers,” I replied.
“But how can I help people, even bounders,
admiring me, if I look pretty?” Babette
said with pathos.
“By not letting them see that you like it.
By not encouraging them, and by keeping
that piece of lace in the place where it be-
longed.”
“Oh!” Babette was hurt. “You really
think that I. could be so—so animal —as to
take that piece of lace out for —for that
man?”
‘‘Well, whom did you take it out for?”
“It was just a stupid impulse, Henry. I
felt so hot, and that theatre really should be
better ventilated, shouldn’t it?”
With this, Babette turned on me the back
that had caused all the trouble. And I
felt furiously jealous of every created male.
That beautiful back was my private propr. ■
erty, and Babette was calmly allowing per-'
feet strangers to inspect the premises.
“But, Babette,” I cried, “don’t you know
I love you? I know you didn’t deliberately
encourage that man; but I suppose you can’t
help encouraging any man. But that bit of
lace that went missing! That was delib-
erate. And though I love you—oh, more
than ever! —how can I ever again respect
you?”
“Henry!” she almost sobbed, “if you can
say such things, if you can think that of
I. . . .1 shall tell mother !”
She hunched herself, patted the pillow,
pulled up the bed-clothes so that a thick
ridge of wool intervened between the nearest
adjacent portions of Babette and me, and
snuggled down, like a curled kitten, to sleep.
But she did not sleep —for hours. So we
lay there, silent, pretending to breathe regu-
larly, in the darkness that had so heavily
descended upon our happiness.
When I woke in the morning I sleepily
turned to wake Babette with the “Good
morning” kiss. But she was not in bed.
Then I remembered. I saw her figure, in its
nightie, bending over her dressing-table.
Some instinct prompted me not to stir. I
felt intensely curious to know what she was
doing at her dressing-table.
In my effort to see I must have made a
noise, for she swiftly turned and saw me.
She_ started. In her hand I distinctly saw
a piece of letter paper. The next moment
she had turned her back to me; and though
there is considerable width in Babette’s back,
I knew that she was making a quick attempt
to conceal something. I sprang from the
bed, but before I reached her she had shut
the top drawer of her dressing-table and
defiantly faced me.
“What are you doing here?” I cried.
(i “Just looking at my nose,” she replied.
“Don’t you think it’s the teeniest bit red?”
The damnable suspicion that had seized
me turned to an equally damnable certainty.
“What was it you shut in that drawer?
You’re hiding a letter!”
“Oh, you saw, did you?” she quietly said,
one hand holding the drawer shut.
“Whose letter?” I hotly demanded. All
the foolishness of last night was nothing to
this dreadful proof of her guilt. That my
wife, that Babette, could have a letter that
she did not want me to see!
“Mine, you stupid.”
“Who sent it?”
“It hasn’t been sent.”
“You mean that it didn’t come through
the post?”
No,’ she actually laughed. It seemed to
sae was almost enjoying herself.
Who gave it to you?”
All Babette gave me was a look of raillery
and a happy “Ah !”
.“That man last night!” I exploded. “I
might have guessed. He slipped a note into
your hand!”
Babette brazenly laughed outright. “No,
dear, she chuckled, “not into my hand.
How could he, sitting behind me like that?
But....there was all my back.”
I shivered. “You mean to say that he
slipped it down your back?”
Well, wouldn’t it be the most convenient
post office?
I was maddened. “Babette,” I stormed,
give me that letter this instant.”
“Oh, no, I really couldn’t. Didn’t I tell
you that it was mine?”
“Then I’ll take it.”
Babette faced me—a new, utterly incom-
prehensible Babette. I paused stupidly to
think that this new being was the wife I
had married. And I was smitten with a
sudden fear —not fear for myself, but for
my marriage. If I took that letter by main
force from the drawer, then I certainly knew
something that held us together would
break. It might be but a minor strand of
the cord that held us, but the weakness
would always be there. I didn’t dare.
“You shan’t!” She defied me, she, so
rounded, so small, so childish, so weak—yet
so strong.
“No,” I said, when that moment of fierce
tension was over, “you will show it to me
yourself.”
To my confusion she replied, “Yes, of
course, but, please, not now.”
“Babette,” I said at length, “I trust you.
Good God, Babette, I must trust you—else
what would there be left of our marriage?
If you’ve done wrong, I can forgive you.
I must forgive you ! But I trust you. I
shan’t look in that drawer —you can leave
it open—but I know that you will show me
that letter.”
“Haven’t I said so?” she smiled. “But
not now. I must go to my bath.” She left
the dressing-table to don her bath robe. “By
the way, dear, the drawer is not locked. But
I know you will not open it.”
If Babette could do a foolish thing, she
could also do a splendid one. She could
leave a jealous husband alone in a room
with the proofs of her infidelity—oh, only
of thought!—under his hand, and yet she
knew that she could depend on my word
not to look at them.
“Babette,” I said, as she paused at the
door, “whatever you have done is wiped out
by the great trust you have in me. You can
leave that drawer unlocked”
“Why shouldn’t I?” she strangely smiled.
Because the letter isn’t there!”
I waited, miserable, for Babette’s return.
Her magnificence had dwindled until now I
saw her a carelessly impenitent sinner. This
adorable wife of mine was also adorable to
other men. Yet her transgression could
surely have been only a tiny one; and I
felt, in that melting mood that comes after
every quarrel that she had only to make
her pitiable confession and I would forgive
her. Honeymoon husbands are like that.
That is why so many marriages survive the
honeymoon.
Babette came running back, glowing in
her bath robe. “Ooh!” she shivered, “the
shower was cold! Hadn’t you better hurry?
We’ll be late for breakfast.”
I went to the bathroom and stepped under
the shower. As I put my hand up to turn
on the water I saw tied to the tap with a
piece of tape a folded note. ■ On it was
hurriedly pencilled, “The Fatal Letter!”
In the bath I read it.
There are no reticences in married life,
but there are reticences about married life.
So the reader will pardon the husband rf
he gives but the gist of Babette’s letter. To
me she had a frankness of phrase that was
nearly as naked as I then was.
She had been right when she claimed the
letter as hers. For she had written it her-
self as soon as she woke that morning, and
I had detected, her just as she had finished
it. I had evidently prevented her conceal-
ing it in the drawer. All the time we bad
stood facing each other she had it and her
pencil hidden in her hand—at which, of
course, I had never thought of looking.
And her letter was a full confession. She
admitted that she must have encouraged that
man by her eyes. (And I admit that
Babette’s eyes are the sort that would en-
courage any man.) And she admitted hav-
ing removed the lace, and not because of the
lack of fresh air in the theatre. But when
it came to explaining why, she honestly
could not tell. It was an impulse, a piece
of pure mischief she didn’t know how
or why. And she had been sorry the mo-
ment after, and if I hadn’t spoken to her
about it, she would have shoved it back at
once. But when I blamed her. she simply
had to brazen it out. Because then she
knew that I could be dreadfully jealous of
her—and that was a thing that she bad long
worried herself over. For if I couldn’t be
jealous, then I couldn’t reallv love her. And
it was so delightful to feel that I was iealous
that she simply had to keep it up. But all
the way home she knew that she had bpen a
silly, naughty, child, and if I had just given
her a good slapning she would have boon as
good as gold. But when I had told her of
that beast’s nasty bet she felt so indignant
with me —she had to be indignant with some:
body and I was the nearest person to vent
it on—that she simply had to sulk. But oh,
how she loved her big, stern, jealous hus-
band ! And if he had only kissed her rood
and hard that night she had been prepared to
be so penitent and meek. And long before
she had gone to sleep she had forgiven him.
She couldn’t have gone to sleep until she
did.
And when he had caught her writing the
letter—this was the postscript she had
scribbled in the bathroom —she was so
thrilled by my furious jealousy that she had
to keep it up. Because it showed her that
I madly loved her. No husband is a hus-
band unless he is ready to be jealous at the
slightest thing. And now she was waiting to
be forgiven. She wouldn’t begin to dress
until she had been completely forgiven....
The letter ended: “Now, darling, turn on
the cold shower— quick!”
HENRY JAMES JAMES.
